Kafalah — Surety
beginner
6 min
Kafalah: Definition, Legal Basis, and Condition
Introduces the book of Kafalah with definitions, evidence, pillars, and condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal definition of kafalah, identify its Quranic and hadith basis, and explain the condition that the subject must be deliverable by the guarantor.

Core Idea
Kafalah is a joining of liability, permissible if the guarantor can deliver the subject.

This lesson opens the chapter on Kafalah (guarantee/suretyship). The commentator explains why kafalah follows sales in the text – it commonly arises after a transaction when one party needs security. We will learn the linguistic and legal meaning of kafalah, its essential condition, and the evidence that grounds its permissibility.

Guided Path
1
Locate the chapter in the fiqh sequence
Notice that kafalah is placed after sales (buyu‘). Why? The commentator tells you: because a guarantee is often needed after a sale when either party lacks trust. Also, kafalah relates to currency exchange (sarf) because it ultimately becomes a compensatory transaction.
This placement shows the practical link between transactions and guarantees.
2
Extract the linguistic definition
Linguistically, kafalah means 'joining' (al-damm). The commentator cites the Quranic verse 'Wa kafalaha Zakariyya' (Quran 3:37) and the hadith about the guardian of the orphan to illustrate this root meaning.
The linguistic sense is foundational for understanding the legal concept.
3
Identify the legal definition
The commentator says 'In the Shari'ah: what the author mentioned.' This refers to the matn's definition of kafalah as a contract guaranteeing a person or debt. The commentary does not repeat it but builds on it.
The legal definition is brief; the commentary expands by explaining pillars and condition.
4
List the pillars (arkan)
The pillars are offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) with specific wordings that will be explained later. Without these, the contract is invalid.
A contract requires both parties' consent.
5
Explain the condition (shart)
The condition is that the subject of kafalah – whether a person (nafs) or property (mal) – must be capable of delivery by the guarantor. If the guarantor cannot deliver, the kafalah is invalid.
This condition ensures the guarantee is feasible; it applies to both types of subjects.

Source Text

Book of Kafalah. 1] His saying: Book of Kafalah. When the author finished explaining the issues of sales, he began the issues of Kafalah due to the connection that in reality it usually occurs after a sale. Indeed, the seller may not trust the buyer and thus needs someone to guarantee the price, or the buyer may not trust the seller and thus needs someone to guarantee the sold item. It also has a particular connection with currency exchange (sarraf) because it ultimately becomes a compensation for what is established in the liability of prices, when recourse is made against the principal. Then it was necessary to present currency exchange because it is one of the chapters of sale preceding Kafalah. Kafalah in language: it means joining. Allah said: 'And Zakariyya took charge of her (kafalaha)' i.e., he joined her to himself. It is said in al-Misbah: 'I guaranteed the property and the person, kafalan from the root qatala, and kafalan also, and the noun is kafalah. Abu Zayd narrated hearing from the Arabs: from the root of ta'iba and qaruba. Ibn al-Qatta' narrated: I guaranteed him and I guaranteed with him, and from him: I undertook it.' Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on Kafalah (guarantee/suretyship) from the commentary on al-Wiqayah. The author, al-Marghinani, explains the placement of this chapter after the book of Sales (Buyu') due to the practical connection: a guarantee often arises after a sale when either party lacks trust in the other. It also relates to currency exchange (Sarraf) because the guarantee becomes a compensatory transaction when the guarantor recovers from the principal. The passage then provides the linguistic definition of kafalah as 'joining' (al-dammu), citing the Quranic verse about Prophet Zakariyya taking charge of Maryam (kafalaha) and the hadith about the guardian of the orphan. The lexical explanation from al-Misbah and other sources distinguishes between kafil (guarantor) and kafil (supporter). The legal definition is briefly stated as what the author (al-Quduri) mentioned. The pillars of kafalah are offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) with specific wordings to be detailed later. The key condition is that the subject matter of kafalah—whether a person (nafs) or property (mal)—must be capable of being delivered by the guarantor. This condition ensures that the guarantee is feasible; if the subject cannot be delivered, the kafalah is invalid. The passage sets the stage for the detailed rules that follow in the chapter.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): In this text, kafalah is defined legally as what the author mentioned in the original matn (likely: 'a contract guaranteeing a person or debt'). The commentary elaborates that it is a joining (ضم) of liability.
كافل (kafil): In the linguistic discussion, kafil is used as the agent noun for kafalah of property (guarantor). It is distinguished from kāfil (one who supports an orphan).
شرط الكفالة (shart al-kafalah): The condition of kafalah: the subject (person or property) must be capable of delivery by the guarantor; if not, the contract is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is a key condition (shart) for a valid kafalah?
A) The guarantor must be a relative of the principal
B) The subject of kafalah must be capable of delivery by the guarantor
C) The contract must be written and witnessed
D) The principal must consent

Key Takeaway
Kafalah is a permissible contract of joining liability, valid only if the guarantee's subject is deliverable by the guarantor.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Definition, Pillars, and Conditions of Kafalah (Suretyship)
Opening of the Kafalah chapter: definitions, pillars, conditions, and dhimma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and technical definitions of kafalah, identify its pillar and condition, name the parties, and explain the Hanafi concept of dhimma as a cause for legal capacity.

Core Idea
Kafalah joins one dhimma to another; it requires a valid offer/acceptance and a deliverable guarantee.

Kafalah (suretyship) is a contract that frequently follows sales when a party needs assurance. This lesson unpacks its core elements: the linguistic and technical definitions, the pillar (offer and acceptance), the condition (deliverability of the guaranteed object), and the parties involved. We will also explore the Hanafi understanding of dhimma—a divinely created quality that makes a person eligible for obligations.

Guided Path
1
Place kafalah in context
Understand why the author begins this chapter after sales: a seller or buyer may need a guarantor, and kafalah often arises from transactions.
This explains the sequential arrangement of the fiqh topics.
2
Master the definitions
Learn the linguistic meaning (damm = joining) and the technical definition (joining one dhimma to another). Note the Quranic and hadith evidence for the linguistic sense.
The technical definition is the core legal concept.
3
Identify the pillar (rukn)
The contract is formed by an offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul), which must be expressed in valid words.
Without a valid offer and acceptance, the kafalah is batil (void).
4
Recognize the condition (shart) and its implications
The guaranteed object (person or property) must be deliverable by the guarantor. This excludes hudud, qisas, and certain debts like badal al-kitabah.
Deliverability is an external condition, not part of the essence.
5
Understand the parties and the concept of dhimma
The guarantor (kafil) must be legally capable (ahl al-tabarru'). The creditor is makful lahu, the debtor is makful anhu. Dhimma is a divine description (wasf) that forms the basis for obligations, distinct from intellect.
Dhimma is like a cause (sabab) for legal capacity; intellect is a condition (shart).

Source Text

والكفالة : في اللغة: هي الضم ، قال الله : وَكَفَلَها زَكَرِيَّا ) (١) : أي ضمها إلى نفسه، وقال رسول الله : «أنا وكافل اليتيم كهاتين (٢) ؛ أي انضمام اليتيم إلى نفسه. وقال في «المصباح» (۳) : «كفلتُ بالمال وبالنفس كفلاً من باب قتل، وكفالاً أيضاً، والاسم الكفالة، وحكى أبو زيد سماعاً من العرب: من بابي تعب وقرب، وحكى ابن القطاع له : كفلته وكفلت به ، وعنه : تحملت به. ويتعدى إلى مفعول ثان بالتضعيف والهمزة، والفاعل من كفالة المال : كفيل به للرجل والمرأة، وقال ابنُ الأَعْرَابي الله : فكافل أيضاً مثل ضمين وضامن، وفرَّق بينهما الليث الله فقال : الكفيل : الضَّامن والكافل : هو الذي يعـولُ إنساناً وينفق عليه». انتهى مختصراً.
وفي الشرع : ما ذكره المصنف .
وركنها : إيجاب وقبول بألفاظ يأتي ذكرها.
وشرطها : كون المكفول به نفساً كان أو مالاً مقدور التسليم من الكفيل، حتى لا تصح الكفالة بالحدودِ والقصاص، كما سيأتي ذكره إن شاء الله تعالى، وفي الدين كور صحيحاً، حتى لا تجوز الكفالة ببدل الكتابةِ كما سيجيء تحقيقه.
وأهلها من هو أهل للتبرع ، فلا تصح من العبد والصبي والمجنون، لكن العبد يطالب بعد العتق ، كما صرحوا به، والمدعي : مكفول لـه ؛ لأنَّ فائدة الكفالة ترجع إليه، والمدعى عليه مكفول عنه ، ويسمى بالأصيل أيضاً، والنَّفس في الكفالة بالنفس، والمالُ في الكفالة بالمالِ مكفول به، ففي الكفالة بالنفس المكفول عنه وبه واحد، ومن لزمته المطالبة يسمى كفيلاً.

ثم هذا الوصف غير العقل بل العقل إنما هو لمجرَّدِ فهم الخطاب، فالوجوب مبني على ذلك الوصف المسمى بالذمة، حتى لو فرض ثبوت العقل بدون ذلك الوصف لم يثبت الوجوب له عليه والحاصل أن هذا الوصف بمنزلة السبب ؛ لكون الإنسان أهلاً للوجوب له وعليه، والعقل بمنزلة الشرط.
وفسرها فخر الإسلام بالنفس والرقبة التي لها عهد والمراد بها العهد فقولهم : في ذمته؛ أي في نفسه باعتبار عهدها، من باب إطلاق الحال وإرادة المحل، وهذا المعنى أنسب بهذا المقام، وزيادة التفصيل في «النهر»، وغيره.
Book of Kafalah. [1] His saying: Book of Kafalah. When the author finished explaining the topics of sales, he began on the topics of kafalah because it frequently occurs after a sale: the seller may not trust the buyer and needs someone to guarantee the price, or the buyer may not trust the seller and needs someone to guarantee the merchandise. It has a special connection with currency exchange (sarf) because it ultimately becomes an exchange for what is established in the liability (dhimma) of the guaranteed person when recourse is had against him. Then it was necessary to place sarf before kafalah because it is a type of sale that precedes kafalah. Kafalah linguistically is al-damm (joining/adding). Allah said: 'And Zakariya took custody (kafalah) of her' (3:37), i.e., he took her into his care. The Messenger of Allah (s) said: 'I and the guardian of the orphan are like these two' – referring to joining the orphan to himself. In al-Misbah: 'I guaranteed (kafaltu) property and a person, a verbal noun kafal and kifalah, and the noun is al-kafalah. Abu Zayd narrated from the Arabs: from the patterns of ta'iba and qaruba. Ibn al-Qatta' narrated: kafaltuhu and kafaltu bihi; from him: tahammaltu bihi. It becomes transitive to a second object by doubling the verb and by the alif. The agent in property kafalah: kafil bihi, for a man and a woman. Ibn al-A'rabi said: 'Kafil is like dhamin and dhamin. Al-Layth distinguished between them: al-kafil is the guarantor, and al-kaafil is the one who supports and spends on someone.' End of summary. Technically (in the Sacred Law): as the author mentioned. Its pillar (rukn): an offer and acceptance using words that will be mentioned. Its condition: that the guaranteed object, whether a person or property, must be deliverable by the guarantor. Thus, kafalah for hadd punishments and qisas is not valid, as will be mentioned. In debt it is valid, but kafalah for the substitute for kitabah (contract of manumission) is not valid, as will be investigated. Its parties: the one who is qualified to make a gift (ahl al-tabarru'), so it is not valid from a slave, a child, or a lunatic; however, the slave is pursued after emancipation, as they have stated. The creditor is the beneficiary of the kafalah because its benefit goes back to him. The debtor is the principal (makful anhu), also called al-asil. The person in guarantee of a person, and the property in guarantee of property is the guaranteed object. In guarantee of a person, the principal and the guaranteed object are the same. The one obliged to respond to a claim is called the guarantor. [1] His saying: 'joining of one obligation (dhimma) to another'. In al-Misbah al-Munir: 'Al-Dhimma is interpreted as covenant, trust, and also guarantee. The protected person (dhimmi) is named after dhimma meaning covenant.' End of summary. In al-Mughrib: 'Al-Dhimma: the covenant; it is expressed as trust and guarantee. The place where liability is established is called dhimma in their saying: 'my dhimma is established for such-and-such.' Al-Qadi Abu Zayd said: Dhimma is a description by which a person becomes qualified for what is for him and against him. Verily, because Allah created man and honored him with intellect and dhimma, he became qualified for obligations (wujub) for him and against him, and he established for him rights of immunity, freedom, and ownership. This is the covenant that took place between Allah and His servants on the day of the covenant. This description is other than intellect; intellect is merely for understanding speech. Obligation is built upon this description called dhimma. Thus, if intellect existed without this description, obligation would not be established for him or against him. The conclusion is that this description is like a cause (sabab) for man being qualified for obligation for and against him, while intellect is like a condition. Fakhr al-Islam interpreted it as 'the self (nafs) and the neck that has a covenant', and what is meant is the covenant. Their saying 'in his dhimma' means 'in his self considering its covenant', by way of applying the state and intending the place. This meaning is more suitable for this context. Further detail is in al-Nahr and elsewhere.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Kafalah (suretyship/guarantee) in the Hanafi fiqh context. It is from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on Sharh al-Wiqayah. The author first explains why kafalah follows sales: it frequently arises in transactions where one party lacks trust in the other, necessitating a guarantor. Then he provides the linguistic definition: kafalah means 'joining' (damm), as in the Qur'anic verse about Zakariya taking custody of Maryam and the hadith comparing the guarantor of an orphan to two fingers. He then quotes lexicographical sources to show the verbal forms and the distinction between kafil (guarantor) and kafil (supporter). The technical definition in the Sacred Law is simply 'what the author mentioned', i.e., 'joining one liability (dhimma) to another'. Next, he states the pillar (rukn) of kafalah: an offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) with specific words to be detailed later. The condition (shart) is that the guaranteed object (whether a person or property) must be deliverable by the guarantor. Thus, kafalah is not valid for hudud punishments or qisas because these are not deliverable by the guarantor; it is valid for debts. An exception is that kafalah for the substitute (badal) in a kitabah contract (contract of manumission by payment) is invalid, a point to be clarified later. The parties to the contract: the guarantor must be a person qualified to make a gratuitous contract (ahl al-tabarru'), so it is not valid from a slave, a minor, or a lunatic. However, a slave can be pursued after emancipation. The creditor (plaintiff) is the beneficiary (makful lahu), the debtor is the principal (makful anhu, also called al-asil). The guaranteed object (makful bihi) is the person in personal suretyship and the property in property suretyship. In personal suretyship, the principal and the guaranteed object are the same person. The guarantor is called kafil. The passage then delves into the meaning of 'dhimma' which is central to the definition of kafalah as 'joining one dhimma to another'. It quotes al-Misbah al-Munir: dhimma means covenant ('ahd), trust (aman), and guarantee (daman). The dhimmi (protected non-Muslim) is named from dhimma meaning covenant. Al-Mughrib adds that dhimma is the covenant, expressed as trust and guarantee, and is the place where liability is established. Al-Qadi Abu Zayd explains that dhimma is a quality (wasf) by which a person becomes capable of having rights and obligations. It is a divine gift from the day of the covenant (mithaq), separate from intellect ('aql). Intellect is only for understanding address (khitab); obligation (wujub) is built on dhimma. So dhimma is like the cause (sabab) for legal capacity (ahliyyah wujub), while intellect is a condition (shart) for obligation of performance. Fakhr al-Islam (al-Bazdawi) interprets dhimma as 'the self (nafs) and the neck that has a covenant', meaning the covenant itself. Thus, 'in his dhimma' means 'in his self with respect to his covenant'. This explanation is deemed more appropriate for this context.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafālah): A contract by which a person joins his liability (dhimma) to the liability of another, undertaking to fulfill an obligation.
الذمة (al-dhimmah): A divine quality (wasf) in a person that makes him capable of having rights and obligations; it is like a cause (sabab) for legal capacity (ahliyyah wujub).
الركن (al-rukn): The essential integral part of the contract without which it is invalid; here it is offer and acceptance.
الشرط (al-shart): An external condition required for the validity of the contract; here it is the deliverability of the guaranteed object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a condition for the validity of a kafalah contract according to this passage?
A) The guarantor must be a free adult Muslim.
B) The guaranteed object must be deliverable by the guarantor.
C) The creditor must explicitly request the guarantee.
D) The debt must be recorded in writing.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of kafalah centers on joining two dhimmas; its validity hinges on deliverability of the guaranteed object and the legal capacity of the guarantor.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
10 min
The Definition of Kafalah: Joining in Claim, Not in Debt
Defining kafalah and clarifying its nature

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct Hanafi definition of kafalah and explain why it is defined as joining in claim rather than debt.

Core Idea
Kafalah joins liability for claim only – the debt itself remains single and unduplicated.

The opening of the Book of Kafalah in al-Hidayah tackles a fundamental question: What exactly is a contract of suretyship? The Hanafi school distinguishes itself by defining kafalah as a joining of liabilities (dhimma) for the purpose of claim (mutalaba), not for the debt itself. This lesson unpacks that definition, the reasoning behind it, and the practical consequences that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Contrasting Definitions
Notice that the text presents two definitions: one defines kafalah as joining liability for claim, the other as joining liability for debt. Identify which is said to be 'al-asahh' (the most correct).
The phrase 'في المطالبة لا في الدين، هو الأصح' signals the preferred view.
2
Follow the Reasoning for the Preferred Definition
The text argues that if the debt were not established, the claim could not arise. Thus, the debt must be established first, but the guarantor's liability is only for the claim. Understand the logical sequence.
The debt remains single; only the right to demand payment attaches to the guarantor.
3
Analyze the Usurper Analogy
The text compares the situation to a usurper (ghasib) and a second usurper. Both are liable for the value of the property, but the owner's right is to a single value. Payment by one frees the other. This illustrates non-duplication of the debt.
This analogy reinforces that kafalah does not duplicate the debt.
4
Distinguish Kafalah bi'l-Mal and Kafalah bi'n-Nafs
In kafalah bi'l-mal, both principal and guarantor are demanded for the same money. In kafalah bi'n-nafs, the principal is demanded for money, the guarantor for surrendering the person. Yet both fall under 'claim'.
The definition in claim encompasses both types, while a definition in debt would not cover bi'n-nafs.
5
Apply the Definition to Validate Edge Cases
The text shows that waiver of the debt to the guarantor is valid, and buying with the debt from the guarantor is valid, because the debt is considered to be on the guarantor for those purposes. This confirms the definition.
These rulings would not make sense if the debt were merely duplicated.

Book of Kafalah: Its definition, conditions, and rulings. In claim, not in debt; that is the most correct opinion. (In claim, not in debt; that is the most correct opinion), and according to some, it is joining of liability to liability in debt. Because if the debt is not established, the claim is not established; and the most correct is the first. [11] His statement: 'in claim'. And he said in al-Manh: Its basis is that the guarantor and the principal become jointly demanded by the creditor, whether the demand from one is the same as the demand from the other, as in kafalah bi'l-mal, or not, as in kafalah bi'n-nafs, for the demand from the principal is money, and from the guarantor is the surrender of the person. And the term 'claim' in its absolute sense encompasses both. This is according to the view of some of them. And Miskeen asserted in Sharh al-Kanz that the demand from both is one, which is the surrender of the person, for the principal is required to surrender himself, and the guarantor has undertaken it. If you know this, it becomes clear to you that there is no need for the saying of Mulla Khusraw: It is joining of liability to liability in claim of the person, money, or surrender; because claim encompasses that. End quote. And let it not escape you that Mulla Khusraw said after it: 'I have chosen a correct definition, explicitly encompassing all divisions.' End quote. And it is apparent that explicitness in the definition is preferable. [2] His statement: 'because' etc. Its clarification: The undertaking of claim is based on the undertaking of the original debt. Thus the debt is established in the liability of the guarantor while remaining in the liability of the principal. And it is not collected except from one of them, like the usurper and the usurper's usurper. For each of them is liable for the value, and the right of the owner pertains to a single value. And his choice to hold one liable causes the acquittal of the other. For this reason, if the creditor waives the debt to the guarantor, it is valid, and the guarantor may claim it back from the principal. Even though gift of a debt from someone other than the debtor is not valid. And if the creditor buys from the guarantor a dead item with the debt, it is valid, despite the fact that buying with a debt from someone other than the debtor is not valid. Zubdat al-Nihaya li'Umdat al-Ri'aya. Because the debt is not duplicated; for if one of them pays it, nothing remains upon the other.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of kafalah (suretyship) and clarifies its fundamental nature within the Hanafi school. It begins by stating that the correct definition of kafalah is the joining of one liability (dhimma) to another in the context of claim (mutalaba), not in the debt itself. The author notes a differing view that defines it as joining in the debt, but the relied-upon position is the first. The passage then elaborates that the basis of kafalah is that both the guarantor (kafil) and the principal (asil) become subject to claim by the creditor, though the nature of the claim may differ: in kafalah bi'l-mal, both are demanded for the same money; in kafalah bi'n-nafs, the principal is demanded for money and the guarantor for surrendering the person. The author refines this by explaining that the debt itself is not duplicated; rather, only the claim attaches to the guarantor. The debt remains a single obligation, and payment by one party absolves the other. This is illustrated by the analogy of a usurper and his usurper, where both are liable for the value but the owner's right is to a single value. The passage also addresses apparent contradictions: if the creditor forgives the debt to the guarantor, it is valid, though forgiveness to a non-debtor generally is not, because in this case the debt is deemed to exist on the guarantor for the purpose of validating the transaction. Similarly, buying with the debt from the guarantor is valid. The concluding lines emphasize the principle that the debt is not duplicated; if one pays, the other is freed. This definition and clarification establish the foundational understanding of kafalah in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafāla): A contract of suretyship/guarantee; joining of one liability to another for the purpose of claim.
ذمة (dhimma): Legal liability or capacity for obligation; the entity in which debts and rights reside.
مطالبة (muṭālaba): Claim or demand; the right of the creditor to seek payment or performance from either the principal or the guarantor.
ضم (ḍamm): Joining or adding; in this context, adding one liability to another.
أصيل (aṣīl): The principal debtor; the original obligor for whom the guarantor provides surety.
كفيل (kafīl): The guarantor or surety; one who joins his liability to the principal's for the purpose of claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi definition, kafalah is the joining of liability to liability in what?
A) The debt (al-dayn)
B) The claim (al-mutalaba)
C) The person (al-nafs)
D) The property (al-mal)

Key Takeaway
Kafalah joins the guarantor's liability to the principal's for the purpose of claim, not debt, so the debt remains single and is not duplicated.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
12 min
The Validity of Kafalah bi'n-Nafs: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Explaining the two types of kafalah and rebutting the Shafi'i objection to kafalah bi'n-nafs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on kafalah bi'n-nafs and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith 'al-za'imu gharim'.

Core Idea
Kafalah bi'n-nafs is valid in Hanafi fiqh; the general hadith 'al-za'imu gharim' embraces both person and property suretyship.

Kafalah (suretyship) is divided into two types: kafalah bi'l-mal (suretyship for debt) and kafalah bi'n-nafs (suretyship for the person). While kafalah bi'l-mal is universally accepted, the validity of kafalah bi'n-nafs is disputed. Imam al-Shafi'i, in one opinion, considers it invalid because the guarantor cannot guarantee the surrender of a free person who may resist. The Hanafi school, however, upholds its validity based on a general hadith and practical reasoning. This lesson examines the proofs on both sides and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the two types of kafalah as stated in the text: kafalah bi'n-nafs and kafalah bi'l-mal. The Hanafi position is that both are valid.
2
Identify the opposing school and its argument
Imam al-Shafi'i, in one opinion, rejects kafalah bi'n-nafs because the guarantor cannot control the principal's body; the principal may resist. Note that this objection is logical but focuses on physical impossibility.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof
The Hanafis cite the hadith 'al-za'imu gharim' (the surety is liable) from Sunan al-Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud. The absolute wording covers both types. They also argue that surrender can be enforced via court officials, and the guarantor only undertakes for a compliant person.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection
The text rebuts the objection by showing that the hadith is general, and practical enforcement is possible through legal authorities. The term 'gharim' includes loss of liberty or property.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafi school permits suretyship for the person (e.g., bail bonds), while the Shafi'i school in that opinion does not. This affects real-world contracts and court procedures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

منه شيئاً.

[2] (And it is two types: 1. Of the person. 2. And of property. [1] His statement: 'because' etc.; its detail is that the debt is single, not duplicated, evidence being that if one of them pays it off, nothing remains upon the other. So making a single debt in the ruling of two debts would entail inverting the reality, thus it is not resorted to except in necessity, such as when the creditor gifts the debt to the guarantor, or buys something from him with it. For the debt is deemed to be upon the guarantor as well out of necessity to validate his transaction, but there is no necessity here, because the joint demand is achieved by multiplicity of claimants. As for usurpation from the usurper, only one debt is owed, from one of them, not specifically; thus if the owner chooses one of them, he cannot claim from the other, because it entails transferring ownership from him, as stated in more than one reliable source. [2] His statement: 'And it is...' etc., i.e., kafalah is divided into two types: one is kafalah of the person, the second is kafalah of property. Al-Shafi'i has a disagreement concerning the first, for according to him, kafalah of the person is not permissible in one opinion, because the guarantor is not able to surrender the guaranteed person, since the person is not compliant but rather resists and fights, unlike kafalah of property, because he is able to surrender his own property. And our proof: His statement: 'al-za'imu gharim' reported by al-Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud, meaning: the surety is a guarantor. The aspect of inference is that its absolute wording indicates the legitimacy of kafalah in both types. It cannot be said that there is no 'uzm (obligation) in kafalah of the person, for we say: 'uzm means that loss becomes binding upon him. And from it is His statement, peace be upon him: 'Indeed its punishment was a loss (gharam).' And it is possible to act upon it by making him (the principal) surrender to the creditor in a way that he cannot avoid, or by seeking the assistance of the judge's officers to surrender him, given that he only guarantees the person of one who is able to surrender himself and is compliant.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage outlines the two categories of kafalah (suretyship) and addresses a key inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity of kafalah bi'n-nafs (suretyship for the person). The author first reiterates that the debt is single and not duplicated, as established earlier. Then he presents the two types: kafalah of the person and kafalah of property. The main discussion centers on the Shafi'i objection to the first type. According to one opinion of Imam al-Shafi'i, kafalah bi'n-nafs is invalid because the guarantor cannot guarantee the surrender of a free person who may resist. The Hanafi response is based on the hadith 'al-za'imu gharim' (the surety is liable), which in its absolute wording supports both types. The Hanafis also argue that the guarantor can ensure surrender either by making arrangements for the principal to comply voluntarily or by utilizing the court's authority to enforce surrender. Additionally, the guarantor only undertakes for someone who is capable of surrender and is compliant. Thus, kafalah bi'n-nafs is valid and permissible. This passage demonstrates the Hanafi reliance on general textual evidence (the hadith) and practical reasoning to uphold a broader scope of suretyship.

Key Terms
كفالة بالنفس (kafāla bi'l-nafs): Suretyship for the person of the principal; the guarantor undertakes to produce the principal or surrender him to the creditor.
كفالة بالمال (kafāla bi'l-māl): Suretyship for property/debt; the guarantor is liable for the money owed by the principal.
زعيم (za'īm): Surety, guarantor; derived from hadith 'al-za'imu gharim'.
غارم (ghārim): Liable, responsible; one who bears loss or debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Kafalah bi'n-nafs is valid in the Hanafi school based on the general hadith 'al-za'imu gharim' and practical enforceability via court authorities.

Kafalah — Surety

Valid Expressions for Kafalah and Obligation to Produce
Opening of the book on kafalah defining valid expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid and invalid expressions for concluding a kafalah contract according to the Hanafi school, and explain the obligation to produce the guaranteed person.

Core Idea
Kafalah is concluded by clear expressions of personal guarantee; ambiguous phrases like 'guaranteeing acquaintance' are invalid.

The Hanafi school carefully defines which verbal expressions create a binding kafalah (personal surety) contract. This lesson unpacks the key wordings from the classical text, explains why some phrases are valid and others not, and shows how the obligation to produce the guaranteed person arises. You will learn to distinguish valid guarantees from invalid ones, both in classical terms and in contemporary contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary valid expression
The text begins by stating the core valid expression: 'kafaltu bi nafsihi' (I guarantee his person). Note that it must refer to the entire person, not just a part.
The expression 'bi nafsihi' is the standard; other phrases are analogized to it.
2
Analyze the scope of valid limb-based expressions
Expressions like 'head', 'face', or 'neck' are valid because they customarily represent the whole body. However, 'hand' or 'foot' are not. Understand the reasoning: these parts are not customarily used to denote the entire person in legal transactions.
The Hanafi position differs from Shafi'i and Maliki views on which parts suffice.
3
Examine expressions for fractional parts
Saying 'half' or 'third' is valid because the person is indivisible for kafalah purposes; mentioning a part implies the whole. The contract is concluded by mentioning any common fraction.
This reasoning is unique to Hanafi and relies on legal indivisibility.
4
Parse other valid synonyms and particles
Words like 'dimanah' (I guarantee), 'ala' (upon), 'ila' (to), 'za'im' (guarantor), and 'qabil' (surety) are all valid because they explicitly or implicitly create a commitment to deliver the person.
The text cites Quranic usage for 'za'im' and a hadith for 'ila' meaning 'upon'.
5
Identify the invalid expression and its consequence
The phrase 'ana damin li ma'rifatihi' (I am a guarantor for his acquaintance) is invalid because it guarantees knowledge of the person, not delivery. Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute and why the majority view (invalid) is relied upon for fatwa.
While Abu Yusuf allowed it customarily, the mufta bihi is that it does not conclude kafalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فالأول ينعقد: بكفلت بنفسه، ونحوها مما يعبر به عن بدنه، وبنصفه وبثلثه، وبضمنته، أو علي، أو إلي، أو أنا به زعيم، أو قبيل. ويلزمه إحضار المكفول به إن طلب المكفول له، فإن لم يحضره يحبسه الحاكم.
قوله: يكفلت؛ ولم يذكر محمد له ما إذا كفلت بعينه، قال البلخي له: لا يصح كما في الطلاق، إلا أن ينوي به البدن، والذي يجب أن تصح الكفالة به كالطلاق، إذ العين مما يعبر به عن الكل، يقال: عين الكل، وهو عين في الناس، ولعله لم يكن معروفاً في زمانهم، أما في زماننا فلا شك في ذلك، كذا في «الفتح».
قوله: مما يعبر به عن بدنه؛ أي مما يعبر به من أعضائه عن جميع البدن كرأسه ووجهه ورقبته وعنقه وجسده وبديه، بأن يقول: تكفلت برأسه أو بوجهه... الخ؛ لأن هذه الألفاظ يعبر بها عن جميع البدن حقيقة أو عرفاً، ولو كفل بيده أو برجله لا تنعقد الكفالة؛ لأنهما لا يعبر بها عن البدن؛ ولهذا لا يقع الطلاق ولا العتاق بهما. وقال الشافعي: تنعقد الكفالة بجزء لا يمكن فصله كالقلب والكبد، وبه قال أحمد في رواية، وقال مالك: بكل عضو من البدن، ذكره العلامة السمني في «كمال الدراية».
قوله: وبنصفه؛ وغير ذلك من الأجزاء الشائعة؛ لأن النفس الواحدة في حق الكفالة لا تتجزؤ، إذ المستحق بكفالتها إحضارها وإحضار جزءها الشائع دون كلها لا يتصور، فصار ذكره كذكر كلها.
قوله: وبضمنته؛ أي بقوله: ضمنت لك فلاناً؛ لأنه تصريح بمقتضى عقد الكفالة، فإنه يصير به ضامناً للتسليم، والعقد ينعقد بالتصريح بمقتضاه، كما أن البيع ينعقد بلفظ التمليك.
وأما علي فلان: كلمة علي للالتزام، فكأنه قال: أنا الملتزم تسليمه، وأما إلي فلان: كلمة إلي بمعنى علي، كما في قوله: (من ترك كلاً فإلي، ومن ترك مالاً فلورثته) رواه أبو داود والنسائي وابن ماجة من حديث المقدام بن معد يكرب.
قوله: أو أنا به زعيم؛ لأن الكفيل يسمى زعيماً، قال الله تعالى حكاية عن صاحب يوسف: (وأنا به زعيم) أي كفيل، وكذا القبيل، ولهذا سمي الصك: قباله؛ لأنه يحفظ الحق كالكفيل، ولا تنعقد الكفالة بقوله: أنا ضامن لمعرفته؛ لأنه ضمن المعرفة لا التسليم.
وقال أبو يوسف: يصير ضامناً للعرف، وقال الفقيه أبو الليث: هذا القول عن أبي يوسف غير مشهور، والظاهر ما عنهما، وبظاهر الرواية يفتى، كما في أكثر الكتب.
Book of Kafalah: its definition, conditions, and rulings. The first (type) is concluded: by (saying) 'I guarantee his person' and similar expressions that refer to his body, and by half of him and by a third of him, and by 'I guarantee him', or 'upon me', or 'to me', or 'I am his guarantor (za'im)', or 'qabil'. And it is obligatory for him to produce the guaranteed person if the beneficiary demands, and if he does not produce him, the judge imprisons him. (Commentary): His statement: 'I guarantee (kafaltu)'; Muhammad did not mention the case where one guarantees by his 'ayn (self). Al-Balkhi said: it is not valid as in divorce, unless he intends the body. What is correct is that kafalah is valid by it like divorce, since 'ayn denotes the whole. It is said: 'ayn of the whole, and it is 'ayn in people. Perhaps it was not known in their time, but in our time there is no doubt about it, as in al-Fath. His statement: 'what refers to his body' meaning the limbs that refer to the entire body, like his head, face, neck, throat, torso, and body, such as saying 'I guarantee his head' etc., because these words refer to the entire body literally or customarily. If one guarantees by his hand or foot, the kafalah is not concluded because they do not refer to the body; therefore divorce and manumission do not occur by them. Al-Shafi'i said: kafalah is concluded with a part that cannot be separated, like the heart and liver, and Ahmad agreed in one narration. Malik said: with every limb of the body. This was mentioned by al-Simnani in Kamal al-Dirayah. His statement: 'and by half of him' and other common parts, because the single person with respect to kafalah is indivisible, since the right to produce him is to produce him and producing his common part without all of him is inconceivable, so mentioning it is like mentioning all of him. His statement: 'and by my guarantee (dimanah)' i.e., his saying 'I guarantee you so-and-so', because it is explicit in the requirement of the kafalah contract, for he thereby becomes a guarantor for delivery, and the contract is concluded by explicit statement of its requirement, just as sale is concluded by the word of ownership transfer. As for 'upon me so-and-so': the word 'upon' is for commitment, as if he said 'I am committed to deliver him.' As for 'to me so-and-so': the word 'to' means 'upon', as in his statement: 'Whoever leaves a dependant, then to me; whoever leaves wealth, then to his heirs' reported by Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i, and Ibn Majah from the hadith of al-Miqdam ibn Ma'dikarib. His statement: 'or I am a za'im for him': because the guarantor is called za'im; Allah Almighty said relating from the master of Yusuf: 'And I am a za'im for him' i.e., a guarantor. Likewise al-qabil, and therefore the document is called qabala because it preserves the right like the guarantor. Kafalah is not concluded by saying 'I am a guarantor for his acquaintance' because he guaranteed acquaintance, not delivery. Abu Yusuf said: he becomes a guarantor customarily. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: this statement from Abu Yusuf is not well-known, and the apparent is from both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and by the apparent transmission fatwa is given, as in most books.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on kafalah (suretyship) in the Hanafi madhhab. It first defines the valid expressions that conclude a kafalah contract. The primary valid expression is 'I guarantee his person' (kafaltu bi nafsihi) or similar words that refer to the body. The text then lists specific particles: 'bi nisfihi' (half), 'bi thulthihi' (third), 'bidimanatihi' (his guarantee), 'ala' (upon), 'ila' (to), 'ana bihi za'im' (I am his guarantor), and 'qabil' (surety). The legal effect is that the guarantor (kafil) must produce the person when demanded; otherwise, the judge may imprison him. The commentary elaborates on each term. On 'kafaltu bi'aynihi, al-Balkhi's view is cited that it is invalid unless intending the body, but later scholars accept it as valid due to custom. On limbs: expressions like 'head' or 'face' are valid because they represent the whole body customarily, but 'hand' or 'foot' are not. A dispute among madhhabs is noted: Shafi'i allows kafalah with a non-separable part (heart, liver), Malik with any limb. Hanafi ruling restricts to what customarily represents the body. For 'bidthulthihi' (third) and other common parts, the reasoning is that the person is legally indivisible in kafalah, so mentioning a share is like mentioning the whole. The words 'dimanah', 'ala', 'ila', 'za'im', 'qabil' are all valid because they imply a commitment to deliver. Importantly, the phrase 'I am a guarantor for his acquaintance' (ana damin li ma'rifatihi) is invalid because it guarantees knowledge, not delivery. Abu Yusuf reportedly allowed it customarily, but the relied-upon (mufta bihi) opinion is the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) as per the zahir al-riwayah. This demonstrates the Hanafi method of parsing legal language and prioritizing explicit intent over custom.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contract guaranteeing the presence of a person (suretyship for the person).
زعيم (za'im): A guarantor or surety for a person's presence.
قبيل (qabil): A guarantor; also the word from which 'qabala' (document) is derived.
ضمنت (dimanah): Expression of guarantee; becomes a commitment to deliver the person.
عين (ayn): The self or essence of a person; in kafalah, it denotes the whole person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following expressions does NOT conclude a valid kafalah (suretyship)?
A) بكفلت بنفسه (I guarantee his person)
B) أنا به زعيم (I am his guarantor/za'im)
C) أنا ضامن لمعرفته (I am a guarantor for his acquaintance)
D) ضمنته (I guarantee him)

Key Takeaway
A valid kafalah requires a clear expression guaranteeing the delivery of the person, not merely knowledge of them; the Hanafi school provides specific valid wordings and rejects ambiguous ones.

Obligation to Produce the Guaranteed Person and Imprisonment
Ruling on the guarantor's duty and imprisonment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a guarantor is imprisoned for failing to produce the guaranteed person in Hanafi law, and distinguish the procedure for admitting versus denying guarantees.

Core Idea
A guarantor's imprisonment requires demand, failure without excuse, and a warning process for those who admit.

This passage from the Hanafi fiqh tradition addresses a critical aspect of kafalah bi al-nafs: the legal consequences when a guarantor fails to produce the person. The text outlines not only the general rule of imprisonment but also important distinctions based on the guarantor's acknowledgment and the whereabouts of the guaranteed person. Understanding these nuances is essential for applying the law correctly in a judicial setting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary obligation
Note that the guarantor is legally bound (lazim) to produce the guaranteed person upon demand by the guarantee-holder. This is the starting point for any enforcement.
The text uses 'ويلزمه' indicating a binding duty.
2
State the consequence of failure without excuse
If the guarantor does not produce the person after demand, and the failure is not due to genuine inability ('ajz), the judge may imprison the guarantor.
The condition 'بغير عجز' is critical; imprisonment is a coercive measure, not a punishment for incapacity.
3
Distinguish between acknowledging and denying guarantors
The passage from al-Khazanah introduces a procedural difference: if the guarantor admits the kafalah, he is not imprisoned immediately but after two or three warnings (pushed). If he denies, and the demandant proves the kafalah (or the guarantor refuses the oath), imprisonment is immediate.
This distinction reflects a leniency toward those who acknowledge their obligation.
4
Handle the absence of the guaranteed person
If the guaranteed person himself is absent, the judge grants the guarantor a period sufficient for travel and return. If the period expires without production, then imprisonment follows due to confirmed refusal.
This shows the court's concern for practicality and fairness.
5
Synthesize the conditions for imprisonment
Combine all elements: demand, failure without 'ajz, appropriate warnings (if admitting), or immediate enforcement (if denying and proven), and allowance for absence.
The Hanafi school aims to balance the right of the guarantee-holder with the guarantor's circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلزمه إحضار المكفول به إن طلب المكفول له، فإن لم يحضره يحبسه الحاكم. فإن لم يحضره : أي فإن لم يحضر الكفيل المكفول به بعد الطلب بغيرِ عَجْز، حبسه الحاكم ؛ لامتناعه عن إيفاء ما وجب عليه. وفي «الخزانة»: إذا كان مقراً بالكفالة لا يحبسه أوّل مرة ، وإنما يحبس بعد الدفع مرتين أو ثلاث مرات، وإن كان منكراً وقامت البيِّنة، أو نكل عن الحلف عند القاضي يحبسه في أوَّل مرَّة، كذا في عامَّةِ الحقوق، ذكره البرْجَنْدِيُّ في «شرح النقاية». وهكذا في «البزازية» (١) ، ولو غاب المكفولُ بنفسه أمهله الحاكم مدة ذهابه ومجيئه ، فإن مضت ولم يحضره يحبسه لتحقق امتناعه عن إيفاء الحق ، كذا في «الهداية»(۲).
And it is incumbent upon him to produce the guaranteed person if the guarantee-holder demands, and if he does not produce him, the judge imprisons him. 'If he does not produce him' means: if the guarantor does not produce the guaranteed person after the demand without inability, the judge imprisons him due to his refusal to fulfill what is obligatory upon him. And in al-Khazanah: If he admits the kafalah, he is not imprisoned the first time; rather, he is imprisoned after being pushed two or three times. But if he denies and evidence is established, or he refuses to take an oath before the judge, he is imprisoned the first time. This applies to all rights generally, as mentioned by al-Birjandi in Sharh al-Niqayah. And similarly in al-Bazzaziyyah. And if the guaranteed person himself is absent, the judge gives him time for his travel and return; if the time passes and he does not produce him, he imprisons him because his refusal to fulfill the right is confirmed, as in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage deals with the consequences of a guarantor's failure to produce the guaranteed person (al-makful bihi) in a kafalah bi al-nafs (suretyship for the person). The matn states that the guarantor must produce the person upon demand; otherwise the judge (hakim) may imprison him. The commentary clarifies that imprisonment is only valid if the failure is without valid excuse (bi ghayr 'ajz). The text then discusses a distinction from al-Khazanah: if the guarantor acknowledges the kafalah, he is not imprisoned immediately but after being warned two or three times; if he denies the kafalah and the demandant proves it, or if he refuses the oath (nukul), he is imprisoned at once. This is the general rule for all financial rights (huquq). Another case: if the guaranteed person is absent, the judge gives a period for travel and return; if the guarantor fails to produce him after that, he is imprisoned for confirmed refusal. The authority weight is from later fatwa works (al-Khazanah, al-Bazzaziyyah, al-Hidayah).

Key Terms
حبس (habs): Imprisonment by the judge as a coercive measure to compel the guarantor to produce the person.
مقر (muqirr): One who acknowledges (confesses) the kafalah.
عجز ('ajz): Inability to produce the person due to genuine incapacity (e.g., death, unknown location).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately upon demand
B) After one warning
C) After two or three pushes (warnings)
D) Only if he denies the kafalah

Key Takeaway
A guarantor's imprisonment for failing to produce the guaranteed person requires demand, lack of genuine inability, and follows a graduated procedure based on acknowledgment.

Time-Specified Kafalah and Discharge by Death
Rulings on time-specified kafalah and discharge by death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on time-specified kafalah and explain why death of the guaranteed person discharges the guarantor, distinguishing personal from property kafalah.

Core Idea
A specified time delays liability, and death ends personal kafalah permanently.

This passage from `Umdat al-Ri`ayah presents two clear rulings from the Hanafi school on kafalah. First, if a guarantor specifies a time for delivery, he is only bound after that time, but early production discharges him. Second, if the guaranteed person dies, the guarantor is fully released because delivering a deceased person is impossible. Understanding these distinctions—especially the difference between personal and property kafalah—is essential for correctly applying the rules of suretyship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the passage and locate the two distinct legal statements: the ruling on time-specified kafalah and the ruling on discharge by death of the guaranteed person (makful bihi).
2
Analyze the time-specified ruling
The text states that if a time for delivery is specified, the guarantor must produce the person at that time or after, but not before. If he produces earlier, he is discharged even if the creditor refuses. Understand that the guarantor's undertaking is for a single delivery, and early performance fulfills it.
Al-Minah adds that in zahir al-riwayah, the guarantor cannot be demanded immediately; fatwa is on this.
3
Analyze the death ruling
The passage explains that death discharges the guarantor due to total impossibility (al-'ajz al-kulli). The heirs do not inherit the obligation of personal kafalah because they inherit only assets, not liabilities related to personal presence.
This contrasts with kafalah al-mal, where the guarantor remains liable even after the debtor's death.
4
Distinguish personal from property kafalah
The text explicitly compares: 'unlike kafalah for property (mal).' In property kafalah, death of the principal does not discharge the guarantor because the debt can be recovered from the estate. In personal kafalah, the obligation ends with the person's life.
This distinction is a key feature of Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن عَيَّنَ وقت التسليم لزمَهُ ذلك. ويبرأ : بموتِ مَن كفل به [١] قوله : لزمه ذلك ؛ أي لزم الكفينُ إحضار المكفول به في ذلك الوقت، إذا طلب المكفول لـه في ذلك الوقت أو بعده ؛ لأنه التزمه كذلك ، فإن سلَّمه إليه قبل مجيء ذلك الوقت برئ الكفيل، وإن لم يقبله المكفول له ؛ لأنه ما التزم تسليمه إلا مرة، وقد أتى به. وفي «المنح : إذا كفل إلى ثلاثةِ أَيَّام كان كفيلاً بعد الثلاثة، ولا يطالب في الحالِ في ظاهر الرواية ، وبه يفتى. انتهى. وزيادة التفصيل في «البحر»(4).
[٢] قوله : ويبرأ بموت من كفل به ; أي يبرأ الكفيل بموتِ المكفول به ؛ لحصول العجز الكلي عن تسليم المطلوب من الكفيل، وورثته لم يكلفوه شيئا، وإنما يخلفونه فيما له، لا فيما عليه، ولا تبقى الكفالة باعتبار تركيه ؛ لامتناع استيفاء النفس من المال بخلاف الكفالة في المال.
And if he specified a time for delivery, that becomes binding upon him. And he is discharged by the death of the one he guaranteed. [1] His saying 'that becomes binding upon him' means: the guarantor is bound to produce the guaranteed person at that time, if the guarantee-holder demands at that time or after, because he undertook it that way. If he hands him over before the arrival of that time, the guarantor is discharged, even if the guarantee-holder does not accept him, because he only undertook delivery once, and he has performed it. And in al-Minah: If he guaranteed until three days, he becomes a guarantor after the three days, and he is not demanded immediately, according to zahir al-riwayah, and this is given fatwa. End. And further detail in al-Bahr. [2] His saying 'and he is discharged by the death of the one he guaranteed' means: the guarantor is discharged by the death of the guaranteed person, due to total incapacity to deliver the demanded from the guarantor. His heirs have not imposed anything on him; they only succeed him in what he has, not in what he owes. And the kafalah does not remain based on his estate because it is impossible to exact the person from property, unlike kafalah for property (mal).
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage covers two sub-rules of kafalah. First, if a time for delivery is specified (e.g., 'I guarantee to produce him by a certain date'), the guarantor must produce at that time. However, if he produces the person before the time, he is discharged even if the creditor refuses to accept, because he has fulfilled his undertaking. The text from al-Minah mentions a case: if kafalah is for three days, the guarantor is only responsible after three days, and cannot be demanded immediately in zahir al-riwayah, and this is the fatwa position. Second, the guarantor is discharged upon the death of the guaranteed person (al-makful bihi). The reason is complete inability (al-'ajz al-kulli) to deliver the person. The heirs do not assume the obligation because they inherit what the deceased owned, not what he owed in terms of personal presence. Kafalah al-nafs (for the person) ends with death, unlike kafalah al-mal (for property), which may be pursued from the estate. This distinction is crucial: in property kafalah, the guarantor remains liable even after the original debtor's death, but in personal kafalah, death of the guaranteed ends the obligation. The authority weight for the time-specified rule is zahir al-riwayah and the fatwa position; for the death ruling, it is a direct ruling from the matn.

Key Terms
عجز كلي ('ajz kulli): Total impossibility of performing the obligation (here, delivering the person due to death).
براءة (bara'ah): Legal discharge or release from the obligation of kafalah.
تركه (tarkah): Estate of the deceased (inheritance).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The guarantor is not discharged and must produce again at the time
B) The guarantor is discharged because he has performed his undertaking once
C) The guarantor must continue to hold the person until the time
D) The guarantor may withdraw from the kafalah

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a time-specified personal guarantee binds only after the term, and death of the guaranteed person completely discharges the guarantor due to impossibility.

Fixing Time for Delivery in Kafalah: Early Discharge
Ruling on fixing delivery time and early discharge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a guarantor specifies a delivery time and explain the effect of early delivery.

Core Idea
The surety is discharged by delivering the person even before the agreed time.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling from the chapter of kafalah (surety). The matn declares that if a guarantor specifies a time to produce the guaranteed person, that time becomes binding. The commentary then clarifies the surprising consequence: delivering the person early discharges the guarantor, even if the beneficiary refuses to accept. Understanding this rule requires appreciating the nature of the obligation—it is a single undertaking, not a recurring duty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is the guarantor specifying a time for delivery. The ruling: it becomes binding (lazim) to deliver at that time.
The act is a condition set by the surety; it is not inherent in the contract itself.
2
Locate the basis of the ruling
The commentary states 'because he undertook it thus' (li-annahu iltazamahu kathalik). The obligation arises from the voluntary undertaking.
The proof is not a revealed text but the principle of al-umur bi-maqasidiha and iltizam.
3
Trace the consequence of early delivery
The key point: delivering before the time discharges the surety, even if the beneficiary does not accept. Why? Because the surety only undertook to surrender once (ma iltazama taslimahu illa marrah), and he has already done so.
Early delivery fulfills the obligation; the beneficiary's refusal does not undo the discharge.
4
Note the sub-ruling on deferred kafalah
The commentary adds: if the guarantee is 'for three days', the surety is not demanded immediately; he is only a surety after three days. This is from zahir al-riwayah and is the fatwa.
This clarifies that the time clause creates a deferment; the surety cannot be pressured earlier.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text indicates that the ruling that the surety is not demanded immediately is the fatwa position. The Hanafi school relies on this, though some scholars may differ on early discharge.
The cross-madhhab dispute is noted: some hold early delivery does not discharge, but the Hanafi position is as stated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن عَيَّنَ وقت التسليم لزمَهُ ذلك. ويبرأُ : بموتِ مَن كفل به [١] قوله : لزمه ذلك ; أي لزم الكفينُ إحضار المكفول به في ذلك الوقت، إذا طلب المكفول لـه في ذلك الوقت أو بعده ; لأنه التزمه كذلك، فإن سلَّمه إليه قبل مجيء ذلك الوقت برئ الكفيل، وإن لم يقبله المكفول له ; لأنه ما التزم تسليمه إلا مرة، وقد أتى به. (۳) وفي «المنح : إذا كفل إلى ثلاثةِ أَيَّام كان كفيلاً بعد الثلاثة، ولا يطالب في الحالِ في ظاهر الرواية ، وبه يفتى. انتهى. وزيادة التفصيل في «البحر»(4).
And if he specifies a time for delivery, that becomes binding upon him. And he is discharged: by the death of the one for whom he became surety. [1] His statement: 'that becomes binding upon him' means that it becomes obligatory upon the surety to produce the guaranteed person at that time, when the beneficiary requests it at that time or after it, because he undertook it thus. If he surrenders him to him before the arrival of that time, the surety is discharged, even if the beneficiary does not accept him, because he only undertook to surrender him once, and he has already brought him. (3) And in al-Manh: 'If he becomes surety for three days, he is a surety after the three days, and he is not demanded immediately in the apparent narration, and this is the fatwa. End.' More detail in al-Bahr (4).
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn with its commentary. It deals with kafalah bil-nafs (surety for the person). The base text states two rulings: first, if the surety specifies a time for producing the guaranteed person, he is bound to deliver at that time. Second, the surety is discharged by the death of the guaranteed person. The commentary explains the first ruling: the surety must produce the person at the specified time if the beneficiary requests then or later. If the surety delivers the person before that time, he is discharged even if the beneficiary does not accept, because the surety's obligation was only to deliver once. The commentary also quotes from al-Manh that if the surety is for three days, he is a surety only after three days; he is not demanded immediately in the zahir al-riwayah, and that is the fatwa. Further detail is found in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): Surety, guarantee; here specifically kafalah bil-nafs, a guarantee to produce a person.
المكفول به (al-makful bihi): The person for whom the surety is given; the guaranteed person.
المكفول له (al-makful lahu): The beneficiary of the guarantee; the one to whom the person is to be delivered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Discharge of Surety by Death of Guaranteed Person
Ruling with proof (ta'lil) on discharge by death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling and reasoning for discharge of a surety (kafalah bil-nafs) upon the death of the guaranteed person, and distinguish it from kafalah bil-mal.

Core Idea
Death of the guaranteed person automatically discharges the surety because personal delivery is impossible and cannot be converted into a property claim.

In Hanafi kafalah, suretyship for a person (kafalah bil-nafs) is distinct from suretyship for property (kafalah bil-mal). This passage from a commentary on Al-Wiqayah establishes that the surety is discharged upon the death of the guaranteed person due to complete inability to deliver. It also clarifies that the obligation does not pass to the deceased's estate, because a personal attendance cannot be extracted from property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The base text states: the surety is discharged by the death of the person for whom he became surety.
This is an automatic discharge, not requiring any action.
2
Find the reasoning (ta'lil)
The commentary gives the cause: complete inability to deliver the demanded thing (العجز الكلي عن تسليم المطلوب).
The 'illah is impossibility – a central concept in Hanafi ta'lil.
3
Compare with kafalah bil-mal
The passage contrasts this with surety for property: 'بخلاف الكفالة في المال' – in property suretyship, the obligation may transfer to the estate.
This highlights the different nature of the two types of kafalah.
4
Understand the implication for heirs
Heirs inherit what the deceased owned, not what he owed concerning personal suretyship.
The obligation is personal to the debtor and cannot be enforced against his assets.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools may treat kafalah bil-nafs as transferring to the estate; the Hanafi school distinguishes based on the nature of the obligation.
This ikhtilaf has practical consequences if the surety is sued after the debtor's death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبرأُ : بموتِ مَن كفل به [٢] قوله : ويبرأ بموت من كفل به ; أي يبرأ الكفيل بموتِ المكفول به ; لحصول العجز الكلي عن تسليم المطلوب من الكفيل، وورثته لم يكلفوه شيئا، وإنما يخلفونه فيما له، لا فيما عليه، ولا تبقى الكفالة باعتبار تركيه ; لامتناع استيفاء النفس من المال بخلاف الكفالة في المال.
And he is discharged: by the death of the one for whom he became surety. [2] His statement: 'And he is discharged by the death of the one for whom he became surety' means that the surety is discharged by the death of the guaranteed person, because of the complete inability to deliver the demanded thing from the surety. His heirs have not obliged him to do anything; they only succeed him in what he owns, not in what he owes. And the suretyship does not remain with regard to his estate, because it is impossible to exact the soul from property, unlike suretyship for property.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and its explanation from the commentary. The base text states that the surety is discharged by the death of the guaranteed person. The commentary provides the reasoning: death creates a complete inability to deliver the person. The heirs of the deceased are not liable for this obligation; they inherit only assets, not liabilities of this nature. The suretyship does not transfer to the estate because it is impossible to extract a person's presence from property. This distinguishes kafalah bil-nafs from kafalah bil-mal (surety for property), where the obligation may transfer to the estate.

Key Terms
الكفالة في المال (al-kafalah fi al-mal): Surety for property or financial obligation, as opposed to surety for the person.
الكفالة في النفس (al-kafalah fi al-nafs): Surety for the person – guaranteeing that someone will appear or perform a personal act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi passage, why is a surety for the person (kafalah bil-nafs) discharged when the guaranteed person dies?
A) Because the creditors forgive the debt
B) Because of complete inability to deliver the person
C) Because the surety can pay instead
D) Because the heirs refuse to cooperate

Key Takeaway
Death of the guaranteed person automatically discharges a kafalah bil-nafs because personal delivery becomes impossible and cannot be converted into a property claim.

Surety for a Slave: Death Discharge Conditions
Ruling on discharge when the guaranteed person is a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions under which a surety is discharged or liable when the guaranteed slave dies, and apply the distinction based on the nature of the claim.

Core Idea
Discharge of surety for a slave depends on whether the claim is for money or for the slave himself.

This passage from a commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies a nuanced rule in kafalah. When a person becomes surety for the person of a slave and the slave dies, the surety is not automatically discharged; the outcome hinges on what exactly was claimed from the slave. We will examine the distinction drawn in al-Khulasah and ensure we apply it correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage carefully and note the two cases: (1) the claim is for money owed by the slave, (2) the claim is for the slave himself (e.g., ownership).
The key phrase is 'إن كان المدعى به المال' vs 'إن كان المدعى به نفس العبد'.
2
Understand the legal consequence for each
For a money claim, the surety is discharged upon the slave's death. For a claim on the slave himself, the surety is not discharged and must pay the slave's value.
Death ends the possibility of delivering the slave's person, but if the claim was for the person, the surety's obligation converts to monetary compensation.
3
Locate the authority of the ruling
The passage explicitly cites al-Khulasah as the source. Note that it is a later Hanafi work (fatawa mutakhkhirin), so the ruling reflects established school principles.
The authority_weight is 'fatawa_mutakhkhirin'.
4
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Consider a case where the surety guaranteed the slave against a claim of stolen property. Determine which scenario applies.
If the claim is for return of the stolen goods (money equivalent), it is a money claim; if the claim is that the slave himself is stolen property, it is a claim for the slave himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أنه عبد [١] قوله : ولو أنه عبد ; وهذا إذا كان على العبد مال مطالب به، وكَفل بنفسه رجل، أما إذا كان المدعي به نفس العبد لا يبرأ ، صرَّح به في «الخلاصة»، حيث قال: لو كفل بنفس عبد فمات العبد برئ الكفيلُ إن كان المدعى به المال على العبد، وإن كان المدعى به نفس العبدِ لا يبرأ، وضمن قيمته. انتهى.
And even if he is a slave. [1] His statement: 'And even if he is a slave'; this applies if there is a monetary claim against the slave, and a man becomes surety for his person. But if the claim is for the slave himself, he is not discharged. It is explicitly stated in al-Khulasah: 'If he becomes surety for the person of a slave and the slave dies, the surety is discharged if the claim was for money owed by the slave. But if the claim was for the slave himself, he is not discharged and is liable for his value.' End.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario in kafalah bil-nafs where the guaranteed person is a slave. The ruling distinguishes two cases: if the claim against the slave is for a monetary debt (i.e., the slave owes money), then the surety is discharged upon the slave's death. However, if the claim is for the slave himself (e.g., someone claims ownership of the slave), then the surety is not discharged and must pay the slave's value. This distinction is taken from al-Khulasah. The reasoning is that in the first case, the obligation is essentially financial, so death ends the possibility of delivery; in the second case, the object of the guarantee is the slave's person, so his death creates a duty to compensate with his value.

Key Terms
المدعى به (al-mudda'a bihi): That which is claimed; the subject of the lawsuit.
كفيل (kafil): Surety; one who guarantees the person (kafalah bil-nafs) of another.
الخلاصة (al-Khulasah): A later Hanafi fiqh compilation (fatawa mutakhkhirin) often cited for relied-upon rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man becomes surety for the person of a slave, and the slave dies, when is the surety NOT discharged?
A) When the claim against the slave is for a debt
B) When the claim is for the slave himself
C) When the surety is also a slave
D) When the slave dies a natural death

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi kafalah, the surety for a slave is discharged upon the slave's death only if the claim was monetary; if the claim was for the slave himself, the surety must pay the slave's value.

Delivery of the Guaranteed Person: Place and Conditions for Discharge
Ruling on discharge of surety by delivering the guaranteed person, with conditions and analogical proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a surety is discharged from liability for the person in Hanafi kafalah and apply the qiyas analogy to similar cases.

Core Idea
Delivery of the guaranteed person anywhere litigation is possible discharges the surety, just as paying a debt discharges the debtor.

In Hanafi kafalah bi al-nafs, the surety is responsible for producing the guaranteed person when required. This lesson examines the ruling on how the surety can be discharged by delivering the person to the beneficiary. The classic text from al-Hidayah clarifies that discharge is automatic upon effective delivery, even if place conditions are not met.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The surety delivers the guaranteed person. The ruling is that the surety is discharged from liability for the person.
The discharge is a necessary consequence of delivery.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The author uses analogy (qiyas) to the debtor who pays a debt, the usurper who returns property, and the seller who delivers goods.
These analogies show that discharge follows from the act itself.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the analogy prove the ruling? In each case, the obligation is fulfilled by the act that achieves its purpose, so discharge is automatic without needing explicit declaration.
The common illa is that the obligation is completed by delivering the subject matter.
4
Note the conditions regarding request
Does the ruling change if the delivery is without the beneficiary's request? If delivery is after request, no explicit words needed. If without request, the surety must say 'I deliver him by virtue of the suretyship'.
Otherwise the delivery may be construed as mere friendly action, not discharge.
5
Understand the definition of delivery
What constitutes delivery here? It means takhliyah (relinquishing control) by removing obstacles so the beneficiary can take the person.
Simply pointing out the person suffices if the beneficiary can take him.

And he delivers him to the one for whom he became surety where it is possible for him to litigate, even if he does not say 'I have delivered him to you, so I am discharged.' If he stipulated delivery in the judge's council, and delivers him in the market, or in another city, he is discharged. [2] His statement: 'And by delivering him...' means that if the surety delivers the guaranteed person to the beneficiary where it is possible for him to litigate, the surety is discharged from the liability for the person, even if he does not say 'I have delivered him to you, so I am discharged,' because discharge is the necessary consequence of delivery, so it is established even if not explicitly stated, like the debtor when he pays the debt, the usurper when he returns the usurped item, and the seller when he delivers the sold item. Then it is either that he delivers him after being asked or not. If after being asked, he is discharged even if he does not say 'I have delivered him to you by virtue of the suretyship,' because it implies affirming the claimant's request. If he delivers without being asked, he is not discharged until he says 'I have delivered him to you by virtue of the suretyship.' What is meant by delivery here is: relinquishing control between him and the opponent, by removing obstacles, so he says: 'This is your opponent, take him if you wish.' [3] His statement: 'he is discharged' because the purpose has been achieved. The purpose of delivery in the judge's council...
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with its detailed commentary. The base text states two key points: (1) the surety is discharged by delivering the guaranteed person to the beneficiary in a place where the beneficiary can initiate legal proceedings (i.e., where he can access a court). (2) Even if the surety stipulated delivery in the judge's council, delivering elsewhere (market, another city) still discharges him. The commentary elaborates: discharge occurs even without the surety explicitly saying 'I am discharged,' because the delivery itself implies the discharge, analogous to returning a debt, usurped property, or sold goods. However, if the surety delivers without being asked, he must explicitly state that he is delivering by virtue of the suretyship, otherwise discharge is not automatic. If delivery is after request, saying 'I deliver him to you' suffices. 'Delivery' here means removing obstacles so the beneficiary can take the person, e.g., 'This is your opponent, take him if you wish.' The purpose of delivery in the judge's council is achieved by any effective delivery.

Key Terms
المخاصمة (al-mukhasamah): Litigation; the ability to bring a legal claim before a judge.
التخلية (al-takhliyah): Relinquishing control; removing obstacles so that the beneficiary can take possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Release of the Surety by Delivery: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Location
Explains rulings on surety release and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on delivery location

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the release of a surety by delivery of the principal outside the judge's court and identify the mufta bihi position in later times.

Core Idea
The surety is released by delivering the principal where litigation is possible, but later Hanafi fatwa restricts release to delivery in court due to corruption.

This lesson delves into a key intra-Hanafi dispute concerning kafāla bi al-nafs (suretyship for the person). The central question: does delivery of the principal (makfūl bihī) to the creditor anywhere release the surety, or must it be in the judge's court? The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya traces the early position of Imam Abū Ḥanīfa, the objections of his two companions (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad), and the later adjustment by Zufr that became operative in fatwa. Understanding this evolution shows how Hanafi jurists responded to changing social conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core act and its default rule
Determine what act triggers release of the surety (kafīl) and what the default condition is according to the text.
The act is taslīm (delivery) of the principal to the creditor. The default rule is that delivery anywhere enabling litigation releases the surety.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
Report Imam Abū Ḥanīfa's view: delivery in any location where litigation is possible releases the surety. Identify his rational proof (qiyās on debtor paying debt).
Abū Ḥanīfa uses qiyās: just as a debtor is released by paying anywhere, the surety is released by delivering anywhere litigation can occur.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi variant and its proof
State the view of the Ṣāḥibayn (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad): only delivery in the stipulated court releases. Identify their proof: the condition of the court is meaningful because witnesses may be specific to that court.
They argue that the stipulation of the court is a condition; breaking it prevents release.
4
Analyze the later adjustment by Zufr
Explain Zufr's extension: even delivery in the same city but outside the court session does not release in later times. Identify the social rationale: corruption and lack of cooperation.
Zufr's view becomes the mufta bihi because of widespread negligence in establishing rights.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilāf and mufta bihi
Conclude the practical difference: under Abū Ḥanīfa, delivery in the market releases; under the later fatwa, it does not. The mufta bihi is Zufr's opinion.
The shift shows how Hanafi fiqh adapts to changing circumstances while preserving the legal framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أنه عبد ) ؛ وإِنما قال هذا دفعاً لتوهم أنَّ العبد مال ، فإذا تعدَّرَ تسليمه لزمَهُ قيمته.
ويدفعه إلى من كفل له حيث يُمْكِنُهُ مخاصمته، وإن لم يقل إذا دفعت إليك فأنا بريء، فإن شَرْطَ تسليمه في مجلس القاضي، وسَلَّمَهُ في السُّوقِ، أو في مصر آخر برئ 
واعلم أن المصنف له تبع صاحب الهداية) في بطلانها بمــوت الكفيل ، واختاروه في أكثر المتون، لكن ذكرَ في «السراج الوهاج» أنه في «مختصر الكَرْخي» في «باب الصلح عن الحقوق التي ليست بمال أنها لا تبطل بموت الكفيل، ويطالب وارثه بإحضاره. انتهى. [١] قوله : ولو أنه عبد ؛ وهذا إذا كان على العبد مال مطالب به، وكَفل بنفسه رجل، أما إذا كان المدعي به نفس العبد لا يبرأ ، صرَّح به في «الخلاصة»، حيث قال: لو كفل بنفس عبد فمات العبد برئ الكفيلُ إن كان المدعى به المال على العبد، وإن كان المدعى به نفس العبدِ لا يبرأ، وضمن قيمته. انتهى.
