[۳] قوله : برئ ؛ لحصول المقصود ، فإنَّ المقصود من التسليم في مجلس القاضي إمكان الخصومة واستخراج الحق بإثبات حقه عليه، وهذا الإمكان حاصل من تسلمه في مكان آخر.
وقال شمس الأئمة السرخسي : في زماننا لا يبرؤ إذا شرط التسليم في مجلس القضاء بالتسليم في مكان آخر ؛ لأنَّ الظاهر أنه لا يعاونه أحد من أهل الطريق والسوق على إحضار المكفول به في مجلس القاضي ، بل يعاونونه على الامتناع ؛ لغلبة الفسَّاقِ والمفسدين، وقيل: يجب أن يفتى بذلك ، كذا في «الفتح» وفي «الملتقى»(۲) : والمختار في زماننا أنه لا يبرأ. انتهى. أى سواء كان التسليم في سوق ذلك المصر أو في سوق مصر آخر. وفي «المنح»(۳) : وهو قول زفر له، وبه يفتى في زماننا ؛ لتهاونِ النَّاسِ في إقامة الحق، ومحل الاختلاف في بلد لم يعتادوا نزع الغريم من يد الخصم. كذا في «البحر» «التاتارخانية»، وهذه إحدى المسائل التي يفتى فيها بقول زفر رحمه الله علة. انتهى.
نقلا عن (٤) انتهى.
وأما إذا سلمه في مصر آخر غير المصر الذي كفل فيه، فقد برئ عند الإمام ؛ للقدرة على المخاصمة في ذلك المصر، فإنَّ المحاكمة تتحقق عند كـل قاض، فصار التسليم في البلدان سواء، وعندهما لا يبرؤ ؛ لأنه قد يكون شهودُهُ فيما عينه فيتعسر عليه إقامة البينة في بلد آخر.
وقيل : اختلاف عصر وزمان، فإن أبا حنيفة الله كان في الثاني، وقد شهد رسولُ الله لأهله بالصدق، فكانت الغلبة لأهل الصلاح، والقضاة لا يرغبون إلى الرشوة، وعامل كل مصر منقاد لأمر الخليفة، فلا يقعُ التفاوتُ بالتسليم إليه في ذلك المصر أو في مصر آخر.
And if he is a slave) – he said this only to refute the misunderstanding that a slave is property, so if his delivery becomes impossible, he (the surety) is liable for his value. And he delivers him to the one for whom he stood surety as long as it is possible for him to litigate against him, even if he did not say: 'If I pay you, I am free.' For if he stipulated delivery in the judge's court, but delivered him in the market, or in another city, he is released. Know that the author follows the author of al-Hidayah regarding its invalidity upon the death of the surety, and they chose this in most texts. But it is mentioned in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that in Mukhtasar al-Karkhi, in the chapter on compromise over rights that are not property, it does not become invalid upon the death of the surety, and his heir is asked to present him. This is explicitly stated in al-Khulasa, where he said: 'If one stands surety for the person of a slave and the slave dies, the surety is released if the claim is for property owed by the slave; but if the claim is for the slave himself, he is not released and is liable for his value.' End. [2] His statement: 'And by delivering him...' etc. – meaning if the surety delivers the principal (makful bihi) to the creditor (makful lahu) where it is possible to litigate against him, the surety for the person is released, even if he did not say: 'If I pay you, I am free,' because the effect of delivery is release, so it is established even if not explicitly stated, like the debtor when he pays the debt, the usurper when he returns the usurped item, and the seller when he delivers the sold item. Then it is either after demand or not. If after demand, he is released even if he did not say: 'I deliver him to you by virtue of suretyship,' because it implies repeating the claimant's statement. But if he delivers without demand, he is not released until he says: 'I deliver him to you by way of suretyship.' The meaning of 'delivery' here is vacating between him and the opponent, i.e., removing impediments, so he says: 'This is your opponent, take him if you wish.' [3] His statement: 'He is released' – because the purpose is achieved, for the purpose of delivery in the judge's court is the possibility of litigation and extracting the right by establishing his claim against him, and this possibility is achieved by delivery in another place. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: In our time, he is not released if delivery is stipulated in the court but occurs elsewhere, because it is apparent that no one from the road or market will help him bring the principal to the judge's court; rather, they help him to refuse due to the prevalence of transgressors and corruptors. It is said that fatwa should be given accordingly. As in al-Fath and al-Multaqa: The chosen position in our time is that he is not released. End. That is, whether the delivery is in the market of that city or in the market of another city. In al-Munah: This is the opinion of Zufr, and fatwa is given accordingly in our time due to people's negligence in establishing rights. The place of disagreement is in a town where they are not accustomed to extracting the debtor from the hand of the opponent. As in al-Bahr and al-Tatarkhaniyya. This is one of the issues in which fatwa is given according to the opinion of Zufr, may Allah have mercy on him. End. Quoted from (4) End. As for if he delivers him in another city other than the city in which he stood surety, he is released according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa) because of the ability to litigate in that city, for litigation can occur before any judge, so delivery in all cities is the same. But according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he is not released because his witnesses may be in the specified city, making it difficult for him to establish evidence in another city. It is said (this is) a difference of time and era. Abu Hanifa – may Allah be pleased with him – was in the second century, when the Messenger of Allah testified to the truthfulness of his family, so the virtuous prevailed, judges were not inclined to bribery, and every city's governor was subject to the caliph's order, so there was no difference between delivery in that city or another city.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqaya, itself a commentary on al-Hidayah, dealing with kafala bi al-nafs (suretyship for the person). The passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the conditions under which a surety (kafil) is released from liability when delivering the principal (makful bihi) to the creditor (makful lahu). The author first clarifies a special case: if the principal is a slave and the claim is for property owed by the slave, the surety is released if the slave dies; but if the claim is for the slave himself, the surety remains liable for the slave's value. Then, the main rule: the surety is released by delivering the principal to the creditor where litigation is possible, even without an explicit statement of release, similar to a debtor paying a debt or a seller delivering goods. However, if the surety stipulated delivery in the judge's court, but delivers elsewhere (market or another city), the rule is that he is released because the purpose — enabling litigation — is achieved. Yet, Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi and later scholars modify this: in later times (due to corruption), delivery outside the court does not release the surety, as people will not help bring the principal to court. This is the opinion of Zufr, which becomes the mufta bihi (operative for fatwa). The crux of the dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa says delivery in another city releases the surety because litigation is possible before any judge. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree, fearing loss of witnesses. Later scholars, observing moral decay, adopt Zufr's stricter view. The passage thus records a shift in the madhhab's operative ruling over time, a hallmark of Hanafi juristic development.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafāla): Suretyship; guaranteeing the presence of a person (kafāla bi al-nafs) or payment of a debt (kafāla bi al-māl).
مكفول به (makfūl bihī): The principal whose person or debt is guaranteed by the surety.
كفيل (kafīl): The surety who guarantees to produce the principal or pay the debt.
تسليم (taslīm): Delivery; giving over the principal to the creditor, or delivering a sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later fatwa (mufta bihi) mentioned in the text, when does the surety (kafil) not be released despite delivering the principal?
A) When he delivers the principal in the judge's court as stipulated.
B) When he delivers the principal in the market or another city without court delivery.
C) When he delivers the principal but does not say the release phrase.
D) When he delivers the principal after the creditor's demand.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's operative ruling on surety release shifted from delivery anywhere litigation is possible to requiring delivery in the judge's court, reflecting adaptation to social corruption.

Delivery in Another City: Discharge and Change of Fatwa Over Time
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn on discharge when kafil delivers in a different city

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on discharge when a guarantor delivers in a different city, explain the rationale for the change of fatwa, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A guarantor is only discharged if delivery is in the city of guarantee, because later times required restricting to the original city due to corruption.

This passage addresses a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: if a guarantor hands over the guaranteed person in a city other than the one of the guarantee, does he become discharged? Imam Abu Hanifa held that he does, based on the universal jurisdiction of judges. But Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad disagreed, citing the difficulty of producing witnesses in a foreign city. The text then reveals a remarkable principle: the difference is attributed to the change of times, and the fatwa later shifted to the view that discharge does not occur.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine the precise question: whether delivering the guaranteed person in a city different from the city of guarantee discharges the guarantor from liability.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa rules that the guarantor is discharged because the creditor can litigate before any judge, making all cities equivalent for jurisdictional purposes.
His proof is the ability to bring a case in the city of delivery.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the guarantor is not discharged because the creditor's witnesses may be in the original city, causing hardship in presenting evidence elsewhere.
Their proof focuses on the practical difficulty for the creditor.
4
Understand the temporal explanation
The text explains that the difference is due to the era: Abu Hanifa lived in a righteous time with honest judges and obedient governors, so delivery anywhere was safe. Later, with corruption, the fatwa shifted to restrict delivery to the city of guarantee.
This shows that fiqh rulings can adapt to changing social conditions.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon fatwa today is that discharge does not occur when delivery is in a different city. This follows the view of Zufar, who agreed with the Sahibayn.
The change in fatwa is a practical application of the principle that rulings can change with time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا سلمه في مصر آخر غير المصر الذي كفل فيه، فقد برئ عند الإمام؛ للقدرة على المخاصمة في ذلك المصر، فإنَّ المحاكمة تتحقق عند كـل قاض، فصار التسليم في البلدان سواء، وعندهما لا يبرؤ؛ لأنه قد يكون شهودُهُ فيما عينه فيتعسر عليه إقامة البينة في بلد آخر. وقيل: اختلاف عصر وزمان، فإن أبا حنيفة الله كان في الثاني، وقد شهد رسولُ الله لأهله بالصدق، فكانت الغلبة لأهل الصلاح، والقضاة لا يرغبون إلى الرشوة، وعامل كل مصر منقاد لأمر الخليفة، فلا يقعُ التفاوتُ بالتسليم إليه في ذلك المصر أو في مصر آخر.
And as for if he delivers him in another city other than the city in which he stood surety, then he is discharged according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa) due to the ability to litigate in that city, because litigation can occur before any judge, so delivery in all cities is the same. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he is not discharged, because his witnesses might be in the designated city and it would be difficult for him to present evidence in another city. And it is said: This is a difference of era and time, for Abu Hanifa was in the second [century], and the Messenger of God testified to his family's truthfulness, so the righteous were dominant, and judges did not desire bribes, and the governor of each city was submissive to the caliph's command, so there was no variation in delivering to him in that city or in another city.

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on a guarantor (kafil) who hands over the guaranteed person (makful bihi) in a city different from the one where the guarantee was made. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the guarantor is discharged because the creditor can initiate legal proceedings in that other city, as any judge has jurisdiction. Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad disagree, arguing that the creditor's witnesses may be in the original city, making it difficult to prove his claim elsewhere. The text then notes that this difference is tied to the era: Abu Hanifa lived in a time of general righteousness, when judges were honest and governors obeyed the caliph, so delivering in any city was equally effective. However, in later times, corruption spread, and the fatwa shifted to the view that discharge does not occur unless delivery is in the city of guarantee. This change reflects the adaptation of fiqh to social conditions.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where one person guarantees the presence or debt of another.
المكفول به (al-makful bihi): The person or debt for which the guarantor stands surety.
البراءة (al-bara'ah): Discharge; the guarantor being freed from liability after fulfilling the guarantee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Any city, because all judges have jurisdiction.
B) Only if he delivers in a location where the creditor can easily bring the person to court.
C) Never.
D) Only if the delivery is in the same city as the guarantee and in a place under the same qadi's authority.

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi fatwa restricts discharge to delivery in the city of guarantee, a shift from Abu Hanifa's view, justified by changing temporal conditions.

Delivery in Desert, Village, or Prison: The Purpose of Kafalah is Litigation
Ruling that a guarantor is not discharged by delivering in a desert, village, or prison where litigation is impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a guarantor is discharged from liability by delivering the guaranteed person, and apply the rule that inability to litigate prevents discharge.

Core Idea
A guarantor is not discharged unless the delivery location enables the creditor to bring the debtor to court.

This lesson examines a key principle of kafalah: the guarantor's liability is not discharged simply by handing over the person; the delivery must be effective in enabling the creditor to litigate. We will explore the specific cases of desert, village, and prison, and understand how the purpose (maqsad) governs the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Identify the act being ruled upon: delivery of the guaranteed person in specific locations (desert, village, prison). The ruling is that the guarantor is not discharged (lā yabra').
The base text states this clearly.
2
Understand the 'illa (reason)
The reason the delivery does not discharge is that the creditor cannot bring the debtor to court in those locations. The purpose of kafalah is to enable litigation, not just physical handover.
This is the key legal reasoning (talil).
3
Apply the 'illa to prison cases
If the prison is under the same judge who hears the case, the judge can summon the prisoner, so the creditor can litigate; thus discharge occurs. But if it's a different judge's prison, the creditor cannot easily bring the debtor to court, so no discharge.
The commentary from al-Muhit provides this crucial distinction.
4
Extend the reasoning to modern contexts
The text adds that in the commentator's time, even delivery in the market does not discharge because no one helps bring the debtor to court. Analogously, today any location where litigation is impractical prevents discharge.
This shows the principle adapts to circumstances.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on delivery of the guaranteed person in places where the creditor cannot easily bring him to court. Invalid Delivery Locations in Kafalah
Explains when delivery of the guaranteed person does not discharge the guarantor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on valid delivery (taslim) in kafalah and identify which locations discharge the guarantor and which do not.

Core Idea
Delivery of a guaranteed person only discharges the guarantor if it enables the creditor to bring the debtor to court.

This passage clarifies that not every physical surrender of the guaranteed person releases the guarantor. The decisive factor is whether the creditor gains the practical ability to litigate. The commentary explores deserts, rural areas, and prisons, and discusses a later change due to judicial corruption. This lesson will walk through the conditions for valid delivery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what 'taslim' (delivery) is and when it discharges the guarantor. The original ruling states that delivery in a desert, rural area, or a prison where someone else imprisoned the debtor does not discharge.
2
List the invalid delivery locations
From the text: desert (bariyyah), rural lands (sawad), and prison (sijn) where the debtor is imprisoned by someone other than the claimant. Understand why each fails: no qadi in desert/rural, no access in prison.
The purpose is to enable the creditor to bring the debtor to court.
3
Understand the rationale (ta'lil)
The underlying reason is achieving the objective (tahsil al-maqsud). If the creditor cannot bring the debtor to a court with jurisdiction, the delivery is futile and does not discharge.
Istihsan is used to prioritize the maqsud over mere physical delivery.
4
Explore the exception: prison of the same qadi
If the debtor is imprisoned in a prison under the same qadi who hears the case, delivery is valid because the qadi can summon the debtor from prison. This is contrasted with a prison in another city under a different qadi.
The qadi's authority over the prison makes bringing the debtor possible.
5
Analyze the later change due to corruption
In the time of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, due to widespread judicial corruption, delivery is now restricted to the same city where the suretyship was made. This change protects the creditor from harm.
The later view becomes the mufta bihi.

And if he delivers in a desert, or in rural lands, or in a prison, and someone else imprisoned him, then it does not discharge: it is said: in our time he is not discharged by delivering in the market because no one assists him in bringing him to the court. Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the matn regarding the discharge of a guarantor (kafil) through delivery of the guaranteed person (makful bihi). The original ruling states that delivery in a desert, rural area, or a prison where someone else imprisoned the debtor does not discharge the guarantor. The commentary explains the legal reasoning: delivery only achieves the objective if the creditor can bring the debtor to a court capable of adjudicating. In a desert or rural area, there is no qadi, so the creditor cannot litigate. In a prison under another's authority, the creditor cannot bring the debtor to court. However, if the prison is under the same qadi, delivery is valid because the qadi can summon the debtor. The commentary also notes a later change in the time of Imam Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) due to corruption among qadis; to protect the creditor, delivery is now restricted to the same city where the suretyship was given, even if the o[؟]iginal ruling might have been broader. The citation from al-'Inayah confirms this.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where the kafil guarantees the presence of the makful bihi (the person) before the makful lahu (the creditor).
تسليم (taslim): Delivery of the guaranteed person by the guarantor to the creditor in a manner that enables the creditor to bring the debtor to court.
مكفول له (makful lahu): The creditor or person entitled to the presence of the guaranteed person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The same city where the suretyship was made
C) A market where people can help
D) Any prison of a qadi

Key Takeaway
Valid delivery must make it possible for the creditor to bring the debtor to court; otherwise the guarantor remains liable.

Valid Discharge Methods in Kafalah: Self-Delivery, Agent, and Creditor's Death
Lists additional discharge methods and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a guarantor is discharged by self-delivery, agent delivery, and after the creditor's death, applying the Hanafi ruling on the necessity of explicit release from suretyship.

Core Idea
A guarantor is discharged only upon explicit delivery 'from the suretyship' — the debtor's self-delivery must be by his order, and the creditor's death transfers the right to heirs.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents three additional scenarios where a guarantor (kafil) is released from his obligation. These cover delivery by the guaranteed person himself, by the guarantor's agent or messenger, and the situation when the creditor dies. The commentary nuances each case, especially the requirement that the delivery be explicitly for release from the suretyship and that self-delivery only discharges if the debtor originally consented to the guarantee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three discharge methods
List the three ways the guarantor is discharged as stated in the matn: (1) self-delivery by the guaranteed person, (2) delivery by the guarantor's agent or messenger, (3) after the creditor's death, the heir or executor may demand the guaranteed person; the guarantor remains obliged until delivery.
The text numbers these as 3 and 4, with the death clause separate.
2
Understand the condition 'from his suretyship'
The phrase 'min kafalatihi' (from his suretyship) means delivery must be explicitly intended to release the guarantor. The author applied it only to self-delivery, but the commentator argues it is a condition for all methods. This is a key intra-Hanafi point.
If the delivery is not explicitly for release, the guarantor is not discharged.
3
Analyze the debtor's consent distinction
Self-delivery discharges only if the suretyship was with the debtor's order (bi-amrihi). Without his order, the debtor need not attend; self-delivery does not discharge. However, if the guarantor later finds and delivers him, he is discharged.
The debtor may flee if not obligated, and does not sin by doing so.
4
Examine the effect of creditor's death
The creditor's death does not void the kafalah. The right to demand the guaranteed person passes to the executor or heir of the creditor. The guarantor remains obligated to them.
This ensures the suretyship continues for the benefit of the estate.
5
Note the imprisonment sub-rule
Using the 'Bahr' and 'Bazzaziyya' sources, if the guarantor is imprisoned with the debtor and delivers him in that imprisonment, he is discharged. If released and re-imprisoned separately, delivery is valid only for trade-related matters, not for state authority matters.
This shows the importance of the context of imprisonment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٣]قوله : ولو مات المكفول له ...الخ ; يعني إذا مات المكفول له لم تبطل الكفالة (۱) «البحر الرائق (٦ : ٢٢٩). (۲) (الفتاوى البزازية (٦ : ١٧). (۳) (حاشية الطحطاوي على الدر(٣: ١٤٨). (٤) منح الغفار» (ق۲ : ۷۹/ب).
3. And by delivery of the one who became surety of himself from his suretyship: meaning delivery of the guaranteed person himself from the suretyship of the guarantor. 4. And by delivery of the agent of the guarantor and his messenger to him: 'to him' is attached to delivery, and the pronoun refers to the creditor. (And if the creditor dies, then the executor and the heir have the right to demand him): meaning demanding the guarantor for the guaranteed person. And in 'al-Bahr' from 'al-Bazzaziyyah': If he is surety while he is imprisoned, and he delivers him in it, he is discharged. And if he is released and then imprisoned a second time, and he hands him over to him in it: the second imprisonment, if in matters of trade and the like, the handing over is valid, but if in matters of the sultan and the like, it is not. [1] His saying: 'and by delivery...' etc.: meaning the guarantor is discharged if the guaranteed person delivers himself, and this is when the suretyship was by the order of the demanded party. But if it was without his order, he is not discharged, and the reason is apparent: because if the suretyship was without his order, the demanded party is not obliged to attend, so he is not required to deliver; thus if he delivers himself, the guarantor is not discharged. As in 'al-Nahr'. And in that case, the guarantor has no claim against him, unless he finds him and delivers him, then he is discharged, and the demanded party does not sin by not enabling himself because he can flee, unlike when it is by his order. As in the Hashiyah of our Shaykh al-Tahtawi on al-Durr al-Mukhtar. [2] His saying: 'from his suretyship': this is a condition in all; meaning the guarantor is not discharged until the guaranteed person says: 'I have delivered myself to you from the suretyship.' And the agent and messenger are like the guaranteed person; delivery must be from them, otherwise he is not discharged. As in 'al-Minah'. And it is not hidden from you that the author made this condition specific to the delivery of the guaranteed person himself, whereas it is a condition in all. So if he had placed this condition later, it would have been better. [3] His saying: 'and if the creditor dies...' etc.: meaning if the creditor dies, the suretyship does not become void.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage states three additional ways the guarantor can be discharged. First, the guaranteed person may deliver himself voluntarily from the suretyship. Second, the guarantor's agent or messenger can deliver the guaranteed person. Third, the death of the creditor does not annul the kafalah; the executor or heir inherits the right to demand the guaranteed person. The commentary clarifies that self-delivery only discharges if the suretyship was with the debtor's consent (bi-amrihi). If it was without his order, the debtor is not obligated to attend; his self-delivery does not discharge the guarantor, though if the guarantor later finds and delivers him, he is discharged. The phrase 'from his suretyship' (min kafalatihi) is a condition that the delivery must be explicitly for release from the kafalah. The author originally applied this condition only to self-delivery, but the commentator notes it should apply to all. On the death of the creditor, the guarantor remains obliged to the heir or executor.

Key Terms
وكيل الكفيل (wakil al-kafil): The agent appointed by the guarantor to deliver the guaranteed person.
رسول الكفيل (rasul al-kafil): The messenger sent by the guarantor to deliver the guaranteed person.
مطالبة (mutalabah): The right to demand the guaranteed person from the guarantor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A guarantor is discharged only when the delivery is explicitly for release from the suretyship; the creditor's death transfers the right to heirs, and self-delivery requires the debtor's consent.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Discharge by Surrender of the Principal in Kafalah
Explaining conditions for surety's discharge by surrendering the principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a surety is discharged by surrendering the principal, and distinguish between suretyship with and without the principal's request.

Core Idea
A surety is discharged only when the principal explicitly surrenders himself from the suretyship, and only if the suretyship was at his request.

In Hanafi fiqh, a surety (kafil) guarantees to produce the principal (makful bihi) to the creditor (makful lahu). The surety may be discharged by surrendering the principal, but this is not automatic. The text from the commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies three key conditions: the principal's own request for the suretyship, an explicit declaration of surrender from the suretyship, and the continued obligation even after the creditor's death. Let's walk through these conditions step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the surrender of the principal by the surety. The ruling is that the surety is discharged (yabra'u al-kafil) when the principal surrenders himself, but only under specific conditions.
The text begins with wa bi taslim man kafala bihi nafsahu min kafalatih.
2
Check the type of suretyship: with or without principal's request
The surety is discharged only if the suretyship was undertaken at the request of the principal (bi amr al-matlub). If without his request, surrendering the principal does not discharge the surety; he must find and surrender him later.
The text: 'hadhihi idha kanat al-kafalah bi amr al-matlub, amma idha kanat bi ghayr amrihi fala yabra'u.'
3
Requirement of explicit declaration from the suretyship
The principal must explicitly say 'I have surrendered myself to you from the suretyship' (sallamtu nafsi ilayka min al-kafalah). Mere physical handover is insufficient. This applies to the principal, his agent, and his messenger.
The text: 'min kafalatih hadha qayd fi al-jami', meaning the surety is not discharged until the principal states this.
4
Effect of the creditor's death on suretyship
When the creditor dies, the suretyship does not become void. The executor (wasi) or heir (warith) steps in and may demand the principal from the surety. If the surety surrenders the principal to one heir only, other heirs retain the right to demand him because each heir's right is independent.
The text mentions an alternate view that discharge occurs at death of claimant, but the relied-upon position (al-madhhab) is that the suretyship continues.
5
Synthesize the conditions for valid discharge
For the surety to be discharged by surrender: (1) suretyship at principal's request, (2) principal explicitly surrenders from the suretyship (himself or via agent/messenger), (3) surrender to the creditor or, after death, to all heirs/executors (not just one).
If any condition is missing, the surety is not discharged except by other means like finding and surrendering the principal later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من كتاب الكفالة: وبتسليم من كفل به نفسه من كفالته وبتسليم وكيل الكفيل ورسوله إليه، ولو مات المكفول له فللوصي والوارث مطالبته به. أي يبرؤ الكفيل إذا سلم المكفول به نفسه، وهذا إذا كانت الكفالة بأمر المطلوب، أما إذا كانت بغير أمره فلا يبرؤ، والوجه فيه ظاهر؛ لأن الكفالة إذا كانت بغير أمره لا يلزم المطلوب الحضور، فليس مطالباً بالتسليم، فإذا سلم نفسه لا يبرؤ الكفيل. كذا في النهر. وحينئذ لا مطالبة للكفيل عليه، إلا أن يجده فيسلمه فيبرؤ ولا يأثم المطلوب بعدم تمكينه، فله الهرب بخلاف ما إذا كان بأمره، كذا في حاشية شيخنا الطحطاوي على الدر المختار. (من كفالته) هذا قيد في الجميع، يعني لا يبرؤ الكفيل حتى يقول المكفول: سلمت نفسي إليك من الكفالة، والوكيل والرسول كالمكفول لا بد من التسليم عنها، وإلا لا يبرؤ، كذا في المنح. ولا يخفى عليك أن المصنف جعل هذا القيد خاصاً بتسليم المكفول له نفسه والحال أنه قيد في الجميع، فلو أخر هذا القيد لكان أولى. (ولو مات المكفول له فللوصي والوارث مطالبته به) يعني إذا مات المكفول له لم تبطل الكفالة، بل للوصي أن يطالب الكفيل، إن لم يكن الوصي فلوارثه المطالبة؛ لأن كل واحد من الوصي والوارث قائم مقام الميت، فلو سلمه إلى أحد من الورثة أو الوصيين خاصة، فللباقي المطالبة بإحضاره. فإن حق المطالبة ثابت لكل واحد من الورثة، فإذا استوفى أحدهم حقه لا يسقط حق الباقين؛ لأن له استيفاء حقه فقط، وإنما قام مقام الباقين في إثبات حقهم، فلا يشكل عليه قولهم: إن أحد الورثة ينتصب خصماً للميت في ماله، وعليه قيل: يبرؤ الكفيل بموت الطالب، والمذهب هو الأول، كذا صرح به في الدر المختار وغيره.
From the book of Kafalah: "And by the surrender of the one for whom he stood surety himself from his suretyship, and by the surrender of the surety's agent and his messenger to him, and if the creditor dies, then the executor and the heir may demand him from the surety." That is, the surety is discharged when the principal surrenders himself, and this is when the suretyship was at the request of the principal. But if it was without his request, he is not discharged, and the reason is apparent because when the suretyship is without his request, the principal is not obliged to attend, so he is not required to surrender; so if he surrenders himself, the surety is not discharged. Thus in al-Nahr. And then there is no claim by the surety against him, unless he finds him and surrenders him, then he is discharged and the principal is not sinful for not enabling him, so he may flee, contrary to when it was at his request. Thus in the gloss of our teacher al-Tahtawi on al-Durr al-Mukhtar. "From his suretyship" - this is a condition in all cases, meaning the surety is not discharged until the principal says: "I have surrendered myself to you from the suretyship." And the agent and the messenger are like the principal; surrender must be from them, otherwise he is not discharged. Thus in al-Minah. And it is not hidden from you that the author made this condition specific to the surrender of the principal himself, whereas it is a condition in all cases; if he had delayed this condition it would have been better. "And if the creditor dies, then the executor and the heir may demand him from the surety" - meaning when the creditor dies, the suretyship is not void; rather the executor may demand the surety, and if there is no executor, then the heir may demand; because each of the executor and the heir stands in place of the deceased. So if he surrenders him to one of the heirs or to one of the executors specifically, the remaining heirs may demand his presence. For the right of demand is established for each of the heirs, so if one of them receives his right, the right of the others does not fall; because he only receives his own right, and he only stands in place of the others in establishing their right. So it should not be problematic their saying: "One of the heirs becomes a litigant for the deceased regarding his wealth." And based on this, it is said: "The surety is discharged by the death of the claimant." And the madhhab is the first. Thus it is explicitly stated in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and others.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah discusses the conditions under which a surety (kafil) is discharged from his obligation by surrendering the person guaranteed (al-makful bihi). The commentator explains that the surety is discharged only when the principal surrenders himself explicitly from the suretyship, not merely by being handed over. This applies whether the surrender is by the principal himself, his agent, or his messenger. However, this rule holds only if the suretyship was undertaken at the request of the principal (bi amr al-matlub). If the surety guaranteed without the principal's request, the principal is not obliged to present himself, and surrendering him does not discharge the surety; rather, if the surety finds and surrenders him, he is discharged, but the principal is not sinful for fleeing. The commentary also clarifies that the phrase "from his suretyship" is a condition in all cases: the principal must state that he surrenders himself from the suretyship. The author of the matn had limited this condition to the principal's own surrender, but the commentator argues it applies generally. Finally, the passage discusses the death of the creditor (makful lahu). The suretyship does not become void; the executor or heir steps in and may demand the principal from the surety. If the surety surrenders the principal to one heir only, the other heirs retain their right to demand him, because each heir's right is independent. There is a reported view that the surety is discharged by the death of the claimant, but the relied-upon position (al-madhhab) is that the suretyship continues. This commentary is based on Hanafi fiqh and references works like al-Nahr, al-Minah, and al-Durr al-Mukhtar.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): Suretyship; a guarantee by which a person (kafil) undertakes to produce the principal (makful bihi) to the creditor (makful lahu).
الكفيل (al-kafil): The surety; the person who guarantees to produce the principal.
المكفول به (al-makful bihi): The person guaranteed; the principal who is to be produced by the surety.
المكفول له (al-makful lahu): The creditor; the one to whom the principal is owed or who has the right to demand the principal.
البراءة (al-bara'ah): Discharge; the release of the surety from his obligation upon fulfilling the condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what must the principal say to discharge the surety when surrendering himself?
A) 'I am here.'
B) 'I surrender myself to you from the suretyship.'
C) 'I am free.'
D) 'The surety is discharged.'

Key Takeaway
For a surety to be discharged by surrendering the principal, the suretyship must be at the principal's request, and the principal must explicitly declare surrender from the suretyship; the creditor's death does not discharge the surety.

Conditional Surety with Debt Guarantee: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional surety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad on the validity of a conditional surety combining person and debt guarantees, and state the basis (istihsan vs risk) for each.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf validates conditional person+debt surety; Imam Muhammad voids the debt part as risk unless by istihsan.

In Hanafi kafalah, sureties can be for a person (kafalah bi al-nafs) or for a debt (kafalah bi al-mal). When both are combined in a conditional clause—'if I fail to produce him, I guarantee his debt'—the validity hinges on whether the debt obligation introduces unacceptable risk (mukhatara) or is permissible by istihsan. This passage presents an internal Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, with practical consequences for drafting sureties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of the surety
Recognize that the surety combines a person guarantee (produce the principal) and a debt guarantee (pay what is owed) under a condition.
The condition is temporal: 'if not produced by tomorrow'.
2
State the condition triggering debt liability
The surety becomes liable for the debt only if the principal is not produced by the deadline. This differs from open-ended demands (e.g., 'whenever demanded') which may be invalid after death.
The matn specifies a fixed time ('tomorrow') to avoid ambiguity.
3
Compare the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Abu Yusuf validates the entire conditional surety without requiring specification of the debt amount. Muhammad holds the person guarantee valid but voids the debt guarantee because it involves risk (mukhatara) when the debt is owed by someone else.
Muhammad's exception: if the debt is against the surety himself, it is valid by istihsan.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Yusuf's view, the creditor can claim both the person and the debt from the surety. Under Muhammad's view, only the person guarantee stands; the debt guarantee is void.
This affects how sureties are structured in practice.
5
Analyze the basis of each position
Abu Yusuf relies on the general permissibility of conditional contracts. Muhammad argues that guaranteeing another's debt conditionally is akin to gambling unless the surety has a personal stake (the debt being against him).
Istihsan (juristic preference) overrides strict analogy for Muhammad in the exception case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كفل بنفسه على أنه إن لم يواف به غداً، فهو ضامن لما عليه (فإن كفل بنفسه على أنه إن لم يواف به غداً) : أي أن يأتي به غداً، (فهو ضامن لما عليه) (إن لم يواف) قيد بعدم الموافاة للاحتراز عما في البزازية: كفل بنفسه على أنه متى طالبه سلمه، فإن لم يسلمه فعليه ما عليه، ومات المطلوب وطالبه بالتسليم وقد عجز لا يلزمه المال؛ لأن المطالبة بالتسليم بعد الموت لا تصح، فإذا لم تصح المطالبة لم يتحقق العجز الموجب للزوم المال، فلم يجب، كذا في البحر. (لما عليه) أشار إلى أنه لا يشترط تعيين قدر المال، وقيد بقوله: لما عليه؛ لأنه لو قال: فالمال الذي لك على فلان رجل أخر، وهو ألف درهم فهو علي، جاز في قول أبي يوسف له. وقال محمد: الكفالة بالنفس جائزة، والكفالة بالمال باطلة؛ لأنه مخاطرة إذا كان المال على غيره، وإنما يجوز إذا كان المال عليه استحسانا، ولو كفل بنفس رجل للطالب عليه مال، فلزم الطالب الكفيل، وأخذ منه كفيلاً بنفسه على أنه إن لم يواف به فالمال الذي على المكفول به الأول عليه، جاز وليس هذا كالذي عليه مال ولم يكفل به أحد، كذا في رد المحتار نقلاً عن كافي الحاكم.
"If he guarantees a person on condition that if he does not produce him tomorrow, he is liable for what is against him." (If he guarantees a person on condition that if he does not produce him tomorrow) i.e., if he does not bring him tomorrow, (he is liable for what is against him). (If he does not produce) This is restricted to non-production to avoid what is in al-Bazzaziyyah: He guaranteed a person on condition that whenever he demands him, he will surrender him; if he does not surrender him, he is liable for what is against him. And the principal died, and he demanded surrender but he was unable; then he is not liable for the money because demanding surrender after death is invalid. So if the demand is invalid, the inability that causes liability for the money does not materialize, so it is not obligatory. Thus in al-Bahr. (For what is against him) This indicates that it is not necessary to specify the amount of the money. And he restricted it by saying 'for what is against him' because if he says: 'The money that you have against so-and-so, another man, and it is one thousand dirhams, is against me', it is valid according to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him. And Muhammad said: The suretyship for a person is valid, but the suretyship for the money is void because it is a gamble when the money is against someone else. It is only valid if the money is against him by istihsan. And if he guarantees the person of a man who has money against the claimant, then the claimant takes the surety, and he takes from him a surety for his person on condition that if he does not produce him, then the money that is against the first principal is against him, it is valid. And this is not like the one who has money against him and no one guarantees him. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar, quoting from Kafi al-Hakim.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage discusses a compound surety where the kafil guarantees a person and also conditionally guarantees the debt if he fails to produce the principal by a specified time. The matn states: 'If he guarantees a person on condition that if he does not produce him tomorrow, he is liable for what is against him.' The commentary clarifies that the condition is specific to non-production by the deadline. It then distinguishes a case from al-Bazzaziyyah where the condition was open-ended ('whenever demanded'); if the principal dies, the demand is impossible, so the surety is not liable for the debt. The phrase 'what is against him' indicates that the amount of debt need not be specified; it is known as the debt of the principal. The commentary then records an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: if someone says 'The money you have against so-and-so, and it is such-and-such, is against me,' Abu Yusuf validates it as a valid kafalah bil mal, but Muhammad says the kafalah bil nafs is valid but the kafalah bil mal is void because it involves risk (mukhatara) when the debt is owed by someone else. Muhammad's view is based on istihsan only when the debt is against the surety himself. The passage ends with a scenario where a chain of sureties is valid, distinguishing it from a case where no one guarantees the debt. This reflects the Hanafi school's nuanced approach to conditional obligations.

Key Terms
الكفالة بالنفس (al-kafalah bi al-nafs): Suretyship for the person; guarantee to produce the principal.
الكفالة بالمال (al-kafalah bi al-mal): Suretyship for debt; guarantee to pay a debt owed by another.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that favors equity over strict analogy.
المخاطرة (al-mukhatara): Risk or gambling; a prohibited element in contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for Imam Muhammad's ruling that the debt guarantee is void?
A) The debt amount is unknown.
B) The condition is open-ended.
C) It involves risk (mukhatara) when the debt is owed by another.
D) The surety did not consent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on conditional sureties combining person and debt, with Abu Yusuf allowing it and Muhammad restricting the debt part due to risk, except by istihsan.

Guarantee of Person with Conditional Liability for Debt
Explains conditional kafalah bil nafs with debt liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on conditional kafalah bil nafs with debt liability and distinguish the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad within the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A conditional guarantee of a person can validly include debt liability upon failure to produce.

This passage expands the ruling on kafalah bil nafs when a condition for debt liability is attached. You will learn when the guarantor becomes liable for the debt, how the amount is left unspecified, and the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Understanding this clarifies the boundaries of risk (gharar) in guarantees.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is a conditional guarantee of a person (kafalah bil nafs) with a stipulation that if the guarantor fails to produce the principal, he becomes liable for the principal's debt.
The ruling is that this conditional guarantee is valid (sahih) in the Hanafi school.
2
Note the condition for debt liability
The debt liability is triggered only upon failure to produce the guaranteed person by the specified time. If the principal dies, the demand becomes invalid, and the debt is not owed.
Condition: 'if he does not bring him tomorrow'.
3
Recognize that specifying the amount is not required
The text indicates that it is not a condition to specify the exact amount of the debt. The guarantor becomes liable for 'what is owed' without needing to quantify it in the guarantee.
The phrase 'لما عليه' implies the debt as it stands.
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf permits a conditional guarantee of a debt on another person, while Muhammad considers it invalid (batil) due to gharar (excessive risk). The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Yusuf's.
This ikhtilaf is recorded in Nawadir.
5
Apply the mufta bihi
In practice, the position of Abu Yusuf is followed: the conditional guarantee of debt is valid. The guarantor must pay the debt if he fails to produce the principal.
Tarjih by Al-Haskafi in al-Durr al-Mukhtar.

[۱]
(فهو ضامن لما عليه ) بل للوصي أن يطالب الكفيل، إن لم يكن الوصي فلوارثه المطالبة ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ من الوصي والوارث قائم مقام الميت، فلو سلَّمه إلى أحدٍ من الورثة أو الوصيين خاصة، فللباقي المطالبة بإحضاره. فإنَّ حق المطالبة ثابت لكلِّ واحدٍ من الورثة ، فإذا استوفى أحدهم حقه لا يسقط حق الباقين؛ لأن له استيفاء حقه فقط، وإنما قام مقام الباقين في إثباتِ حقهم، فلا يشكل عليه قولهم : إنَّ أحد الورثة ينتصب خصماً للميّت في ماله، وعليه قيل : يبرؤ الكفيل بموت الطالب، والمذهب هو الأوّل ، كذا صرَّحَ به في «الدر المختار»)، وغيره. [٢] قوله : لما عليه ؛ أشار إلى أنه لا يشترط تعيين قدر المال، وقيد بقوله : لما عليه ؛ لأنه لو قال : فالمالُ الذي لك على فلان رجل أخر ، وهو ألف درهم فهو عليّ، جازَ في قول أبي يوسف له. وقال محمد : الكفالة بالنفس جائزة ، والكفالة بالمال باطلة ؛ لأنه مخاطرة إذا كان المالُ على غيره، وإنّما يجوز إذا كان المالُ عليه استحسانا، ولو كفل بنفس رجلٍ للطالب عليه مال، فلزم الطالب الكفيل، وأخذ منه كفيلاً بنفسه على أنه إن لم يواف به فالمالُ الذي على المكفول به الأوّل عليه، جازَ وليس هذا كالذي عليه مال ولم يكفل به أحد، كذا في رد المحتار) نقلاً عن «كافي الحاكم».
If he guarantees the person on the condition that if he does not bring him tomorrow, then he is liable for what is owed to him. (If he guarantees the person on the condition that he does not bring him tomorrow) i.e., that he brings him tomorrow, [1] (then he is liable for what is owed to him) rather, the executor may demand from the guarantor; if there is no executor, then the heir may demand, because each of the executor and heir stands in the place of the deceased. If he hands him over to one of the heirs or one of the executors specifically, the remaining ones may demand his production. For the right of demand is established for each of the heirs. When one of them fulfills his right, the right of the others does not fall; because he only fulfills his own right, and he only stands in place of the others in establishing their right. So let it not be problematic what they say: that one of the heirs may be appointed as a litigant for the deceased regarding his property, and upon this it is said: the guarantor is discharged by the death of the claimant, but the relied-upon position is the first. Thus it is explicitly stated in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and others. [1] His saying: 'if he does not bring him'; he conditioned it on non-production to avoid what is in al-Bazzaziyya: he guaranteed the person on condition that whenever he demands, he will hand him over, and if he does not hand him over, then what is owed is upon him, and the demanded person dies, and he demands handing over but he is unable, then the money is not incumbent upon him, because the demand for handing over after death is not valid; since the demand is not valid, the inability that causes obligation of money does not materialize, so it is not obligatory. Thus in al-Bahr. [2] His saying: 'what is owed to him'; indicates that specifying the amount of money is not a condition, and he restricted it by saying 'what is owed to him' because if he said: the money that you have on so-and-so, another man, and it is one thousand dirhams, it is upon me, it is permissible according to Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: the guarantee of the person is valid, and the guarantee of the property is invalid because it is a risk when the property is on another person, and it is only permissible when the property is upon him by istihsan. If he guaranteed the person of a man who owes money to the claimant, and the claimant accepted the guarantor, and took from him a guarantor for his person on condition that if he does not bring him, then the money that is on the first guaranteed person is upon him, it is valid, and this is not like one who owes money and no one has guaranteed him. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar quoting from Kafi al-Hakim.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding the ruling on kafalah bil nafs (guarantee of a person) with a condition for debt liability. The author explains that if a person guarantees to produce another by a certain time, and fails, he becomes liable for the debt owed by that person. The executor or heir may demand the guarantor, and if the guarantor hands the person to only one heir, others still retain the right to demand. The commentary clarifies that the condition of non-production is key: if the guarantor is unable to produce due to the death of the principal, he is not liable because demand after death is invalid. The author also notes that specifying the exact amount of debt is not required, and presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf allows guaranteeing a debt on another person through a conditional guarantee, while Muhammad declares it invalid as a risk, though valid for the person. The relied-upon position is Abu Yusuf's. The passage emphasizes the practice of conditional guarantees in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
كفالة بالنفس (kafālah bi al-nafs): Guarantee to produce a person (the principal) to the claimant on demand.
كفالة بالمال (kafālah bi al-māl): Guarantee of a debt or property obligation, becoming liable if the principal fails to pay.
موافاة (muwāfāh): Producing the guaranteed person to the claimant at the agreed time.

Dispute with Shafi'i on Conditional Guarantee of Debt
Responding to Shafi'i's objection that conditional debt guarantee is invalid like sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi validation of conditional guarantees of debt and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A conditional guarantee is valid when the condition is customary because it resembles a vow, not a sale.

In this passage, the author defends the Hanafi position that a guarantee of debt can be validly suspended on a condition such as failure to produce a person. Imam al-Shafi'i argues this is akin to a suspended sale and thus invalid, but the Hanafi school draws a nuanced analogy to vows (nadhr) and considers custom. Understanding this difference illuminates Hanafi methodology in balancing textual analogy with practical need.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that a conditional guarantee of debt (kafalah bi al-mal mu'allaq) is valid when the condition is customary, e.g., failure to produce a person by a set time. The guarantor must pay the debt if the condition occurs and remains liable for the person until payment.
2
Identify the opponent and their objection
Imam al-Shafi'i (and Malik) argue that this transaction makes money obligatory by a condition (ijab al-mal bi al-shart), which is purely contingent and uncertain, thus invalid like a suspended sale (bay' mu'allaq).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent's proof is qiyas: the conditional guarantee is a suspended obligation, and all such obligations are invalid by analogy to sales suspended on uncertain conditions.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis do not accept the pure analogy to sale. They identify two resemblances (shabah): to a sale in the end (the guarantor's recourse) and to a vow (nadhr) in the beginning (a commitment without immediate exchange). By acting on both, they rule: if the condition is unsuitable (e.g., random natural events), the sale analogy dominates and it is invalid; if suitable and customary (e.g., non-production), the vow analogy dominates and it is valid.
This is a case of istihsan based on custom and need.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis enforce such guarantees, making the guarantor liable; Shafi'is consider them void, so no liability arises. This affects real-world practice in Hanafi versus Shafi'i jurisdictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة / تعريفها وشروطها وأحكامها ولم يسلمه غداً لزمه ما عليه، ولم يبرأ من كفالته بالنفس ، وإن مات المكفولُ ضمِنَ المال [٤] ولم يسلمه غداً لزمَهُ ما عليه ) : خلافاً للشَّافِعِيُّ ، له أَنَّه إيجاب المالِ بالشرط ، فلا يجوز كالبيع. قلنا : إنَّه يشبه البيع ويشبه النَّذر، فإن علقَ بشرط غير ملائم لا يصح، وبملائم يصح عملاً بالشبهين ، (ولم يبرأ من كفالته بالنفس) لعدم سبب البراءة، بل إنما يبرؤ إذا أدَّى المالَ ؛ لأنَّه لم يبق للطالب على المكفول عنه شيء ، فلا فائدة في الكفالة بالنفس . وإن مات المكفول عنه ضَمِنَ المال) [١]قوله : ولم يسلّمه غداً ؛ مع قدرته عليه ، فلو عجز بحبس أو مرض لم يلزمه المال إلا إذا عجز بموت المطلوب أو جنونه ، كذا في «الدر المختار» [٢] قوله : لـه أنـه...الخ ؛ حاصله أنّ الكفالة بالمال إيجاب المال بالشرط ، وهو أمرٌ متردّد قد يكون وقد لا يكون، وهو عدم الموافاة إلى وقت كذا، فينبغي أن لا يجوز كالبيع، وبه قال مالك لله. [٣] قوله : قلنا إنه...الخ ؛ حاصله : إنَّ الكفالة بالمال يشبه البيع انتهاء، باعتبار رجوع الكفيل على الأصيل بما أدّى عنه إذا كان بأمره ، فصار مبادلة المال بالمال، ويشبه النذر ابتداء باعتبار الالتزام، إذ لا يقابله شيء. فقلنا : إن كان تعليق الكفالة بشرط غير ملائم ؛ كهبوب الريح ونزول المطر ونحوها ، لا تصح كالبيع، وإن كان بشرط ملائم متعارف ؛ مثل: عدم الموافاة في وقتِ كذا، تصح كالنذر، والتعليق بعدم الموافاة متعارف، فإنَّ النّاس تعارفوا تعليق الكفالة بالمال ؛ لعدم الموافاة بالنفس. ورغبتهم في ذلك أكثر من رغبتهم في مجرَّدِ الكفالة بالنفس، ولا نسلم أنَّ هذا تعليق سبب وجـ سبب وجوب المال بأمر متردد ، وقد يكون وقد لا يكون، حتى لا يجوز كالبيع، بل إنما هو تعليق وجوب المطالبة. [٤]قوله : المكفول عنه ؛ اللام للعهد والمعهود هو المكفولُ بنفسه الذي شرط كفيله أنه إن لم يواف به غداً فعليه ما عليه من المال، كذا في «كمال الدراية».
Book of Kafalah / Its definition, conditions, and rulings: And if he does not hand him over tomorrow, what is owed becomes incumbent upon him, and he is not discharged from his guarantee of the person, and if the guaranteed person dies, he is liable for the money. [4] (And if he does not hand him over tomorrow, what is owed becomes incumbent upon him) contrary to al-Shafi'i. He argues that it is making money obligatory by a condition, so it is not permissible like a sale. We say: It resembles a sale and resembles a vow. If it is suspended on an unsuitable condition, it is not valid; if on a suitable condition, it is valid by acting on both resemblances. (And he is not discharged from his guarantee of the person) because there is no cause for discharge; rather, he is only discharged if he pays the money, because nothing remains for the claimant against the guaranteed person, so there is no benefit in the guarantee of the person. And if the guaranteed person dies, he is liable for the money. [1] His saying: 'and if he does not hand him over tomorrow' — while he is able to do so. If he is unable due to imprisonment or illness, the money is not incumbent upon him, unless the inability is due to the death of the demanded person or his insanity. Thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtar. [2] His saying: 'he argues that...' — its summary is that the guarantee of the property is making the money obligatory by a condition, which is a matter that may or may not happen, namely non-production by a certain time, so it should not be permissible like a sale, and this is also the view of Malik. [3] His saying: 'we say that...' — its summary: The guarantee of the property resembles a sale in its end, considering the guarantor's recourse against the principal for what he paid if it was by his order, so it becomes an exchange of money for money, and it resembles a vow in its beginning, considering the commitment, since nothing is exchanged for it. So we said: If the suspension of the guarantee is on an unsuitable condition, like the blowing of wind, falling of rain, and the like, it is not valid like a sale. If it is on a suitable customary condition, such as non-production at a certain time, it is valid like a vow. And suspension on non-production is customary, for people customarily suspend the guarantee of the property on non-production of the person. Their desire for this is greater than their desire for mere guarantee of the person. And we do not concede that this is a suspension of the cause of obligation of money on a contingent matter that may or may not happen, such that it is not permissible like a sale; rather, it is a suspension of the obligation of demand. [4] His saying: 'the guaranteed person' — the lam is for the covenant, and the covenanted is the person guaranteed himself whose guarantor stipulated that if he does not bring him tomorrow, then what is owed of money is upon him. Thus in Kamal al-Diraya.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i on the validity of a conditional guarantee of debt. The Hanafi position is that if a person guarantees another's person with the condition that if he fails to produce him by a certain time he will pay the debt, then upon failure, he must pay the debt and remains liable for the person until payment. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that this is making a debt obligation conditional on a contingent event, which is like a suspended sale and thus invalid. The Hanafis respond by analyzing the transaction from two angles: it resembles a sale at the end (because the guarantor can seek reimbursement from the principal), but it resembles a vow (nadhr) at the beginning (a commitment without immediate exchange). Applying the principle that a vow suspended on a suitable condition is valid, they hold the guarantee valid when the condition is customary (like non-production) and invalid when the condition is unsuitable (like random natural events). The passage also clarifies that liability for the money only arises if the guarantor is able to produce; if unable due to imprisonment or illness, no liability. Death or insanity of the principal makes the guarantor liable. The relied-upon position is Hanafi, as this istihsan (juristic preference) is based on custom and need.

Key Terms
إيجاب المال بالشرط (ījāb al-māl bi al-shart): Making a monetary obligation contingent upon a condition, as in conditional guarantees.
شرط ملائم (shart mulā'im): A condition that is suitable and customary, such as non-production of a person by a certain time.
نذر (nadhr): A vow; a commitment to do an act of worship, which can be conditional. Used as an analogy for conditional guarantees.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in the dispute, why is a conditional guarantee of debt valid when the condition is customary?
A) Because it is purely a sale transaction.
B) Because it resembles a vow in its initiation and is thus valid for suitable conditions.
C) Because the condition is always considered suitable.
D) Because Shafi'i changed his opinion.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates conditional guarantees of debt when the condition is customary, based on an analogy to vows and istihsan.

Conditional Kafalah: Validity of Combining Guarantee of Person and Property
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the Hanafi validation of conditional kafalah in response to the Shafi'i objection, using the principle of shabah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on conditional kafalah and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using qiyas al-shabah.

Core Idea
A conditional guarantee of property is valid if the condition is customary, resembling a vow not a sale.

In Hanafi fiqh, a guarantee (kafalah) can be either for the person or for property. What happens when a guarantor of the person promises to pay the debt if he fails to produce the debtor? The Shafi'i school invalidates this as resembling a contingent sale, but the Hanafis uphold it using the principle of shabah (resemblance). This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and the scenario
Identify the specific ruling: a conditional kafalah (guarantee of property tied to failure to produce the debtor) is valid in the Hanafi school. Note the basic fact scenario: a claimant demands a debt, a third party guarantees the debtor's person, and promises to pay if the debtor is not produced.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection and its basis
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i (and Malik) argue this is invalid because it creates an obligation of property upon a contingent condition, resembling a sale with uncertain consideration (gharar).
The condition is 'failure to produce by a certain time,' which is uncertain.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response using shabah
The Hanafis argue that the conditional kafalah resembles both a sale (because eventually there is an exchange if the guarantor seeks recourse) and a vow (because at inception it is a gratuitous commitment). The resemblance to vow is stronger when the condition is customary.
Qiyas al-shabah: weigh which resemblance predominates.
4
Apply the distinction between customary and unsuitable conditions
The condition is valid only if it is mulaim (customary and relevant), like failure to produce the debtor. Unsuitable conditions (e.g., rain) make the guarantee invalid, resembling a sale.
Customary conditions are accepted due to custom (urf) and public need.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding unspecified debt
Muhammad al-Shaybani has two opinions on whether the debt must be specified. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold it valid even without specification. The relied-upon position is validity.
The mufta bihi is validity, as stated by later Hanafi authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يسلمه غداً لزمه ما عليه، ولم يبرأ من كفالته بالنفس، وإن مات المكفول عنه ضمِنَ المال. خلافاً للشَّافِعِيُّ، له أَنَّه إيجاب المالِ بالشرط، فلا يجوز كالبيع. قلنا: إنَّه يشبه البيع ويشبه النَّذر، فإن علقَ بشرط غير ملائم لا يصح، وبملائم يصح عملاً بالشبهين. (ولم يبرأ من كفالته بالنفس) لعدم سبب البراءة، بل إنما يبرؤ إذا أدَّى المالَ؛ لأنَّه لم يبق للطالب على المكفول عنه شيء، فلا فائدة في الكفالة بالنفس. وإن مات المكفول عنه ضَمِنَ المال. [١]قوله: ولم يسلّمه غداً؛ مع قدرته عليه، فلو عجز بحبس أو مرض لم يلزمه المال إلا إذا عجز بموت المطلوب أو جنونه، كذا في «الدر المختار». [٢] قوله: له أنـه...الخ؛ حاصله أنّ الكفالة بالمال إيجاب المال بالشرط، وهو أمرٌ متردّد قد يكون وقد لا يكون، وهو عدم الموافاة إلى وقت كذا، فينبغي أن لا يجوز كالبيع، وبه قال مالك. [٣] قوله: قلنا إنه...الخ؛ حاصله: إنَّ الكفالة بالمال يشبه البيع انتهاء، باعتبار رجوع الكفيل على الأصيل بما أدّى عنه إذا كان بأمره، فصار مبادلة المال بالمال، ويشبه النذر ابتداء باعتبار الالتزام، إذ لا يقابله شيء. فقلنا: إن كان تعليق الكفالة بشرط غير ملائم؛ كهبوب الريح ونزول المطر ونحوها، لا تصح كالبيع، وإن كان بشرط ملائم متعارف؛ مثل: عدم الموافاة في وقتِ كذا، تصح كالنذر، والتعليق بعدم الموافاة متعارف، فإنَّ النّاس تعارفوا تعليق الكفالة بالمال؛ لعدم الموافاة بالنفس. ورغبتهم في ذلك أكثر من رغبتهم في مجرَّدِ الكفالة بالنفس، ولا نسلم أنَّ هذا تعليق سبب وجوب المال بأمر متردد، وقد يكون وقد لا يكون، حتى لا يجوز كالبيع، بل إنما هو تعليق وجوب المطالبة. [٤]قوله: المكفول عنه؛ اللام للعهد والمعهود هو المكفولُ بنفسه الذي شرط كفيله أنه إن لم يواف به غداً فعليه ما عليه من المال، كذا في «كمال الدراية». 

ومن ادَّعى على رجل مالاً بيَّنَه أو لا، فكفل بنفسهِ آخر على أنه إن لم يواف به غداً، فعليه المال صحت، ويجب عند الشرط لوجودِ الشَّرط، وهو عدم الموافاة. (ومن ادعى على رجل مالاً بينه أو لا، فكفل بنفسِهِ آخرُ على أنَّه إن لم يواف به غداً، فعليه المال صحت، ويجب عند الشرط): صورة المسألة ادعى رجل على آخر مئة دينار، فكفل بنفسه رجلٌ على أنَّه إن لم يواف به غداً، فعليه المئة. فقولُه: مالاً: أي مالاً مقدراً. وقوله: بَيْنَهُ أوَّلا: أي بَيْنَ صفتَهُ على وجه تصح الدعوى، أو لم يُبين. وفي المسألة خلاف محمد. 

[١]قوله: لوجود الشرط؛ وهو عدم الموافاة وموتُ المطلوب، وإن أبطل الكفالة، فإنّما هو في حق تسليمه إلى الطالب، لا في حق المال. ذكره شيخنا العلامه الطحطاوي. [٢]قوله: مئة دينار؛ ولا فرق بين أن يبين المئة أو لم يبينها، بأن ادعى رجل على رجل فقال: لي عليك حق، ولم يدَّع عليه مالاً مقدراً، فقال له رجل آخر: دعه فأنا كفيل بنفسه، فإن لم أوفك به غداً فعلي مئة دينار، فادعى المدعي وأثبتها، صرح به العلامة العيني، والزيلعي، وغيرهما. [٣]قوله: وفي المسألة خلاف محمد ... الخ؛ تقريره أنَّ لمحمد في هذه المسألة وجهين: أحدهما: ما قاله العلامة أبو منصور الماتريدي له أنَّ الكفيل علق مالاً مطلقاً بأمر متردي قد يكون وقد لا يكون، حيث لم يقل: علي المئة التي على المدعى عليه، ولم ينسبه إلى ما على المكفول عنه، فكانت هذه رشوة التزمها الكفيل له عند عدم الموافاة به، فهذا يوجب أن لا يصح، وإن بينها المدعي؛ لأنَّ عدم النسبة إليه هو الذي أوجب البطلان.
And if he does not hand him over tomorrow, he is liable for what is upon him, and he is not released from his guarantee of the person, even if the guaranteed person dies. He becomes liable for the property. Contrary to al-Shafi'i—he argued that it is an obligation of property by a condition, so it is not permissible like a sale. We said: It resembles a sale and resembles a vow. If it is attached to an unsuitable condition, it is not valid; but with a suitable condition, it is valid, acting upon both resemblances. (And he is not released from his guarantee of the person) because there is no cause for release; rather, he is released only when he pays the property, because nothing remains for the claimant against the guaranteed person, so there is no benefit in the guarantee of the person. And if the guaranteed person dies, he is liable for the property. [1] His saying: And if he does not hand him over tomorrow—while being able to; if he is incapacitated by imprisonment or illness, the property does not become obligatory unless he is incapacitated by the death of the demanded or his insanity, as in al-Durr al-Mukhtar. [2] His saying: He argued that... summary: The guarantee of property is an obligation of property by a condition, which is a contingent matter that may or may not occur—namely, failure to produce by a certain time—so it should not be permissible like a sale; and Malik also held this. [3] His saying: We said... summary: The guarantee of property resembles a sale in its end result, considering the guarantor's recourse against the principal for what he paid if it was by his order, thus becoming an exchange of property for property; and it resembles a vow at its inception, considering the commitment without a countervalue. So we said: If the attachment of the guarantee is to an unsuitable condition, like the blowing of wind or falling of rain and the like, it is not valid like a sale; but if it is to a suitable customary condition, such as failure to produce by a certain time, it is valid like a vow. And the attachment to failure to produce is customary, for people have customarily attached the guarantee of property to the failure to produce the person, and their desire for that is greater than their desire for mere guarantee of the person. We do not concede that this is attaching the cause of the obligation of property to a contingent matter that may or may not occur, so as to be impermissible like a sale; rather, it is attaching the obligation of the claim. [4] His saying: The one guaranteed; the article is for the known entity, and the known entity is the person guaranteed who is the one whose guarantor stipulated that if he does not produce him tomorrow, he is liable for what he owes of property, as in Kamal al-Diraya.

And whoever claims property from a man, whether he specifies it or not, and another person guarantees his person on the condition that if he does not produce him tomorrow, he is liable for the property—it is valid, and payment becomes obligatory at the condition due to the existence of the condition, which is failure to produce. (And whoever claims property from a man, whether he specifies it or not, and another person guarantees his person on the condition that if he does not produce him tomorrow, he is liable for the property—it is valid, and payment becomes obligatory at the condition): The fact scenario: A man claims one hundred dinars from another, and a man guarantees his person on the condition that if he does not produce him tomorrow, he is liable for the hundred. His saying: property—means quantified property. His saying: whether he specifies it or not—means whether he describes it in a manner that makes the claim valid, or does not describe it. And in this issue there is a difference of opinion from Muhammad.

[1] His saying: due to the existence of the condition—which is failure to produce and the death of the demanded. And if it invalidates the guarantee, that is only regarding handing him over to the claimant, not regarding the property. Our teacher al-Tahawi mentioned it. [2] His saying: one hundred dinars—and there is no difference between specifying the hundred or not specifying it, such as when a man claims from another and says: I have a right against you, without claiming quantified property, and another man says: Let him go, I am his guarantor for his person; if I do not produce him to you tomorrow, I owe one hundred dinars. Then the claimant establishes it—al-Ayni, al-Zayla'i, and others stated this explicitly. [3] His saying: And in this issue there is a difference of opinion from Muhammad... its explanation: Muhammad has two positions in this issue: One is what the scholar Abu Mansur al-Maturidi reported from him: that the guarantor attached an absolute property to a contingent matter that may or may not occur, since he did not say: I owe the hundred that is upon the defendant, and he did not attribute it to what is upon the guaranteed person. So this is a bribe that the guarantor obligated for himself upon failure to produce him, and this necessitates that it is not valid, even if the claimant specified it, because the lack of attribution to him is what necessitated invalidity.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a sharh on a matn from al-Hidayah regarding conditional kafalah. The base ruling states: If a guarantor (kafil bil-nafs) stipulates that if he does not produce the debtor (mukful) by a certain time, he will be liable for the debt, then that payment becomes obligatory if the condition occurs. However, the guarantor is not released from his guarantee of the person merely by that condition; he is only released when he actually pays the debt. This is because the claimant's right against the debtor remains until payment. The passage then presents the inter-madhhab dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Malik argue that such a conditional guarantee is invalid, likening it to a sale with a contingent condition (bay' bi al-shart), which is impermissible due to uncertainty (gharar). The Hanafi response uses the principle of shabah (resemblance): the conditional kafalah resembles both a sale (bay') in its end—since the guarantor can recover from the debtor—and a vow (nadhr) in its initiation—since it is a gratuitous commitment. The Hanafis rule: if the condition is ghayr mulaim (unsuitable, e.g., linked to wind or rain), the guarantee is invalid like a sale; if it is mulaim (customary, e.g., failure to produce the debtor), it is valid like a vow. The condition of failure to produce is customary because people commonly require such guarantees. The passage further addresses the case where the debt amount is unspecified: a claimant says 'I have a right against you' without quantifying, and a guarantor says 'If I do not produce him, I owe 100 dinars.' According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, this is valid. Muhammad al-Shaybani has two opinions: one that it is invalid because the guarantor did not specify the debt, making it akin to a bribe; the other that it is valid. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that such a conditional kafalah is valid when the condition is customary, as upheld by later scholars like al-Tahawi, al-Ayni, and al-Zayla'i.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contract of guarantee where a person (kafil) becomes responsible for another's obligation, either for the person (kafalah bil-nafs) or for property (kafalah bil-mal).
كفالة بالنفس (kafalah bil-nafs): A guarantee to produce the debtor (mukful) on demand, not to pay the debt.
كفالة بالمال (kafalah bil-mal): A guarantee to pay the debt if the principal debtor fails to do so.
شرط ملائم (shart mulaim): A condition that is suitable, customary, and relevant to the transaction, such as failure to produce the debtor.
شبه (shabah): Resemblance; a type of qiyas where a thing has two aspects, one resembling a valid analogy and another resembling an invalid analogy; the ruling depends on which aspect prevails.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what determines whether a conditional kafalah (guarantee of property conditioned on failure to produce the debtor) is valid?
A) The condition must be suitable and customary (mulaim).
B) The condition must be explicitly mentioned in the contract.
C) The debt must be specified in amount.
D) The guarantor must be the debtor himself.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a conditional guarantee of property is valid if the condition is customary, based on the principle of shabah (resemblance to a vow rather than a sale).

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Conditional Kafalah for Money When Debt Unspecified
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of conditional suretyship for money without specifying debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi positions regarding the validity of conditional suretyship for property when the debt is unspecified, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A conditional promise to pay money must be linked to the debtor's debt; otherwise it may be invalid or even considered a bribe.

This passage delves into a subtle issue in Hanafi kafalah: what happens when a surety promises to pay a debt if he fails to bring the debtor, but neither he nor the claimant specifies the amount of the debt at the time of the contract? The Sahibayn uphold validity based on custom and the principle of validating transactions, while Imam Muhammad's reported views offer stricter conditions. Understanding this dispute sharpens your appreciation for how Hanafi jurists balance textual principles with practical custom.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the preferred Hanafi position
Identify the core scenario: a claimant demands an unspecified debt, a surety promises to pay if he fails to produce the debtor. The Sahibayn hold this valid.
The preferred position is that the suretyship is valid and the obligation arises upon failure to bring.
2
Identify Imam Muhammad's opposing views
There are two interpretations (wajhayn) of Imam Muhammad's stance: (1) invalid always unless explicit attribution to the debtor's debt (Maturidi); (2) invalid only if the initial claim was unspecified, but valid if specified (Karkhi).
The Maturidi view treats the promise as a bribe; the Karkhi view focuses on the invalidity of the claim.
3
Follow the proof for each opposing view
For the Maturidi view: the surety's promise is absolute, not linked to the debtor's obligation, thus a prohibited bribe. For the Karkhi view: the unspecified claim is corrupt, so personal suretyship fails, and property suretyship fails with it.
Both views require explicit attribution or specification, but differ on the exact requirement.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response (Sahibayn)
The Sahibayn argue that custom supports vague claims outside court; later specification retroactively links the promise to the original debt. The definite article 'al-' in 'al-mi'ah' indicates the well-known debt even when unspecified.
The response emphasizes that the law validates transactions when possible, and the condition does not turn the promise into a bribe if it can be linked to the debtor's debt.
5
Determine the tarjih (mufta bihi)
The position of the Sahibayn is the mufta bihi because it is based on custom and the principle of facilitating transactions. The later jurists rely on this view.
The practical consequence: a judge following the mufta bihi will uphold the suretyship even without initial specification.
