(ومن ادعى على رجل مالاً بينه أو لا، فكفل بنفسِهِ آخرُ على أنَّه إن لم يواف به غداً، فعليه المال صحت ، ويجب عند الشرط) : صورة المسألة ادعى رجل على آخر مئة دينار، فكفل بنفسه رجلٌ على أنَّه إن لم يواف به غداً، فعليه المئة. فقولُه : مالاً : أي مالاً مقدراً. وقوله : بَيْنَهُ أوَّلاً: أي بَيْنَ صفتَهُ على وجه تصح الدعوى، أو لم يُبين.
وفي المسألة خلاف محمد ه
[1]قوله : لوجود الشرط ؛ وهو عدم الموافاة وموتُ المطلوب، وإن أبطل الكفالة، فإنّما هو في حق تسليمه إلى الطالب، لا في حق المال. ذكره شيخنا العلامه (1) الطحطاوي" [٢]قوله : مئة دينار ؛ ولا فرق بين أن يبين المئة أو لم يبينها، بأن ادعى رجل على رجل فقال : لي عليك حق ، ولم يدَّع عليه مالاً مقدراً، فقال له رجل آخر : دعه فأنا كفيل بنفسه، فإن لم أوفك به غداً فعلي مئة دينار، فادعى المدعي وأثبتها ، صرح به العلامة العيني) ، والزيلعي ، وغيرهما. [٣]قوله : وفي المسألة خلاف محمد ... الخ ؛ تقريره أنَّ لمحمد له في هذه المسألة وجهين :
أحدهما : ما قاله العلامة أبو منصور الماتريدي له وهو أنَّ الكفيل علق مالاً مطلقاً بأمر متردي قد يكون وقد لا يكون، حيث لم يقل : علي المئة التي على المدعى عليه، ولم ينسبه إلى ما على المكفول عنه ، فكانت هذه رشوة التزمها الكفيل له عند عدم الموافاة به ، فهذا يوجب أن لا يصح، وإن بينها المدعي ؛ لأنَّ عدم النسبة إليه هو الذي أوجب البطلان.
فقيل : عدم الجواز عنده مبني على أنه قال : فعليه المئة، ولم يقل المئة التي على المدعى عليه، فعلى هذا إن بين المدعي المئة لا يكونُ كفالة عنده صحيحاً أيضاً، كما إذا لم يُبين إلا أن يقول : فعليه المئة التي يدعيها. وقيل : إنَّه مبني على أنَّه لما لم يُبين لم يصح الدعوى، فلم يستوجب إحضاره إلى مجلس القاضي، فلم يصح الكفالة بالنفس، ولا يجوز الكفالة بالمال، فعلى هذا إن بين يكون الكفالة صحيحة .
ولهما : أنه قال : فعليه المئة ، أو عليه المال، فيراد به المعهود، فإن بَيَّنَ المدعي فظاهر، وإن لم يبين فبعد ذلك، إذا بين التحق البيان بأصل الدعوى ، فتبين صحة الكفالة بالنَّفس، فيترتب عليها الكفالة بالمال.
والثاني : : ما قاله الشيخ أبو الحسن الكرخي له : وهو أنَّ المدعي لما لم يبين مالاً مقدراً لم يستوجب إحضاره إلى مجلس القاضي ؛ لفساد الدعوى، فلا تصح الكفالة بالنفس ؛ لعدم صحة الدعوى، ولا تصح الكفالة بالمال أيضاً ؛ لأنها مبنية على الكفالة بالنفس، فإذا بطل الأصلُ بطل الفرع ، وهذا الوجه يوجب أن تصح الكفالة إذا بين المال عند الدعوى. [١] قوله : ولهما ... الخ ؛ تحريره : إنّ هذه كفالة أمكن تصحيحها فتصح ، أما إذا بَيْنَ المال عند الدعوى فظاهر أنه ذكر معرَّفاً باللام ، وقال : فعليه المئة، فينصرف إلى ما على المدعى عليه بجعل اللام للعهد، فكانت النسبة موجودة، فخرج المالُ عن كونه رشوة، فيلزم وتصح الكفالة. وأما إذا لم يُبين ؛ فلأنَّ العادة جرت بالإيهام في الدعاوي في غير مجلس القضاء ، فيحملونها إجمالاً، ولا يبينونها إلا عند القاضي دفعاً لحيل الخصوم، وصونا لكلامهم إلى وقت الحاجة، فصحت الدعوى، والملازمة على احتمال البيان من جهته ، فإذا بَيْنَ انصرف بيانه إلى ابتداء الدعوى، فظهر به أنَّ الكفالة بالنفس قد صحت، فتصح الكفالة بالمال أيضاً ؛ لأنها مبنية عليها ، ولأنه لو جعل التزاماً عليه تصح وإلا فلا ، فيحمل عليه تصحيحاً لتصرفه. [٢] قوله : المئة ؛ وذكر في «الجامع الصغير) (۱) وغيره منكراً فلا تستقيم هذه النكتة على هذا الوجه.
And whoever claims a debt from a man, whether he specifies it or not, then another person becomes a surety for his person on the condition that if he does not bring him tomorrow, then he is liable for the debt, it is valid, and it becomes obligatory upon the condition, because of the existence of the condition, which is failure to bring him. The image of the issue: A man claimed one hundred dinars from another, then a man became a surety for his person on the condition that if he does not bring him tomorrow, then he owes the hundred. His saying: 'malan' means quantified property. His saying: 'bayyanahu aw la' means whether he described it in a way that makes the claim valid, or not. In this issue there is a disagreement from Muhammad. [1] His saying: because of the existence of the condition, which is failure to bring him and the death of the claimed person, even if it voids the suretyship, that is only regarding handing him over to the claimant, not regarding the property. Our teacher al-Tahtawi mentioned it. [2] His saying: one hundred dinars; and there is no difference between specifying the hundred or not, such as if a man claimed from another and said: I have a right against you, and he did not claim a quantified amount, then another man said to him: Leave him, I am a surety for his person, if I do not bring him to you tomorrow then I owe one hundred dinars, then the claimant claimed and established it, as stated by al-'Ayni, al-Zayla'i, and others. [3] His saying: And in the issue there is disagreement from Muhammad... its summary: That Muhammad has two faces in this issue: One of them: What Abu Mansur al-Maturidi said regarding him, that the surety attached an absolute property to a contingent matter that may or may not happen, since he did not say: I owe the hundred that is against the claimed person, and he did not attribute it to what is against the principal debtor, so this was a bribe that the surety undertook for him upon failure to bring him, and this necessitates that it is not valid, even if the claimant specifies it, because the lack of attribution to him is what caused the invalidity. So it is said: The non-permissibility according to him is based on that he said: 'upon him the hundred', and he did not say 'the hundred that is against the claimed person'. According to this, if the claimant specifies the hundred, it is not valid according to him either, just as if he did not specify, unless he says: 'upon him the hundred that he claims'. And it is said: It is based on that since he did not specify, the claim is not valid, so he does not become entitled to bringing him to the court, so the suretyship for the person is not valid, and the suretyship for property is not allowed. According to this, if he specifies, the suretyship is valid. And for them two: He said: 'upon him the hundred', or 'upon him the property', and by that is meant the well-known (al-ma'hood). If the claimant specified, it is obvious; if he did not specify, then afterwards when he specifies, the specification attaches to the original claim, so it becomes clear that the suretyship for the person is valid, and the suretyship for property follows from it. The second: What Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi said regarding him: That since the claimant did not specify a quantified amount, he did not become entitled to bringing him to the court due to the corruption of the claim, so the suretyship for the person is not valid due to the invalidity of the claim, and the suretyship for property is also not valid because it is based on the suretyship for the person; if the origin is void, the branch is void. This face necessitates that the suretyship is valid if he specifies the property at the time of the claim. [1] His saying: And for them two... its refinement: This is a suretyship that can be validated, so it is valid. As for when he specifies the property at the time of the claim, it is obvious that he mentioned it with the definite article (al-) and said 'upon him the hundred', so it is diverted to what is against the claimed person by making the 'al-' for covenant (al-'ahd), so the attribution exists, and the property exits from being a bribe, so it becomes binding and the suretyship is valid. As for when he does not specify, it is because custom has established vagueness in claims outside the court, so they are carried summarily, and they only specify before the judge to ward off tricks of opponents and to preserve their speech until the time of need. So the claim is valid, and the obligation (for the surety) is conditional upon the possibility of specification from his side; so when he specifies, his specification goes back to the beginning of the claim, thereby it becomes clear that the suretyship for the person was valid, so the suretyship for property is also valid because it is based on it, and because if it is made a commitment upon him it is valid, otherwise not, so it is carried upon it to validate his transaction. [2] His saying: the hundred; and it is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir and others in the indefinite form (nakira), so this subtle point is not correct according to this face.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific issue in Hanafi kafalah: conditional suretyship for money where the surety says, 'If I do not bring the debtor tomorrow, I owe the (unspecified) debt.' The author begins by stating the ruling: it is valid and the obligation arises when the condition (failure to bring) occurs. Then he gives the scenario: claimant demands 100 dinars from the debtor; a third party becomes a surety for the debtor's person, with the condition that if he fails to produce him, he will pay the 100. The text clarifies that 'malan' means a quantified amount, and 'bayyanahu aw la' means the claimant either specified the amount or did not. The core of the passage is the intra-Hanafi disagreement (khilaf) attributed to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. Two interpretations (wajhayn) of Muhammad's position are reported: 

1. According to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi: Muhammad holds the suretyship invalid because the surety's commitment is not linked to the debt actually owed by the debtor; it is an absolute promise to pay an unspecified amount upon a contingency, which resembles a prohibited bribe (rishwa). This invalidity persists even if the claimant later specifies the debt, because the problem is the lack of attribution to the debtor's obligation. The only exception is if the surety explicitly says 'the hundred that he claims' – then it is valid.

2. According to Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi: Muhammad's objection is based on the initial claim being invalid due to lack of specification. Since the claim is defective, the claimant has no right to demand the debtor's presence, so the suretyship for the person is invalid. Consequently, the suretyship for property—being dependent on the personal suretyship—is also invalid (branch follows root). Under this view, if the claimant specifies the debt at the outset, both the claim and the suretyship become valid.

The text then presents the position of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani – though 'Muhammad' as a companion here differs from the earlier 'Muhammad' in the disagreement; careful: 'lahuma' refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, i.e., the Sahibayn). They hold that the suretyship is valid in both cases. If the claimant specifies the debt at the time of the claim, the surety's phrase 'upon him the hundred' is understood by the definite article 'al-' as referring to the well-known debt (al-ma'hood), so it is attributed to the debtor. If the claimant did not specify initially, the custom is that people state claims vaguely outside court and only specify before the judge. So the initial vague claim is considered valid, and when the claimant later specifies, that specification is retroactively attached to the original claim, proving that the personal suretyship was valid from the start, and thus the property suretyship is also valid as it is built upon it.

The commentary then expands on the Sahibayn's reasoning, emphasizing that the law aims to validate transactions when possible, and the condition is interpreted in a way that avoids treating the commitment as a bribe. The last note mentions that in al-Jami' al-Saghir the word 'the hundred' is in the indefinite form (nakira), which undermines the subtle point that the definite article indicates a known debt. This indicates a textual variant.

Overall, this passage demonstrates the sophisticated legal reasoning within the Hanafi school, where multiple interpretations of an imam's position are explored, and the final relied-upon position (mufta bihi) tends to favor the Sahibayn's validation of the conditional suretyship, as it aligns with the principle of facilitating transactions and the custom of vague preliminary claims.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A contract of guarantee or suretyship, where one person (kafil) becomes responsible for bringing another (makful) or for paying a debt.
الشَّرط (al-shart): The condition stated by the surety: failure to produce the debtor by tomorrow triggers the obligation to pay the money.
الرشوة (al-rishwa): A bribe; an unlawful payment to influence a decision. In this context, the surety's conditional promise is compared to a bribe because it is not linked to the debtor's obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), why is the conditional suretyship valid even if the claimant did not specify the debt at the time of the claim?
A) Because the surety explicitly mentioned the exact amount and attributed it to the debtor.
B) Because custom dictates that claims outside court are vague, and later specification retroactively ties the promise to the original debt.
C) Because the surety's promise is an independent commitment not linked to the debtor's debt.
D) Because the condition is void and the suretyship for property is separate from personal suretyship.

Key Takeaway
A conditional suretyship for property is valid even if the debt is unspecified, as long as custom or later specification links the promise to the debtor's obligation.

Kafalah with Unspecified Debt Amount: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Dispute on validity of kafalah when debt amount is unspecified

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah with unspecified debt and identify the basis for each position.

Core Idea
Kafalah with unspecified amount is disputed: Abu Hanifa deems it invalid; the Sahibayn allow it if specification later occurs.

This passage addresses a critical dispute within the Hanafi school: is a suretyship contract valid if the amount of the debt is not specified at the time of the contract? Two interpretations of Imam Abu Hanifa's position are presented, along with the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this dispute reveals the importance of legal reasoning (istidlal) and the role of custom in shaping rulings.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Identify that Imam Abu Hanifa holds the kafalah is not valid when the amount is unspecified. Note that there are two possible bases for this ruling.
2
Examine the two interpretations of his basis
First basis: the surety's statement 'upon him is the hundred' is ambiguous and does not refer to the specific debt claimed, so it is not binding even if later specified. Second basis: the lawsuit itself is invalid because the amount is unspecified, so the defendant cannot be compelled to appear, making kafalah bi al-nafs invalid, and kafalah bi al-mal follows.
The second basis allows validation if specification occurs at the time of the lawsuit.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
The Sahibayn argue that the phrase 'upon him is the hundred' is understood contextually as the well-known debt (due to the definite article 'al-'), so the kafalah is valid even if unspecified initially. Subsequent specification retroactively validates the contract.
Custom (urf) allows vague claims outside court, and specification later relates back to the original claim.
4
Analyze the tarjih and mufta bihi
The commentary favors the Sahibayn's view, noting that the kafalah can be validated and custom supports it. The mufta bihi is that if specification occurs later, the kafalah is valid.
The practical consequence: according to Abu Hanifa's first basis, even later specification does not validate; according to the second basis, specification at the time of lawsuit validates; according to the Sahibayn, specification always validates.

كتاب الكفالة تعريفها وشروطها وأحكامها

فقيل : عدم الجواز عنده مبني على أنه قال : فعليه المئة، ولم يقل المئة التي على المدعى عليه، فعلى هذا إن بين المدعي المئة لا يكونُ كفالة عنده صحيحاً أيضاً، كما إذا لم يُبين إلا أن يقول : فعليه المئة التي يدعيها. وقيل : إنَّه مبني على أنَّه لما لم يُبين لم يصح الدعوى، فلم يستوجب إحضاره إلى مجلس القاضي، فلم يصح الكفالة بالنفس، ولا يجوز الكفالة بالمال، فعلى هذا إن بين يكون الكفالة صحيحة .

ولهما : أنه قال : فعليه المئة ، أو عليه المال، فيراد به المعهود، فإن بَيَّنَ المدعي فظاهر، وإن لم يبين فبعد ذلك، إذا بين التحق البيان بأصل الدعوى ، فتبين صحة الكفالة بالنَّفس، فيترتب عليها الكفالة بالمال.

والثاني : : ما قاله الشيخ أبو الحسن الكرخي له : وهو أنَّ المدعي لما لم يبين مالاً مقدراً لم يستوجب إحضاره إلى مجلس القاضي ؛ لفساد الدعوى، فلا تصح الكفالة بالنفس ؛ لعدم صحة الدعوى، ولا تصح الكفالة بالمال أيضاً ؛ لأنها مبنية على الكفالة بالنفس، فإذا بطل الأصلُ بطل الفرع ، وهذا الوجه يوجب أن تصح الكفالة إذا بين المال عند الدعوى. [١] قوله : ولهما ... الخ ؛ تحريره : إنّ هذه كفالة أمكن تصحيحها فتصح ، أما إذا بَيْنَ المال عند الدعوى فظاهر أنه ذكر معرَّفاً باللام ، وقال : فعليه المئة، فينصرف إلى ما على المدعى عليه بجعل اللام للعهد، فكانت النسبة موجودة، فخرج المالُ عن كونه رشوة، فيلزم وتصح الكفالة. وأما إذا لم يُبين ؛ فلأنَّ العادة جرت بالإيهام في الدعاوي في غير مجلس القضاء ، فيحملونها إجمالاً، ولا يبينونها إلا عند القاضي دفعاً لحيل الخصوم، وصونا لكلامهم إلى وقت الحاجة، فصحت الدعوى، والملازمة على احتمال البيان من جهته ، فإذا بَيْنَ انصرف بيانه إلى ابتداء الدعوى، فظهر به أنَّ الكفالة بالنفس قد صحت، فتصح الكفالة بالمال أيضاً ؛ لأنها مبنية عليها ، ولأنه لو جعل التزاماً عليه تصح وإلا فلا ، فيحمل عليه تصحيحاً لتصرفه. [٢] قوله : المئة ؛ وذكر في «الجامع الصغير) (۱) وغيره منكراً فلا تستقيم هذه النكتة على هذا الوجه.

(۱) «الجامع الصغير» (ص ۳۷۱).
Book of Kafalah: its definition, conditions, and rulings. It is said: The non-permissibility according to him (Abu Hanifa) is based on that he said: 'Upon him is the hundred,' and he did not say 'the hundred that is upon the defendant.' Accordingly, if the plaintiff specifies the hundred, it is not a valid kafalah according to him either, as if he did not specify, unless he says: 'Upon him is the hundred that he claims.' And it is said: It is based on that since he did not specify, the lawsuit is not valid, so it does not necessitate bringing him to the court, so kafalah by self is not valid, and kafalah by property is not permitted. Accordingly, if he specifies, the kafalah is valid. And for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): He said 'Upon him is the hundred' or 'upon him is the property,' and by it is meant the well-known (debt). If the plaintiff specifies, it is apparent; and if he does not specify, then afterwards, if he specifies, the specification attaches to the original lawsuit, thus the validity of kafalah by self becomes clear, and kafalah by property follows from it. The second (view): What Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi said for him: That since the plaintiff did not specify a quantified property, he did not necessitate bringing him to the court due to the invalidity of the lawsuit, so kafalah by self is not valid due to the lack of validity of the lawsuit, and also kafalah by property is not valid because it is based on kafalah by self; if the original is invalid, the branch is invalid. This view requires that kafalah be valid if he specifies the property at the time of the lawsuit. [1] His statement: 'And for the two... etc.'; its exposition: This is a kafalah that can be validated, so it is valid. As for if he specified the property at the time of the lawsuit, it is apparent that he mentioned it with the definite article (al-lam) and said 'Upon him is the hundred,' so it refers to what is upon the defendant by making the lam for the well-known, so the connection exists, so the property is not considered a bribe, so it is binding and the kafalah is valid. As for if he did not specify, because custom involves vagueness in lawsuits outside the court; they treat them summarily and do not specify except before the judge to avoid tricks of opponents and to preserve their speech until needed, so the lawsuit is valid, and the obligation is on the possibility of specification from his side. So when he specifies, his specification refers back to the beginning of the lawsuit, thus it becomes apparent that kafalah by self was valid, so kafalah by property is also valid because it is based on it. And because if it is made an obligation upon him, it is valid, otherwise not, so it is interpreted in a way that validates his transaction. [2] His statement: 'the hundred'; and it is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir and elsewhere as indefinite, so this nuance is not correct according to this view.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of kafalah (suretyship) when the amount of debt is unspecified at the time of the contract. The author first states two interpretations for Abu Hanifa's position of non-permissibility: (1) The surety said 'upon him is the hundred' without specifying which hundred (the one claimed by plaintiff or another), making it ambiguous and thus invalid. (2) The plaintiff's lawsuit itself is invalid because the amount is unspecified, so the defendant cannot be compelled to appear, making kafalah by self invalid, and subsequently kafalah by property also invalid. However, if the amount is specified at the time of suretyship, kafalah is valid according to the second interpretation. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the phrase 'upon him is the hundred' is understood contextually to refer to the known debt (the one claimed), and even if unspecified initially, subsequent specification by the plaintiff retroactively validates the initial suretyship, as specification attaches to the original claim. Then the author presents a third view attributed to Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi, which aligns with Abu Hanifa's second interpretation. The commentary (marked [1]) explains why the companions' view is correct: if the amount is specified at the outset, it clearly refers to the defendant's debt; if not, custom allows vagueness outside court, so the lawsuit is valid and subsequent specification retroactively validates the kafalah. The note [2] points out that in al-Jami' al-Saghir, the word 'hundred' is mentioned indefinitely, so the nuance about definiteness does not apply in that version.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Legal guarantee or suretyship, where a person (kafil) undertakes to bring the defendant (asil) or pay a debt on their behalf.
المعهود (al-ma'hud): The well-known or previously mentioned debt; the contextually understood obligation.
اللام للعهد (al-lam li al-'ahd): The definite article 'al-' used to refer to a previously mentioned or known entity (the specific debt).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is invalid because the debt is unknown.
B) It is valid, and subsequent specification retroactively validates it.
C) It is valid only if the plaintiff specifies the amount in court immediately.
D) It is valid only for kafalah bi al-nafs, not for kafalah bi al-mal.

Key Takeaway
The validity of kafalah with unspecified amounts depends on whether later specification is considered retroactively effective; the mufta bihi (Sahibayn) allows it.

Compulsion to Provide Surety in Hudud and Qisas: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on compulsion to provide surety in hudud and qisas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on compulsion to provide surety in hudud and qisas, and identify the practical consequence of their difference.

Core Idea
Hudud are built on warding off (dar'), but where a servant's right is involved, the Sahibayn allow compulsion to provide surety.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a judge can compel a defendant in hadd or qisas cases to provide a surety (kafalah bi al-nafs). Imam Abu Hanifa held that compulsion is never allowed because hudud are founded on warding off (dar') – they should be prevented when possible. However, his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, distinguished between pure divine rights and those involving a servant's right, allowing compulsion in qadhf (false accusation) and qisas (retaliation). The commentary clarifies that 'compulsion' here means accompaniment (mulazama), not imprisonment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
The core question: Can a judge force a defendant in a hadd or qisas case to provide a surety to ensure his presence?
This applies when the claimant requests a surety before producing evidence.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position
Abu Hanifa: No compulsion in any hadd or qisas. His dalil: hudud are based on dar' (warding off by doubts), and kafalah (securitization) contradicts this principle. Allah is independent of such security.
For Abu Hanifa, even qadhf and qisas are not exceptions.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
The Sahibayn differentiate: compulsion is allowed in the hadd of qadhf (because it involves the victim's right) and in qisas (pure servant's right). Their dalil: surrendering oneself is obligatory in such claims, so kafalah is valid as a means, similar to property claims.
They agree with Abu Hanifa on other hudud (e.g., theft, zina) that are purely divine.
4
Analyze the Practical Consequence
Under the Sahibayn's view, the judge can order accompaniment (mulazama): the claimant follows the defendant to ensure he does not flee. Under Abu Hanifa, the judge cannot compel a surety, leaving the claimant to rely on other means.
Accompaniment is not imprisonment; if the defendant refuses entry, the claimant can detain him at the door.
5
Determine the Operative Position
The commentary conveys the Sahibayn's view as the operative ruling for qadhf and qisas, while Abu Hanifa's view applies to other hudud. This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
The authority is from later Hanafi works like al-Kifayah and al-Fawa'id al-Dhahiriyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا جبر على إعطاء الكفيل في حد وقصاص ولو سمحت نفسه صح

[0]

[۲] قوله : يجبر ؛ أي المدعى عليه على إعطاء الكفيل، ونقل صاحب «الكفاية»(۱) عن الفوائد الظهيرية»: إنه ليس تفسير الجبر هاهنا الحبس ، لكن يأمره بالملازمة، وليس تفسير الملازمة المنع من الذهاب ؛ لأنه حبس.

بخلاف الحدود الخالصة الله ؛ لأنَّ الكفالة شرعت وثيقة لنا، كيلا يفوت حقنا، والله له غني عن ذلك وبخلافِ نفس الحدّ أو القصاص ؛ لأنه لا يمكن استيفاؤه من الكفيل.

[٤] قوله : إن مبناهما ... الخ ؛ حاصله : إنَّ الكفالة وضعت للاستيثاق، ومبناهما على الدرء، فالإجبار على إعطاء الكفيل فيها يفضي إلى فسادِ الوضع، بخلاف سائرِ الحقوق ؛ لأنها لا تسقط بالشبهات.

(۱) (الكفالة (٦ : ٢٩٥)
There is no compulsion to provide a surety in [cases of] hadd and qisas, and if his soul permits, it is valid. (There is no compulsion to provide a surety in hadd and qisas), this is according to Abu Hanifa. And according to the two (Sahibayn), he is compelled in the hadd of qadhf because there is a right of the servant, and in qisas because it is a pure right of the servant. And for Abu Hanifa, he (argues that) their foundation is on warding off (dar'), so securing (istithaq) is not obligatory. (And if his soul permits, it is valid): i.e., if the one upon whom is the hadd or qisas permits and gives a surety by self, it is valid. [1] His statement: 'There is no compulsion... etc.'; depiction of the issue: If the claimant of qisas or the hadd of qadhf asks the judge to take a surety for the defendant's person until he brings evidence, the judge does not compel him to give the surety like other hadd punishments according to the Imam. [2] His statement: 'he is compelled'; i.e., the defendant is compelled to give the surety. The author of al-Kifayah narrated from al-Fawa'id al-Dhahiriyyah: The interpretation of compulsion here is not imprisonment, but he orders him to accompany (mulazama), and the interpretation of accompaniment is not preventing him from going because that is imprisonment. Rather, the claimant goes with the defendant and turns around with him wherever he goes, so that he does not disappear. When he reaches the door of his house and wants to enter, the claimant asks his permission to enter. If he permits, he enters with him and resides where he resides. If he does not permit, he detains him at the door of his house and prevents him from entering. [3] His statement: 'because... etc.'; clarification of the evidence for compulsion in the hadd of qadhf and qisas: That kafalah by self is legislated, and surrendering oneself is obligatory upon the principal (asil) in claims of hadd and qisas, so kafalah is valid therein as in claims of property. Unlike the hadd punishments that are purely for Allah, because kafalah was legislated as a security for us so that our right does not lapse, and Allah is independent of that. And unlike the actual hadd or qisas itself, because it cannot be exacted from the surety. [4] His statement: 'their foundation... etc.'; its summary: Kafalah was established for securing (rights), and their (hadd and qisas) foundation is on warding off (dar'), so compelling to give a surety therein leads to corruption of the system, unlike other rights because they are not dropped by doubts. [0] His statement: 'it is valid'; by consensus, because surrendering oneself is due from the principal, so it is valid.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute over whether a defendant in cases of hadd (punishments) and qisas (retaliation) can be compelled to provide a surety (kafalah bi al-nafs). According to Abu Hanifa, there is no compulsion in any hadd or qisas, because the foundational principle in these cases is 'warding off' (dar' al-hudud), meaning that punishments are to be prevented by doubts, and security measures contradict this principle. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differentiate: they agree with Abu Hanifa for hadd punishments that are purely the rights of Allah (e.g., hadd for theft, zina), but they rule that compulsion is allowed in the hadd of qadhf (false accusation) because it involves a right of the servant (the victim's right), and in qisas because it is a pure right of the servant. Therefore, the defendant can be compelled to give a surety in these two cases. The commentary (marked [2]) explains that 'compulsion' here does not mean imprisonment, but rather an order of accompaniment (mulazama): the claimant follows the defendant everywhere to ensure his presence. If the defendant enters his home and does not permit the claimant to enter, the claimant can detain him at the door. The evidence (marked [3]) for the Sahibayn is that surrendering oneself is obligatory on the defendant in such claims, so kafalah is valid as a means to secure that obligation. Abu Hanifa's counter-argument (marked [4]) is that the very purpose of kafalah (securitization) conflicts with the principle of dar' in hudud, so compulsion would undermine the system. Finally, it is noted that if the defendant voluntarily provides a surety, it is valid by consensus.

Key Terms
الدرء (al-dar'): Legal principle that hadd punishments are to be warded off by doubts (shubuhat), meaning they should be avoided when possible.
الاستيثاق (al-istithaq): Seeking security or guarantee; the purpose of kafalah is to secure the right of the claimant.
الملاومة (al-mulazama): Accompaniment; the claimant follows the defendant to ensure he does not flee, as an alternative to imprisonment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), in which cases can a defendant be compelled to provide a surety?
A) In all hadd punishments and qisas.
B) Only in hudud that are purely the rights of Allah.
C) In the hadd of qadhf and in qisas.
D) Only in qisas, not in any hadd.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, compulsion to provide surety in hudud is generally not allowed, but an exception is made for qadhf and qisas according to the Sahibayn, based on the involvement of the servant's right.

Kafalah — Surety

No Compulsion for Kafil in Hadd and Qisas
Intra-Hanafi dispute on compulsion to provide surety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on compulsion to provide a surety in hadd and qisas, distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and explain the legal reasoning based on the principle of dar'.

Core Idea
Hadd and qisas are built on avoidance (dar'), so requiring a surety contradicts their purpose—except where a servant's right is involved, according to the disciples.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: can a judge compel a defendant to provide a surety (kafil) in hadd and qisas cases? The matn gives Abu Hanifa's ruling of no compulsion, while the two disciples argue for compulsion in slander (hadd al-qadhf) and retaliation (qisas) because these involve a servant's right. Understanding their reasoning illuminates the Hanafi approach to criminal law and the balance between God's rights and servant's rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue and primary ruling
Read the matn: 'There is no compulsion to give the surety in hadd and qisas.' Note that this is the position of Abu Hanifa. The issue is whether a judge can force a defendant to provide a person-surety (kafalah bi al-nafs) pending trial.
The ruling applies to all hudud and qisas, not just the ones mentioned.
2
Locate the intra-madhhab dispute
The commentary states that according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), compulsion applies in hadd al-qadhf (because it involves a right of the slandered servant) and in qisas (because it is a pure right of the victim). Note the basis: the presence of a servant's right makes suretyship analogous to financial claims.
The Sahibayn do not extend compulsion to other hudud (e.g., theft, drinking) because those are pure rights of God.
3
Understand the key term 'jabr' and its technical meaning
The commentary clarifies that 'jabr' here does not mean imprisonment (habs). Instead, it means the judge orders the claimant to accompany the defendant (mulazama) to prevent absconding. If the defendant refuses permission to enter his house, the claimant may detain him at the doorstep—but this is not full imprisonment.
This nuance is crucial: 'compulsion' is limited to accompaniment, not incarceration.
4
Apply the principle of dar' to derive the ruling
Abu Hanifa's argument: hudud and qisas are built on the principle of dar' (avoidance)—they are to be warded off by doubts. Suretyship (kafalah) was legislated to secure rights, but since these punishments are designed to be avoided, requiring a surety contradicts their foundation. Therefore, no compulsion.
The disciples' response: even though avoidance is a basis, the presence of a servant's right requires securing the right, thus compulsion is justified in those cases.

Butter of the conclusion for the support of the judiciary. There is no compulsion to give the surety in [cases of] prescribed punishment and retaliation, and if he willingly consents, it is valid. (There is no compulsion to give the surety in prescribed punishment and retaliation): this is according to Abu Hanifah. According to the two companions, he is compelled in the prescribed punishment for slander because it involves a right of the servant, and in retaliation because it is a pure right of the servant. For Abu Hanifah is that their basis is on avoidance, so securing assurance is not required in them. (And if he willingly consents, it is valid): meaning if the one upon whom is the prescribed punishment or retaliation willingly consents and gives a surety for the person, it is valid. [0] [1] His statement: 'And no compulsion... etc.': The depiction of the issue: That if the claimant of retaliation or the prescribed punishment for slander asks the judge to take a surety for the person of the defendant until he brings the evidence, the judge does not compel him to give the surety, like other prescribed punishments according to the Imam. [2] His statement: 'Is compelled': meaning the defendant is compelled to give the surety. The author of al-Kifayah transmitted from al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah: that the interpretation of compulsion here is not imprisonment, but he orders him to accompany. And the interpretation of accompaniment is not prevention from going, because that is imprisonment. Rather, the claimant goes with the defendant and circulates with him wherever he goes, so that he does not absent himself. And when he reaches the door of his house and wants to enter, the claimant asks his permission to enter. If he permits him, he enters with him and dwells where he dwells. And if he does not permit him, he imprisons him at the door of his house and prevents him from entering. [3] His statement: 'Because... etc.': Clarification of the evidence for compulsion in the prescribed punishment for slander and retaliation: That suretyship for the person is legislated, and surrender of the person is obligatory upon the principal in the claim of prescribed punishment and retaliation, so suretyship for it is valid in them, as in a claim of property. Unlike the prescribed punishments that are purely for God, because suretyship was legislated as a document for us so that our right does not lapse, and God is independent of that. And unlike the very prescribed punishment or retaliation, because it cannot be exacted from the surety. [4] His statement: 'Indeed their basis... etc.': Its summary: That suretyship was established for securing assurance, and their basis is on avoidance, so compelling to give the surety in them leads to corruption of the establishment, unlike other rights because they are not nullified by ambiguities. [0] His statement: 'Is valid': By consensus, because surrender of the person is due upon the principal, so suretyship for it is valid. Unlike other prescribed punishments. And al-Tumurtashi appended the prescribed punishment for theft to them and made it from the rights of servants, because the claim in it is a condition, unlike others due to the lack of its requirement in it. Thus in al-Bahr. And if you desire detail, refer to it and to al-Kafi.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a judge can compel a defendant to provide a surety (kafil) in hadd (prescribed punishment) and qisas (retaliation) cases. The matn states: 'There is no compulsion to give a surety in hadd and qisas, and if he willingly consents, it is valid.' The commentator clarifies that this is the position of Abu Hanifa. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: they say compulsion is applied in hadd al-qadhf (slander) because it involves a right of the servant (the slandered person's right to have the punishment executed), and in qisas because it is a pure right of the servant (the victim's right to retaliation). Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the basis of hadd and qisas is avoidance (dar') – meaning they are to be warded off by doubts – so securing a surety is not required. The commentary then explains the nature of 'compulsion' (jabr): it does not mean imprisonment, but rather the judge orders the claimant to accompany the defendant to prevent absconding, without using imprisonment. The text also discusses the validity of voluntary suretyship and cites arguments from the two disciples, including analogical reasoning from financial claims. The passage concludes with a note that al-Tumurtashi added theft to the category where suretyship is valid, treating it as a right of the servant because a claim is required.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where a person (kafil) guarantees the presence of another (asil) or payment of a debt.
جبر (jabr): Compulsion; here it means forcing the defendant to provide a surety, but technically it is accompaniment (mulazama), not imprisonment.
الدرء (al-dar'): Avoidance; the principle that hudud and qisas are to be warded off by doubts (shubuhat).
حد القذف (hadd al-qadhf): Prescribed punishment for slander (false accusation of adultery).
ملازمة (mulazama): Accompaniment; the claimant goes with the defendant to prevent absconding, without imprisoning him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is there no compulsion to provide a surety in hadd and qisas cases?
A) Because the defendant has an absolute right to refuse.
B) Because hadd and qisas are based on avoidance (dar'), so securing a surety is not required.
C) Because the judge does not have authority over criminal cases.
D) Because the claimant must provide the surety instead.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the principle of dar' (avoidance) overrides the need for suretyship in hadd and qisas, so a judge cannot compel a defendant to provide a surety, though voluntary suretyship is valid. The disciples allow compulsion only when a servant's right is involved, but the relied-upon view is that of Abu Hanifa.

Imprisonment on Suspicion in Hadd and Qisas Cases
Ruling on the evidentiary threshold for imprisoning a defendant in hudud and qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for imprisoning a defendant in hadd/qisas cases under the Hanafi school and explain the evidentiary threshold (musturan or one just witness).

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, a defendant can be imprisoned for suspicion of corruption based on the testimony of two musturan or one just witness — a lower threshold than for a final conviction.

In the Book of Kafalah, after establishing that there is no compulsion for suretyship according to Abu Hanifa, the matn addresses the claimant's options. If the claimant cannot secure a surety, what can he do? The ruling clarifies that imprisonment is not immediate; it requires at least two musturan witnesses or one just witness to testify, establishing suspicion (tuhma). This lesson unpacks that ruling, the types of witnesses, the nature of the imprisonment (ta'zir, not punishment), and the evidence from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is whether to imprison the defendant. The rule: no imprisonment until witnesses testify. Once they do, imprisonment for suspicion is permissible.
The ruling is about judicial discretion, not automatic punishment.
2
Understand the witness categories
Mustur: a witness whose probity is unknown but not known to be corrupt; acceptable for establishing suspicion. 'Adl: a witness whose justice is known to the judge. One 'adl suffices; for mustur, need two.
This is a lower threshold than for a final judgment, which requires two 'adl witnesses.
3
Grasp the legal basis for imprisonment
The imprisonment is for tuhmat al-fasad (suspicion of corruption), not for precaution or punishment. It is a discretionary measure (ta'zir) to secure the defendant's presence.
The commentary explicitly distinguishes this from precautionary imprisonment.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There are two narrations from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: (a) imprison without surety, (b) take surety without imprisonment. The mufta bihi is imprisonment per the matn.
The ruling in the matn is relied upon for fatwa.
5
Examine the dalil
The hadith of the two Ghifar men: the Prophet (PBUH) imprisoned one suspect based on accusation, establishing the permissibility of imprisonment for suspicion.
The hadith is from Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq; it is ahad and zanni in transmission and indication.

ولا حبس فيهما حتّى يشهد مستوران أو عدل
۳۳۵
( ولا حبس فيهما حتّى يشهد مستوران أو عدل) : لما ذُكِرَ أنَّه لا جبر على الكفالة عند أبي حنيفة " . ذكر ماذا يصنع صاحب الحق ، فعنده يلازمه إلى وقت قيام القاضي عن المجلس، فإن أحضر البيئة فيها، وإن أقام مستورين أو شاهداً عدلاً لا يكفل عند أبي حنيفة ، بل يحبسه "
 الكفاله به بخلاف غيرهما من الحدود، وألحق التُّمُرْتَاشِيُّ حد السرقة بهما، وجعله من حقوق العباد ؛ لكون الدعوى فيه شرطاً بخلاف غيره ؛ لعدم اشتراطها فيه، كذا في «البحر» (١) ، وإن شئت التفصيل فارجع إليه وإلى «الكافي».

Book of Suretyship: Its definition, conditions and rulings. And there is no imprisonment in them until two musturan (acceptable witnesses) or one just [witness] testifies. (And there is no imprisonment in them until two musturan or a just [witness] testifies): Since it was mentioned that there is no compulsion for suretyship according to Abu Hanifah, he mentioned what the right-holder does. According to him, he accompanies him until the time the judge rises from the session. If he brings the evidence therein, and if he brings two musturan or one just witness, he does not give a surety according to Abu Hanifah, but rather he imprisons him. Suretyship for it is valid unlike other prescribed punishments. And al-Tumurtashi appended the prescribed punishment for theft to them and made it from the rights of servants because the claim in it is a condition, unlike others due to the lack of its requirement in it. Thus in al-Bahr. And if you desire detail, refer to it and to al-Kafi. (1) His statement: 'Or a just [witness]': i.e., a just witness whose justice the judge knows, so he does not need to modify him. If he is unknown, he does not imprison him due to the lack of number and justice. It cannot be said: The meaning of precaution in imprisonment is more than taking a surety. Because we say: Imprisonment here is for suspicion of corruption, not for precaution, so he imprisons him as a discretionary punishment. And the testimony of musturan, even if it is not suitable for a ruling, is suitable for establishing suspicion, and the report of a single person is evidence in matters of faith, so suspicion is established by the testimony of a just person, even if the original right is not established by it. [2] His statement: 'According to Abu Hanifah' contrary to what is in a narration. For in this issue there are two narrations from them: In one narration: he imprisons and does not give surety, and in another narration: he gives surety and does not imprison; due to the lack of proof of slander and retaliation by complete evidence. Thus in Majma' al-Anhur. [3] His statement: 'Rather he imprisons him for suspicion': i.e., for suspicion of corruption, and imprisonment for suspicion of corruption is legislated. For what 'Abd al-Razzaq narrated in his Musannaf from 'Urak ibn Malik who said: 'Two men from Banu Ghifar came and stopped at some watering places of Madinah, and with them were people from Ghatafan who had camels. In the morning the Ghatafaniyoon found two of their camels missing, so they accused the Ghifariyoon. They brought them to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), and he imprisoned one of the Ghifariyoon and said to the other: "Go and search." It was not long before he brought them.'
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage discusses the procedure when a claimant in a hadd or qisas case cannot provide a surety (since there is no compulsion according to Abu Hanifa). The ruling: the defendant is not imprisoned until at least two musturan (witnesses whose moral probity is not fully established but acceptable for suspicion) or one just witness (known to be 'adl) testify. Upon such testimony, the judge can imprison the defendant for suspicion of corruption (tuhmat al-fasad). The commentary clarifies that imprisonment here is for ta'zir (discretionary punishment) based on suspicion, not for precaution. The testimony of musturan or a single just witness suffices to establish suspicion, even if not enough for a final judgment. There is also an intra-Hanafi variation narrated: some say imprison without surety, some say take surety without imprisonment. The passage concludes with a hadith from Abd al-Razzaq showing that the Prophet (PBUH) imprisoned a suspect based on accusation.

Key Terms
مستور (mastur): A person whose moral probity is not known to be corrupt but also not fully established as 'adl; acceptable for establishing suspicion.
حبس للتهمة (habs li al-tuhmah): Imprisonment based on suspicion of corruption, not as punishment but as a discretionary measure to secure presence.
عدل (adl): A just witness whose moral probity is known to the judge; his single testimony establishes suspicion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) One male Muslim witness.
C) Two musturan or one just witness.
D) Four witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a defendant in a hadd/qisas case may be imprisoned on suspicion based on testimony of two musturan or one just witness, as a discretionary measure (ta'zir) to secure presence, not as punishment.

Validity of Mortgage and Surety for Kharaj
Stating the ruling and reason for validity of rahn and kafalah for kharaj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of rahn and kafalah for kharaj and explain the rationale that kharaj is a debt.

Core Idea
Kharaj is a debt, so it can be secured by mortgage and surety.

In this lesson, we examine a concise ruling from the Hanafi tradition: it is valid to secure the land tax (kharaj) through a mortgage (rahn) or a personal guarantee (kafalah). The reason given is that kharaj is a debt (dayn). This classification has practical implications for how the state can secure tax revenue. We will unpack the ruling, its proof, and the underlying logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the statement: 'Mortgage and surety for the kharaj are valid'. This is the hukm: validity (sihhah).
The ruling applies to both rahn and kafalah.
2
Locate the 'illa (reason)
The commentary states: 'because it is a debt'. The 'illa is the nature of kharaj as a dayn.
The word 'li-annahu dayn' is the key.
3
Understand the nature of kharaj
Kharaj is a land tax owed to the treasury; it is a financial obligation that can be collected. This distinguishes it from purely religious duties like zakat.
Hanafi law treats it as a regular debt.
4
Apply the general principle
Since rahn and kafalah are valid for any valid debt (dayn sahih), they are valid for kharaj by analogy.
The ruling follows from the general rules of securing debts.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some schools may consider kharaj akin to zakat and not a debt, thus not securable. The Hanafi position is based on its nature as a collectible claim.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وصح الرهن والكفالة بالخراج (۳)(۱) ؛ لأنه (4) دين، (r)4 فصح الرهن والكفالة بالخراج (۱)؛ لأنه دين صحيح ممكن الاستفياء ينظر: «الدر المنتقى»(۲ : ۱۳۰). (2) في «الموطأ» (١٠ : ٢١٦ – ٢١٧)، وغيره. Commentary
This passage states a ruling and its rationale: it is valid to secure the kharaj (land tax) through a mortgage (رهن) or suretyship (كفالة). The commentator explains that the reason is that the kharaj is a debt (دين) – specifically a valid debt that can be collected. The passage also includes cross-references to other works. The commentator in the margin notes that the mention of kharaj here is because it is analogous to zakat in being a religious obligation, yet unlike zakat it is a debt owed to the treasury, so it can be secured like any other debt. The ulama derived this from the general permissibility of securing debts. The proof is the nature of kharaj as a financial obligation that the state can claim; thus it falls under the general rules of rahn and kafalah which are for securing claims. This contrasts with obligations like zakat that are not considered debts in the same way.

Key Terms
خراج (kharaj): Land tax imposed on agricultural land under Muslim rule; a debt owed to the treasury.
رهن (rahn): A mortgage or pledge securing a debt; valid for kharaj because it is a collectible debt.
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship or guarantee for a debt; valid for kharaj as it is a debt.

Mortgage and surety for kharaj are valid in the Hanafi school because kharaj is a debt (dayn) that can be collected.

Multiple Sureties and Surety for Property
Rulings on multiple sureties and surety for property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on multiple sureties and suretyship for property, identify the proof (ijma), and explain the leniency (tawassu') principle.

Core Idea
Surety for property is valid by consensus; multiple sureties for a person are cumulative, not alternative.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya ala Sharh al-Wiqaya addresses two key rulings in the contract of suretyship (kafalah). First, it establishes that taking multiple sureties for the same person does not cancel previous sureties but adds to them. Second, it affirms the validity of surety for property, citing consensus of the ummah as the proof. The commentator also explains the leniency inherent in the contract, allowing for ignorance of the exact amount of the debt and permitting an option clause exceeding three days.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage presents two distinct rulings: (1) taking a surety for a person and then another surety – both are sureties; (2) surety for property is valid.
2
State the rule on multiple sureties
The second surety does not cancel the first; both are valid and cumulative. The reason is that the right of demand can be shared, and the purpose is documentation (tawthiq).
See commentary: 'ليس أخذ الكفيل الثاني تركاً للأول'
3
Identify the proof for surety for property
The proof is ijma (consensus of the ummah). The text states: 'وعليه إجماع الأمة' (And there is consensus of the ummah on it).
Ijma is a definitive source (qat'i al-thubut wa al-dalalah).
4
Explain the principle of tawassu'
Surety is based on leniency (tawassu') because it is a gratuitous contract. This leniency allows ignorance of the exact amount of the debt, as long as the debt itself is valid, and permits stipulating an option for more than three days.
This leniency distinguishes kafalah from other contracts like sale.
5
Distinguish surety from zakat
Zakat is not a true debt but a transfer of ownership anew; hence it cannot be secured by surety. Commentators contrast it with surety which is demandable in life and after death.
The passage uses this distinction to clarify the scope of surety.

Book of Surety: Its definition, conditions, and rulings. And taking a surety for the person, then another, and both are sureties. And surety for property is valid, it can be demanded, unlike zakat because it is merely an act. But he brought this issue here, even though the right is to mention it in surety for property, because it is in the mention of surety for the person in hudud and qisas, and kharaj has an affinity with hudud due to what is known in usul al-fiqh that it has a sense of penalty. So for this affinity he brought it here so that it is known that its ruling is the ruling of properties, such that one is compelled to give surety for the person based on the validity of surety in it. (And taking a surety for the person, then another, and both are sureties) means that taking the second surety is not an abandonment of the first. (And surety for property is valid...) [1] His saying: 'demanded' means it is demanded in life and after death, so the cause of the contract can be arranged upon it in pledge and surety. So he distinguished it from zakat, for it is not truly a debt, but rather an expression of transferring ownership anew; hence it is not taken from his estate after death, as stated by Allamah al-Tamartashi. So there is no objection that the debt of zakat is also demanded from the side of the servants, for in apparent wealth the imam demands it, and in hidden wealth the owners are deputies of the imam. [2] His saying: 'i.e., not...' because the ruling of surety is entitlement to demand, and it can be multiple, so the first commitment does not prevent the second, and the purpose of both is documentation, and taking another surety is an increase in documentation, so the second is valid while the first remains, and likewise the third and above. [3] His saying: 'and surety for property' after finishing the issues of one division of surety, which is surety for the person, he began the issues of the second division, which is surety for property. [4] His saying: 'is valid' because surety is legislated for property, and there is consensus of the ummah on it, and it is based on leniency, for it is a donation initially, so ignorance of the property is tolerated after it is a valid debt. Among the effects of leniency is the permissibility of stipulating an option for more than three days, as they have stated.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, presented as a commentary on a base text. It begins with a section heading "Book of Surety" and then states two rulings. First: If a surety is taken for a person (kafalah bi al-nafs), then another surety is taken for the same person, both are valid sureties—the second does not cancel the first. The reason is that the right of demand (istihqaq al-mutalabah) can be shared, and the purpose is documentation (tawthiq), so additional sureties increase security. Second: Surety for property (kafalah bi al-mal) is valid. The proof cited is consensus (ijma' al-ummah). The commentator distinguishes surety from zakat: zakat is not a true debt but a transfer of ownership, so it cannot be secured by surety. The passage also explains why the author mentions the issue of kharaj alongside hudud: because kharaj partakes of both financial and penal aspects. The commentary notes that surety is based on leniency (tawassu'), so even ignorance of the exact amount of the debt is tolerated as long as the debt itself is valid, and stipulating an option for more than three days is allowed.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafālah): A contract of guarantee where a person (kafil) undertakes to fulfill an obligation of another (makful 'anhu).
الكفيل (al-kafīl): The person who provides the guarantee (surety).
التوسع (al-tawassu‘): Leniency; here referring to the flexibility in the contract of surety allowing for ignorance of the exact debt amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Quranic verse
B) Hadith of the Prophet
C) Consensus of the ummah (ijma)
D) Reasoning by analogy (qiyas)

Key Takeaway
Surety for property is valid by consensus, and multiple sureties for a person are cumulative, each independently binding.

Conditional Kafalah: When a Condition Makes the Guarantee Valid
Ruling on conditional kafalah with appropriate condition and procedural dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a condition attached to a kafalah is appropriate (mulaim) and state the ruling on the validity of such a conditional kafalah according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A conditional kafalah is valid only if the condition is a cause of the debt — not an unrelated event.

The Hanafi school allows a kafalah (suretyship) to be made conditional upon a future event, but with a crucial restriction: the event must be a cause (sabab) for the obligation of the debt. This ensures the guarantee is linked to the debt's origin, not an arbitrary condition. In this lesson, we examine the concept of 'shart mulaim' and its application in the context of sale, usurpation, and the guarantee against eviction (daman al-darak). We also explore an intra-Hanafi difference regarding the order of suing the guarantor.

Guided Path
1
Define shart mulaim
Identify the meaning of 'appropriate condition' – it is a condition that is a cause (sabab) for financial liability, such as a sale or an act of usurpation.
The text uses the examples of 'sale', 'what has become due', and 'usurpation'.
2
Apply the examples
For each example given in the text ('if I have sold', 'what has become due', 'what he has usurped'), explain why the condition is considered mulaim and connect it to the validity of the kafalah.
The condition must be phrased in a way that the guarantee is triggered by the occurrence of the debt-causing event.
3
Examine daman al-darak
The kafalah for eviction (daman al-darak) is valid even though the amount of liability is unknown at the time of contract. Understand why this falls under shart mulaim.
The guarantor assumes liability for the price if the sold property is evicted – this is a direct cause of loss.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
According to the apparent narration, the buyer must first sue the seller before the guarantor. Abu Yusuf allows suing the guarantor first. Recognize the practical difference.
This dispute affects the order of recourse, not the validity of the kafalah itself.
5
Identify the exception
If the sold item turns out to be a free person (who cannot be sold), the buyer may sue either the seller or the guarantor immediately by consensus.
This is a unique case where the cause is so clear that the normal procedure is bypassed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو علق الكفالة بشرط ملائم، نحو: ما بايعت فلاناً، أو ما ذاب لك عليه، أو ما غصبك فعلي. (أو علق الكفالة بشرط ملائم، نحو: ما بايعت فلاناً) ، أو ما ذاب لك عليه، أو ما غصبك فعلي. ما ذابَ أي ما وَجَبَ، ففي هذه الصورة: ما ؛ شرطيّة معناه إن بايعت فلاناً، فيكون في معنى التَّعليق، وعنى بالملائم: المناسب، فإنَّ هذه الأشياء أسباب لوجوب المال، فيناسب ضمَّ الدَّمَةِ إِلى الدَّمة. فقولُه : ما بايعت فلاناً : أي ما بايعت منه. أو ضمان المبيع إن لحقه آفة، فالمكفولُ به مجهول ؛ لاحتمال استحقاق الكل أو البعض، فيضمن الكفيل الكل والبعض. وفي «السراج الوهاج»: إذا استحق المبيع كان للمشتري أن يخاصم البائع أولاً ، فإذا ثبت عليه استحقاق المبيع كان له أن يأخذ الثمن من أيهما شاء، وليس له أن يخاصم الكفيل أولاً في ظاهر الرواية ، وعن أبي يوسف له أنَّ له ذلك، وأجمعوا على أنَّ المبيع لو ظهر حُراً كان له أن يخاصم أيهما شاء.
Or he suspends the kafalah upon an appropriate condition, such as: 'if I have sold to so-and-so', or 'what has become due upon him', or 'what he has usurped from me is my liability'. 'What has become due' meaning what is obligatory. In this phrase, 'ma' is conditional, meaning 'if I have sold to so-and-so', so it is in the sense of suspension. By 'appropriate' he means suitable, for these things are causes for the obligation of property, so it is fitting to join liability to liability. His saying 'if I have sold to so-and-so' means 'if I have sold from him'. Or guarantee of the sold item if it is affected by a defect, so the guaranteed thing is unknown due to the probability of full or partial eviction, so the guarantor guarantees the whole or part. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If the sold item is evicted, the buyer may first sue the seller; once eviction is established against him, he may take the price from whichever he wishes, and he may not sue the guarantor first in the apparent narration, and from Abu Yusuf that he may do so. They are agreed that if the sold item appears to be a free person, he may sue whichever he wishes.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage discusses the permissibility of a conditional kafalah where the condition is 'appropriate' (mulaim), meaning the condition is a cause for the obligation of the debt. Examples include a guarantee conditioned on the guarantor having sold something to the debtor ('if I have sold to so-and-so'), or on something becoming due upon him ('what has become due'), or on an act of usurpation ('what he has usurped from me'). These events are causes (sabab) for financial liability, so linking the guarantee to them is logical. The comment explains that 'ma' is a conditional particle meaning 'if'. The intended meaning is that the guarantor promises to cover whatever debt arises from that transaction. There is also discussion of daman al-darak again with a nuance: if the sold item turns out to be defective or evicted, the guarantee covers partial or full loss. An intra-Hanafi dispute is mentioned: in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), the buyer cannot sue the guarantor first; he must first sue the seller for eviction. Abu Yusuf holds that he can sue the guarantor first. However, if the sold item proves to be a free person (who cannot be sold), the buyer can sue either party immediately by consensus.

Key Terms
شرط ملائم (shart mulaim): An appropriate condition that is a cause for the obligation of the debt, such as sale, guarantee of eviction, or usurpation.
ضمان الدرك (daman al-darak): Guarantee against eviction; the guarantor is liable for the price if the sold property is reclaimed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Sue both simultaneously.
D) Forgive the debt.

Key Takeaway
A conditional kafalah is valid only when the condition is a cause of the debt; unrelated conditions render the guarantee invalid; the buyer must typically sue the seller before the guarantor under the relied-upon view.

Surety's Liability and Invalid Conditions in Kafalah
Commentary on surety's liability with discussion on invalid conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the surety's liability in kafalah when the debt is proven by evidence, and distinguish between an invalid condition (ta'liq) and an invalid term (ta'jil) in the contract, applying the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
An unsuitable condition voids kafalah; an unsuitable term voids only itself, making the debt due immediately.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies two key rulings in kafalah: first, how evidence affects the surety's liability; second, how to treat conditions that are not customary for the contract. The author navigates a subtle distinction between suspending the contract (ta'liq) and deferring payment (ta'jil), reconciling apparent contradictions from earlier sources. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the contract of suretyship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the surety's liability rule
The text states that the surety is liable for the amount proven by evidence; without evidence, he is only liable for what he acknowledges on oath, and the principal debtor is believed regarding any greater amount acknowledged against himself alone.
This establishes the burden of proof in kafalah.
2
Recognize the textual error and its correction
The matn might have said 'immediately (haalan)' which the commentator marks as an error because the condition discussed (e.g., 'if you enter the house') is unsuitable, so the kafalah is void. The correct ruling is that the kafalah is void, not merely immediate.
The error arises from confusing a condition with a term.
3
Distinguish between condition (ta'liq) and term (ta'jil)
If the condition suspends the kafalah (e.g., 'if the wind blows') and is unsuitable, the kafalah is void. If it is a deferred term (e.g., 'pay when the wind blows'), the kafalah is valid but the term is void, making the debt due immediately.
The key is whether the condition affects the contract's existence or its timing.
4
Apply the reconciliation of conflicting reports
Some sources (al-Mabsut) say the kafalah is void; others (al-Fath) say it is valid with void condition. The commentator reconciles by interpreting 'suspension' (ta'liq) as 'deferment' (ta'jil) in the context of unsuitable events, so the kafalah remains valid.
This reconciliation is the preferred view (mufta bihi).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة تعريفها وشروطها وأحكامها. فإن كفل بما لك عليه ضمن قدر ما قامت به بينة، وبلا بينة صدق الكفيل فيما يقر به مع حلفه، والأصيل فيما يقر بأكثر منه على نفسه فقط. حالا، وهو سهو؛ لأن الحكم فيه أن التعليق لا يصح، ولا يلزمه المال؛ لأن الشرط غير ملائم، فصار كما لو علقه بدخول الدار ونحوه، مما ليس بملائم. نعم؛ لو جعل الأجل في الكفالة إلى هبوب الريح ونحوه، لا يصح التأجيل، ويجب المال حالا. انتهى. وهكذا في «التبيين» و«المنح» لكن أجاب عنه صاحب «البحر»: بأن قوله: إلا أنه تصح الكفالة؛ إنما يعود إلى الأصل بنحو: إن هبت الريح، لا إلى التعليق بالشرط، وقوله: لما صح تعليقها؛ معناه: لما صح تأجيلها بأجل متعارف مجازاً جاز عدم الثبوت في الحال في كل منهما. وقال في «الفتح»: فالحاصل أن الشرط الغير ملائم لا تصح معه الكفالة أصلاً، ومع الأجل الغير الملائم تصح حالة، ويبطل الأجل، لكن تعليل المصنف لهذا، بقوله: لأن الكفالة لما صح تعليقها بالشرط لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة كالطلاق والعتاق يقتضي أن في التعليق بغير الملائم يصح الكفالة حالة، وإنما يبطل الشرط. والمصرح به في «المبسوط» وفي فتاوى قاضي خان: أن الكفالة باطلة، فتصحيحه أن يحمل لفظ تعليقها على معنى تأجيلها، بجامع أن في كل منهما عدم ثبوت الحكم في الحال، وقلد المصنف الله في هذا الاستعمال لفظ «المبسوط»، فإنه ذكر التعليق وأراد التأجيل. انتهى. ولعلمي أن هذا أحسن وجوه التصحيح، فتأمل فيه ولا تعجل. قوله: ضمن قدر ما قامت به بينة؛ لأن الثابت بالبينة كالثابت عياناً، ولو عاين ما عليه وكفل عنه لزمه ما عليه، فكذا إذا ثبت بالبينة.
Book of Kafalah: Its definition, conditions, and rulings. If he stands surety for what you have against him, he is liable for the amount proven by evidence, and without evidence the surety is believed regarding what he acknowledges with his oath, and the principal debtor is believed regarding what he acknowledges exceeding that, but only against himself. Immediately, and it is an error; because the ruling in it is that the suspension is not valid, and he is not liable for the money; because the condition is unsuitable, so it is as if he suspended it on entering the house and the like, which is not suitable. Yes; if he made the term in kafalah dependent on the blowing of the wind and the like, the deferment is not valid, and the money is due immediately. End. And similarly in al-Tabyin and al-Manh, but the author of al-Bahr responded: that his statement: 'Rather the kafalah is valid' only refers to the original basis like 'if the wind blows', not to suspension by condition, and his statement: 'since its suspension is valid' means: since its deferment by a customary term is valid metaphorically, it is permissible for the ruling not to be established immediately in each. And he said in al-Fath: the conclusion is that the unsuitable condition does not make the kafalah valid at all, and with the unsuitable term it is valid immediately, and the term is void, but the author's reasoning for this, by his statement: 'because since the kafalah is valid with suspension by condition, it is not invalidated by corrupt conditions like divorce and manumission' implies that in suspension by unsuitable things, the kafalah is valid immediately, and only the condition is void. And what is explicitly stated in al-Mabsut and in Fatawa Qadi Khan: that the kafalah is void. So its correction is to interpret the word 'suspension' in the sense of 'deferment', on the basis that in each there is non-establishment of the ruling immediately, and the author followed the usage of al-Mabsut in this, for he mentioned suspension and intended deferment. End. And to my knowledge this is the best of the corrections, so contemplate it and do not be hasty. His statement: 'he is liable for the amount proven by evidence'; because what is established by evidence is like what is established by direct sight, and if he had witnessed what was against him and stood surety, he would be liable for what was against him, so similarly when it is established by evidence.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding a surety's liability when the debtor owes a debt. The original matn states that if a person stands surety for a debt that is owed to you, he is liable for the amount proven by evidence. If there is no evidence, the surety is believed regarding what he acknowledges, with his oath, and the principal debtor is believed regarding any amount greater than that, but only against himself (i.e., the principal's acknowledgment does not bind the surety). The commentator then addresses a potential error: the text might have said 'immediately', which is a mistake because if the condition (e.g., 'if the wind blows') is unsuitable, the kafalah is not valid and he is not liable. However, if the term (ajal) is set based on an unsuitable event, the deferment is invalid and the debt is due immediately. There is a scholarly debate: some say the kafalah is valid but the condition is void, others say the kafalah is void. The author reconciles by interpreting 'suspension' (ta‘līq) to mean 'deferment' (ta’jīl) in this context, based on the usage in al-Mabsut. The commentator then clarifies that what is established by evidence is like what is established by direct sight: if one witnesses the debt and then stands surety, he is liable.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafālah): A contract by which a person (the surety) guarantees a debt or obligation of another (the principal debtor) to the creditor.
التعليق (al-ta‘līq): Suspending the validity or effect of a contract on a future condition.
التأجيل (al-ta’jīl): Postponing the payment or obligation to a future fixed term.
الشرط غير الملائم (al-sharṭ ghayr al-mulā’im): A condition that is not appropriate or customary for the contract, such as 'if the wind blows'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a surety guarantees a debt but makes the liability conditional on 'the wind blowing', what is the ruling according to the preferred view in this text?
A) The kafalah is void and he is not liable.
B) The kafalah is valid but the condition is void, so the debt is due immediately.
C) The kafalah is valid and the condition is upheld.
D) The surety is liable only if the wind blows.

Key Takeaway
Distinguish between a suspensive condition (voids kafalah) and a deferred term (only the term is void; kafalah stands and payment is immediate).
