Creditor's Options and Surety's Oath in Kafalah
Ruling on creditor's options and surety's oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the creditor's options in demanding payment from the principal and surety, and explain the surety's oath of knowledge regarding the debt amount.

Core Idea
In kafalah, the creditor may demand from either party; the surety's acknowledgment limits his liability, and the principal's greater acknowledgment binds only himself.

This passage explains the creditor's procedural rights in kafalah and the rules of evidence when the debt amount is disputed. Understanding these rules protects both parties from unjust claims and clarifies the limits of the surety's liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the creditor's options
State that the creditor may demand payment from the principal, the surety, or both, and that demanding from one does not preclude demanding from the other.
This is based on the nature of kafalah as inclusion (damm).
2
Understand the surety's oath
When the debt amount is disputed, the surety is believed on the amount he acknowledges, provided he swears an oath denying any excess. The oath is on knowledge (al-ḥilf ʿalā al-ʿilm) because it concerns another's liability.
The surety cannot swear to the fact itself, only to his lack of knowledge.
3
Distinguish the effect of principal's acknowledgment
If the principal acknowledges a greater amount than the surety, that acknowledgment is confined to the principal and does not bind the surety. The surety remains liable only for what he acknowledged.
Acknowledgment (iqrār) is a limited proof (ḥujja qāṣira) that only affects the person making it.
4
Contrast with the case of usurpers
The text notes a difference from the case of two usurpers, where the owner's choice against one does not release the other. In kafalah, the creditor can proceed against both.
This highlights the distinct nature of guarantee versus unlawful possession.
5
Recognize the distinction between kafalah and hawalah
If release of the principal is stipulated, the contract becomes hawalah (transfer) rather than kafalah. Contracts are interpreted by their meanings and intent, not merely wording.
This reinforces the principle of intention in contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية. وللطالب مطالبه من شاء من أصيله وكفيله، ومطالبتهما، فإن طالب أحدهما فله مطالبة الآخر أي إن لم يقم البينة صدق الكفيل في مقدار ما يقر به مع أنه يحلف على نفي الزيادة، وينبغي أن يحلف على العلم بأنك لا تعلم أن أكثر من هذا واجب على الأصيل، فإن كفل أو أقر بالزائد لزم عليه، وإنما يحلف على العلم؛ لأن الحلف فيما يجب على الغير ليس إلا على العلم. وإن أقر الأصيل بأكثر مما أقر به الكفيل يكون ذلك مقتصراً عليه؛ لأن الإقرار حجة قاصرة. وكلمة: ما؛ في قوله: فيما يقر به؛ موصولة، والضمير في به راجع إلى: ما. قوله: فيما يقر بأكثر منه مصدرية: أي صدق الأصيل في إقراره بأكثر أي مما يقر به الكفيل، ولو جعلت موصولة يفسد المعنى؛ لأنه حينئذ يصير تقدير الكلام صدق الأصيل في الشيء الذي يقر بأكثر منه: أي من ذلك الشيء، فالشيء الذي يقر الأصيل بأكثر منه، هو ما أقر به الكفيل، والغرض أن الأصيل يصدق في الأكثر لا أنه يصدق فيما أقر به الكفيل. هذا بخلاف المالك إذا اختار أحد الغاصبين. قوله: صدق الكفيل ... الخ؛ لأنه منكر الزيادة التي يدعيها الطالب، والقول قول المنكر مع يمينه، وإذا أقر المطلوب بأكثر مما أقر به الكفيل لا يسري كلامه في الكفيل؛ لعدم ولايته عليه؛ لأن الإقرار على الغير لا ينفذ إلا عن ولاية. وهذا بخلاف ما إذا قال: ما ذاب لك على فلان فعلي، فأقر على نفسه بألف مثلاً، فأنكر الكفيل ما أقر به، حيث يلزمه ما أقر به المطلوب استحساناً، والقياس ألا يلزمه شيء. قوله: وللطالب...الخ؛ أي يثبت الخيار للطالب في المطالبة، إن شاء طالب الأصيل، وإن شاء طالب الكفيل، وإن شاء طالبهما معاً؛ لأنه موجب الكفالة، إذ هي تنبى عن الضم، كما سبق تقريره، وذلك يقتضي بقاء الذمة الأولى لا البراءة، إلا إذا شرط براءة الأصيل، فحينئذ تكون حوالة، كما أن الحوالة بشرط عدم براءة المحيل كفالة؛ لأن العبرة في العقود للمعاني، لا لمجرد اللفظ.
Cream of the End for the Support of the Care. And for the creditor is to demand from whomever he wishes of his principal debtor and his surety, and from both of them. If he demands from one of them, he has the right to demand from the other. That is, if he does not present evidence, the surety is believed regarding the amount he acknowledges, while he takes an oath denying the excess. It is preferable that he take an oath of knowledge: 'You do not know that more than this is owed by the principal.' If he stands surety or acknowledges the excess, it becomes binding upon him. He only takes an oath of knowledge because the oath regarding what is owed by another is only on knowledge. And if the principal acknowledges an amount greater than what the surety acknowledged, that is confined to him, because acknowledgment is a limited proof. The word 'ma' in his statement 'fima yuqirru bihi' is a relative pronoun, and the pronoun in 'bihi' refers to 'ma'. His statement 'fima yuqirru bi-akthara minhu' is a verbal noun: i.e., the principal is believed in his acknowledgment of more, i.e., more than what the surety acknowledges. If it were made a relative pronoun, the meaning would be corrupted; because then the estimated statement would be: the principal is believed regarding the thing of which he acknowledges more, i.e., that thing. So the thing of which the principal acknowledges more is that which the surety acknowledged, but the purpose is that the principal is believed regarding the excess, not that he is believed regarding what the surety acknowledged. This is contrary to the case of the owner if he chooses one of the two usurpers. His statement: 'the surety is believed...' etc.; because he is denying the excess claimed by the creditor, and the statement is the statement of the denier with his oath. And if the debtor acknowledges more than what the surety acknowledged, his speech does not extend to the surety due to lack of authority over him; because acknowledgment against another is effective only with authority. This is contrary to the case where he says: 'What you have against so-and-so is on me,' and he acknowledges against himself a thousand, for example, and the surety denies what he acknowledged, where what the debtor acknowledged becomes binding on him by istihsan, while by qiyas nothing becomes binding on him. His statement: 'And for the creditor...' etc.; i.e., the creditor has the option in demanding: if he wishes he demands from the principal, if he wishes he demands from the surety, and if he wishes he demands from both together; because this is the requirement of kafalah, which is based on inclusion, as previously established. This implies the continuation of the first liability, not release, unless he stipulates the release of the principal, in which case it is a hawalah (transfer), just as a hawalah with the condition of non-release of the transferor is a kafalah; because consideration in contracts is for meanings, not mere wording.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on several rulings regarding kafalah. It begins by stating that the creditor has the right to demand payment from either the principal debtor or the surety, or from both. If the creditor demands from one, he may still demand from the other. If there is no evidence of the debt amount, the surety is believed regarding the amount he acknowledges, with an oath denying any excess. The oath should be on knowledge, i.e., he swears he does not know of any greater amount owed. If the surety subsequently acknowledges or stands surety for the excess, it becomes binding. The principal's acknowledgment of a greater amount is effective only against himself, not the surety, because an acknowledgment is a limited proof that does not affect others. The text clarifies the grammatical structure to avoid misinterpretation. It then contrasts this with the case of a usurper (ghasib) where the owner's choice binds one usurper but not both. Finally, it explains that the creditor's option to demand from any party is inherent in kafalah, as it is based on inclusion (damm). The surety's liability does not release the principal unless release is stipulated, in which case it becomes hawalah (transfer). Contracts are interpreted by their meanings, not just wording.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrār): Acknowledgment or confession; a unilateral declaration that imposes liability only on the declarant.
الحوالة (al-ḥawālah): Transfer of debt from one debtor to another, with the original debtor being released.
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juristic preference, a method of reasoning that departs from strict analogy for reasons of equity or custom.
الحلف على العلم (al-ḥilf ʿalā al-ʿilm): An oath of knowledge, where the swearer testifies that he does not know of a certain fact, rather than swearing to the fact itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must the surety take an oath of knowledge rather than swearing about the debt directly?
A) Because the surety may forget the exact amount
B) Because the debt is owed by another, and one cannot swear to what another owes
C) Because the creditor cannot prove the amount
D) Because oaths are always on knowledge in Hanafi fiqh

Key Takeaway
In kafalah, the creditor may demand from either party, and the surety's liability is limited to what he acknowledges under oath, while the principal's greater acknowledgment binds only himself.

Kafalah — Surety

Admission in Suretyship: Who is Believed?
Commentary on admission rules in kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for determining whether the guarantor or principal is believed regarding the amount owed, and apply the principle that admission is a limited proof (hujja qasira).

Core Idea
The guarantor is believed on oath; the principal's admission does not bind the guarantor.

When a creditor claims a debt from a principal and guarantor, disputes may arise about the exact amount. Hanafi fiqh provides clear rules: the guarantor's statement is accepted with an oath, while the principal's admission is confined to himself. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind these rules, including the concept of iqrar as a limited proof and an exceptional istihsan case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Determine who is claiming what. The creditor claims a larger amount; the guarantor admits a smaller amount; the principal admits a larger amount. Each party's position triggers different rules.
2
Apply the qawl al-munkar principle
The guarantor is the denier of the excess. His statement is accepted with an oath (qawl al-munkar), so he is believed regarding the amount he admits.
He must swear on knowledge that he does not know more is owed.
3
Analyze the effect of the principal's admission
The principal's admission of a larger amount is hujja qasira (limited proof) and only binds himself, not the guarantor. This is because admission against another requires authority (wilayah), which the principal lacks over the guarantor.
4
Recognize the istihsan exception
In a specific scenario where a person says 'What is owed to you by so-and-so is on me' and later admits a specific amount, his admission binds him even if he denies the same elsewhere. This exception is based on istihsan, overriding the general qiyas rule.
The text contrasts this with the general rule where admission does not bind the guarantor.

Source Text

وللطالب مطالبه من شاء من أصيله وكفيله، ومطالبتهما، فإن طالب أحدهما، فله مطالبة الآخر أي إن لم يقم البينة صُدِّقَ الكفيل في مقدار ما يُقر به مع أنه يحلف على نفي الزيادة، وينبغي أن يحلف على العلم بأنَّكَ لا تعلمُ أنَّ أكثر من هذا واجب على الأصيل، فإن كَفِلَ أو أقرَّ بالزَّائدِ لَزِمَ عليه، وإنما يحلف على العلم؛ لأنَّ الحلف فيما يجب على الغير ليس إلا على العلم.

وإن أقرَّ الأصيل بأكثر مما أقرَّ به الكفيل يكون ذلك مقتصراً عليه؛ لأنَّ الإقرار حجةٌ قاصرة. وكلمة : ما ؛ في قوله : فيما يُقِرُّ به ؛ موصولة، والضمير في به راجع إلى : ما. قوله : فيما يُقِرُّ بأكثر منه مصدرية : أي صُدِّقَ الأصيل في إقراره بأكثر أي مما يقر به الكفيل، ولو جعلت موصولة يفسد المعنى؛ لأنَّه حينئذ يصيرُ تقدير الكلام صدق الأصيلُ في الشَّيء الذي يُقِرُّ بأكثر منه : أي من ذلك الشيء، فالشيء الذي يُقِرُّ الأصيلُ بأكثرَ منه، هو ما أقر به الكفيل، والغرض أن الأصيل يُصَدَّقُ في الأكثرِ لا أَنَّه يُصَدَّقُ فيما أقرَّ به الكفيل.

أمَّا رجوعه على الآمر ؛ فلأنه أدَّى دينه بأمرِه فيرجعُ به عليه، وأما بما ضمنه ؛ فلأنَّ رجوعه بحكم الكفالة، فكان بما دخل تحتها؛ ولأنَّ الكفيل ملك الدِّينَ بالأداء، فكان بمنزلة الطَّالب، وصار كما لو ملكه بهبة أو إرث.

The cream of the conclusion for the mainstay of care. The creditor has the right to demand payment from whomever he wishes from his principal and his guarantor, and to demand from both. If he demands from one of them, he may demand from the other. That is, if he does not provide evidence, the guarantor is believed regarding the amount he admits, and he must swear to negate the increase. It is recommended that he swear upon knowledge: 'You do not know that more than this is owed by the principal.' If he becomes surety or admits to the excess, it is binding upon him. He only swears upon knowledge because swearing regarding what is owed by another is only upon knowledge. And if the principal admits to more than what the guarantor admitted, that is confined to him because admission is a limited proof. The word 'what' in his saying 'regarding what he admits' is a relative pronoun, and the pronoun in 'bi-hi' refers to 'what'. His saying 'in what he admits more than it' is a verbal noun: meaning the principal is believed in his admission of more, i.e., more than what the guarantor admits. If it were made a relative pronoun, the meaning would be corrupted because then the estimate of the speech would be: 'the principal is believed in the thing which he admits more than it', i.e., more than that thing. The thing which the principal admits more than it is what the guarantor admitted, and the purpose is that the principal is believed in the excess, not that he is believed in what the guarantor admitted. (And the creditor has the right to demand from whomever he wishes from his principal and his guarantor, and to demand from both. If he demands from one of them, he may demand from the other.) This is unlike the owner if he chooses one of the two usurpers. The statement 'the guarantor is believed' etc.; because he denies the excess claimed by the creditor, and the statement of the denier is accepted with his oath. If the debtor admits to more than what the guarantor admitted, his statement does not affect the guarantor due to the lack of authority over him, because admission against another is not effective except through authority. And this is unlike the case where one says: 'What is owed to you by so-and-so is upon me,' and he admits against himself a thousand, for example, and the guarantor denies what he admitted, where what the debtor admitted is binding upon him by istihsan, while by qiyas nothing would be binding on him. (2) His statement: 'and the creditor...' etc.; meaning the creditor has the option in demanding payment: if he wishes he demands the principal, if he wishes he demands the guarantor, and if he wishes he demands both together; because that is the requirement of kafalah, since it is based on inclusion (dam), as previously established, and that requires the continuation of the first liability, not discharge, unless he stipulates the discharge of the principal, in which case it becomes hawala, just as hawala with the condition of non-discharge of the transferor is kafalah; because the consideration in contracts is for meanings, not merely for wording. The book of kafalah: its definition, conditions, and rulings. And it is valid with and without the command of the principal. Then if he commanded him, he has recourse on him. Because his choosing one of them entails his ownership of it. Meaning when the judge rules accordingly. Such is in the Mabsut of Shaykh al-Islam. So when he owns one of them, it is not possible for him to own the other. (And it is valid with and without the command of the principal, then if he commanded him, he has recourse on him.) (1) His statement: 'entails' etc.; meaning it entails ownership from him when the judge rules it, and he cannot give ownership to the other after that. And demanding payment in kafalah only necessitates that as long as actual satisfaction has not occurred from him. (2) His statement: 'and it is valid' etc.; because it is an act of the guarantor regarding himself by undertaking that the creditor will demand from him, and there is no harm to the principal in that. (3) His statement: 'he has recourse on him after payment' for what he guaranteed, whether he paid what he guaranteed or paid something else. So if he guaranteed one thousand good coins and paid one thousand counterfeit coins with the creditor's consent, he has recourse in good coins. And if he guaranteed one thousand counterfeit coins and paid good coins, he has recourse in counterfeit coins. As for his recourse on the commander, it is because he paid his debt by his command, so he has recourse on him. And as for what he guaranteed, it is because his recourse is by the ruling of kafalah, so it is for what falls under it; and because the guarantor owns the debt by payment, so he is like the creditor, and it becomes as if he owned it by gift or inheritance. Unlike the one commanded to pay the debt, who has recourse only for what he paid if he paid inferior to the debt, and for the debt if he paid superior; because nothing is owed to him until he owns the debt by payment, rather he was a lender. And unlike the case where he settles for less than the debt, and it is of the same type, where he only has recourse for what he paid unless he settles on condition that the creditor remits the remainder and he does so, then he has recourse in full; because he owned the debt wholly or partially by payment, and partially by gift. And the recourse of the guarantor against the principal is not absolute, but is conditioned upon the command being from someone whose admission of debts is valid and who is allowed to donate. So if a minor under guardianship, or a slave under guardianship, commands a man to stand surety for him, the suretyship is valid because its validity depends on the guarantor's undertaking voluntarily, not on the command of the commander.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the rulings of kafalah (suretyship) from the Hanafi matn. It covers three main areas: (1) the believability of the guarantor and principal regarding the amount owed, (2) the creditor's option to demand payment from either party, and (3) the validity of kafalah with or without the principal's command and the guarantor's recourse (ruju'). 

First, regarding admission: When the creditor claims an amount, and the guarantor admits a lesser amount, the guarantor is believed on oath (qawl al-munkar) because he is the denier of the excess. He swears that he does not know that more is owed. If the principal admits a greater amount, that admission only binds the principal himself (iqrar qasir), not the guarantor. An exception is a special case where someone says 'What is owed to you by so-and-so is on me' and then admits a specific amount; by istihsan, that admission binds him even if he denies the same in another context. The text clarifies that the general rule is qiyas, but istihsan is applied here.

Second, the creditor has an absolute option: he may demand payment from the principal, the guarantor, or both together. This is because kafalah is built on inclusion (dam), meaning the guarantor's liability is additional, not substitutional. The principal's liability remains unless the condition of discharge (bara'ah) is stipulated, in which case it becomes hawala (transfer of debt).

Third, kafalah is valid whether the principal commands it or not. If the principal commands it, the guarantor has recourse on him after payment, recovering exactly what he guaranteed, not necessarily what he paid. For example, if he guaranteed good coins and paid bad coins with the creditor's consent, he recovers good coins. The text distinguishes this from a loan where the payer recovers according to what is owed. The guarantor's recourse is conditional on the principal being legally capable of commanding (not a minor or slave under guardianship). Even if the commander is incapable, the kafalah itself is valid because it depends on the guarantor's own undertaking.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A contract whereby a person (guarantor) undertakes to fulfill an obligation owed by another (principal) to a creditor.
الأصيل (al-asil): The principal debtor in a kafalah contract.
الكفيل (al-kafil): The guarantor who undertakes to pay the debt if the principal fails.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): A declaration or admission by a person regarding a right due from him.
الرجوع (al-ruju'): The right of the guarantor to recover from the principal after paying the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the guarantor (kafil) admits a debt of 500, and the principal (asil) admits 800, how much is the guarantor liable for?
A) 800
B) 500
C) 1300
D) 0

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi kafalah, the guarantor's liability is determined by his own admission, not the principal's, because admission is a limited proof.

Kafalah — Surety

Creditor's Option in Suretyship
Commentary on the creditor's option in kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the creditor's option to demand payment from the principal, the guarantor, or both, and distinguish kafalah from hawala based on the continuation of the principal's liability.

Core Idea
The creditor may demand from either party or both; kafalah adds liability, it does not transfer it.

A key feature of kafalah is the creditor's flexibility. He can pursue the principal, the guarantor, or both simultaneously. This lesson explains why this option exists and how it differs from hawala (transfer of debt), where the original debtor is released. The underlying principle is that kafalah is built on inclusion (dam), meaning the guarantor's liability is additional.

Guided Path
1
State the creditor's option
The creditor may demand payment from the principal (asil), the guarantor (kafil), or both. He can also switch between them after demanding from one.
This is unlike the case of choosing one of two usurpers.
2
Understand the basis: kafalah as dam (inclusion)
Kafalah is built on inclusion, meaning the guarantor's liability is added to the principal's. The principal's liability continues unless discharge is stipulated.
This is why the creditor has multiple options.
3
Distinguish from hawala
If the creditor stipulates that the principal is discharged, the contract becomes hawala (transfer), not kafalah. Similarly, if hawala is made without discharge, it is considered kafalah.
Contracts are judged by their underlying meaning, not just wording.
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a scenario, determine whether the creditor retains the right to demand from both parties. If the contract specifies discharge, it is hawala and the creditor can only demand from the transferee.

زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية

وللطالب مطالبه من شاء من أصيله وكفيله، ومطالبتهما، فإن طالب أحدهما، فله مطالبة الآخر أي إن لم يقم البينة صُدِّقَ الكفيل في مقدار ما يُقر به مع أنه يحلف على نفي الزيادة، وينبغي أن يحلف على العلم بأنَّكَ لا تعلمُ أنَّ أكثر من هذا واجب على الأصيل، فإن كَفِلَ أو أقرَّ بالزَّائدِ لَزِمَ عليه، وإنما يحلف على العلم؛ لأنَّ الحلف فيما يجب على الغير ليس إلا على العلم.

وإن أقرَّ الأصيل بأكثر مما أقرَّ به الكفيل يكون ذلك مقتصراً عليه؛ لأنَّ الإقرار حجةٌ قاصرة. وكلمة : ما ؛ في قوله : فيما يُقِرُّ به ؛ موصولة، والضمير في به راجع إلى : ما. قوله : فيما يُقِرُّ بأكثر منه مصدرية : أي صُدِّقَ الأصيل في إقراره بأكثر أي مما يقر به الكفيل، ولو جعلت موصولة يفسد المعنى؛ لأنَّه حينئذ يصيرُ تقدير الكلام صدق الأصيلُ في الشَّيء الذي يُقِرُّ بأكثر منه : أي من ذلك الشيء، فالشيء الذي يُقِرُّ الأصيلُ بأكثرَ منه، هو ما أقر به الكفيل، والغرض أن الأصيل يُصَدَّقُ في الأكثرِ لا أَنَّه يُصَدَّقُ فيما أقرَّ به الكفيل.

( وللطالب "" مطالبه من شاءَ من أصيله وكفيله، ومطالبتهما، فإن طالب أحدهما، فله مطالبة الآخر) ، هذا بخلاف المالك إذا اختار أحد الغاصبين ] قوله : صدق الكفيل ... الخ ؛ لأنه منكر الزيادة التي يدعيها الطالب، والقولُ قول المنكر مع يمينه، وإذا أقر المطلوب بأكثر مما أقرَّ به الكفيل لا يسري كلامه في الكفيل؛ لعدم ولايته عليه؛ لأنَّ الإقرار على الغير لا ينفذ إلا عن ولاية.

وهذا بخلاف ما إذا قال : ما ذاب لك على فلان فعلي، فأقر على نفسه بألف مثلاً، فأنكر الكفيل ما أقرَّ به، حيث يلزمُهُ ما أقرَّ به المطلوب استحساناً، والقياس ألا يلزمه شيء.

[٢] قوله : وللطالب...الخ ؛ أي يثبتُ الخيارُ للطَّالب في المطالبة، إن شاء طالب الأصيل، وإن شاء طالب الكفيل، وإن شاء طالبهما معاً؛ لأنه موجب الكفالة، إذ هي تنبى عن الضم، كما سبق تقريره، وذلك يقتضي بقاء الذمة الأولى لا البراءة، إلا إذا شرط براءة الأصيل، فحينئذ تكون حوالة، كما أنَّ الحوالة بشرط عدم براءة المحيل كفالة؛ لأنَّ العبرة في العقود للمعاني، لا لمجرَّدِ اللفظ.

كتاب الكفالة / تعريفها وشروطها وأحكامها
وتصح بأمر الأصيل وبلا أمره، ثمَّ إن أمره رجع عليه
لأنَّ اختياره أحدهما يتضمَّنُ) تمليكه
يعني إذا قضى القاضي بذلك. كذا في مبسوط شيخ الإسلام» ، فإذا مَلكَ أحدهما لا يُمْكِنَهُ أَن يُمَلكَ الآخر.
(وتصح" بأمر الأصيل وبلا أمره ، ثمَّ إن أمره رجعَ عليه"
[١] قوله : يتضمن....الخ ؛ أي يتضمَّنُ التمليك منه عند قضاء القاضي به، ولا يمكنه التمليك من الآخر بعد ذلك، والمطالبة في الكفالة إنما لا تقتضيه ما لم يوجد منه حقيقة الاستيفاء. (٢) قوله : وتصح ...الخ ؛ لأنها تصرف من الكفيل في نفسه بالتزام أن يطالبه الدائن، ولا ضرر على الأصيل في ذلك.
[٣] قوله : رجع عليه بعد أدائه ؛ بما ضمنه سواء أدّى ما ضمنه أو أدى خلافه، حتى لو كفيل بألف جياد وأدّى ألفاً زيوفاً برضاء الطالب رجع بالجياد، ولو كفل بألف زيوف وأدى جياداً رجع بالزيوف.
أمَّا رجوعه على الآمر ؛ فلأنه أدَّى دينه بأمرِه فيرجعُ به عليه، وأما بما ضمنه ؛ فلأنَّ رجوعه بحكم الكفالة، فكان بما دخل تحتها؛ ولأنَّ الكفيل ملك الدِّينَ بالأداء، فكان بمنزلة الطَّالب، وصار كما لو ملكه بهبة أو إرث.
بخلاف المأمور بأداء الدين، حيث يرجعُ بما أدّى إن أدى أردأ من الدين، وبالدين إن أدى أجود منه؛ لأنه لم يجب عليه شيء حتى يملك الدين بالأداء، بل كان مقرضاً، وبخلاف ما إذا صالح على أقل من الدين، وهو من جنسيه، حيث لا يرجع إلا بقدر ما أدّى إلا إذا صالحه على أن يهبه الباقي ففعل، فحئنذ بجميعه؛ لأنه ملك الدِّينَ كلّه أو بعضه بالأداء، وبعضه بالهبة. ورجوع الكفيل على الأصيل ليس على إطلاقه، بل هو مقيد بما إذا كان الأمر ممن يجوز إقراره على نفسه بالديون، ويملك التبرع حتى لو كان صبياً محجوراً، أو عبداً محجوراً، وأمر رجلاً بأن يكفل عنه، صحت الكفالة؛ لأن صحتها تعتمد التزام الكفيل باختياره، لا أمر الأمر.
The cream of the conclusion for the mainstay of care. The creditor has the right to demand payment from whomever he wishes from his principal and his guarantor, and to demand from both. If he demands from one of them, he may demand from the other. That is, if he does not provide evidence, the guarantor is believed regarding the amount he admits, and he must swear to negate the increase. It is recommended that he swear upon knowledge: 'You do not know that more than this is owed by the principal.' If he becomes surety or admits to the excess, it is binding upon him. He only swears upon knowledge because swearing regarding what is owed by another is only upon knowledge. And if the principal admits to more than what the guarantor admitted, that is confined to him because admission is a limited proof. The word 'what' in his saying 'regarding what he admits' is a relative pronoun, and the pronoun in 'bi-hi' refers to 'what'. His saying 'in what he admits more than it' is a verbal noun: meaning the principal is believed in his admission of more, i.e., more than what the guarantor admits. If it were made a relative pronoun, the meaning would be corrupted because then the estimate of the speech would be: 'the principal is believed in the thing which he admits more than it', i.e., more than that thing. The thing which the principal admits more than it is what the guarantor admitted, and the purpose is that the principal is believed in the excess, not that he is believed in what the guarantor admitted. (And the creditor has the right to demand from whomever he wishes from his principal and his guarantor, and to demand from both. If he demands from one of them, he may demand from the other.) This is unlike the owner if he chooses one of the two usurpers. The statement 'the guarantor is believed' etc.; because he denies the excess claimed by the creditor, and the statement of the denier is accepted with his oath. If the debtor admits to more than what the guarantor admitted, his statement does not affect the guarantor due to the lack of authority over him, because admission against another is not effective except through authority. And this is unlike the case where one says: 'What is owed to you by so-and-so is upon me,' and he admits against himself a thousand, for example, and the guarantor denies what he admitted, where what the debtor admitted is binding upon him by istihsan, while by qiyas nothing would be binding on him. (2) His statement: 'and the creditor...' etc.; meaning the creditor has the option in demanding payment: if he wishes he demands the principal, if he wishes he demands the guarantor, and if he wishes he demands both together; because that is the requirement of kafalah, since it is based on inclusion (dam), as previously established, and that requires the continuation of the first liability, not discharge, unless he stipulates the discharge of the principal, in which case it becomes hawala, just as hawala with the condition of non-discharge of the transferor is kafalah; because the consideration in contracts is for meanings, not merely for wording. The book of kafalah: its definition, conditions, and rulings. And it is valid with and without the command of the principal. Then if he commanded him, he has recourse on him. Because his choosing one of them entails his ownership of it. Meaning when the judge rules accordingly. Such is in the Mabsut of Shaykh al-Islam. So when he owns one of them, it is not possible for him to own the other. (And it is valid with and without the command of the principal, then if he commanded him, he has recourse on him.) (1) His statement: 'entails' etc.; meaning it entails ownership from him when the judge rules it, and he cannot give ownership to the other after that. And demanding payment in kafalah only necessitates that as long as actual satisfaction has not occurred from him. (2) His statement: 'and it is valid' etc.; because it is an act of the guarantor regarding himself by undertaking that the creditor will demand from him, and there is no harm to the principal in that. (3) His statement: 'he has recourse on him after payment' for what he guaranteed, whether he paid what he guaranteed or paid something else. So if he guaranteed one thousand good coins and paid one thousand counterfeit coins with the creditor's consent, he has recourse in good coins. And if he guaranteed one thousand counterfeit coins and paid good coins, he has recourse in counterfeit coins. As for his recourse on the commander, it is because he paid his debt by his command, so he has recourse on him. And as for what he guaranteed, it is because his recourse is by the ruling of kafalah, so it is for what falls under it; and because the guarantor owns the debt by payment, so he is like the creditor, and it becomes as if he owned it by gift or inheritance. Unlike the one commanded to pay the debt, who has recourse only for what he paid if he paid inferior to the debt, and for the debt if he paid superior; because nothing is owed to him until he owns the debt by payment, rather he was a lender. And unlike the case where he settles for less than the debt, and it is of the same type, where he only has recourse for what he paid unless he settles on condition that the creditor remits the remainder and he does so, then he has recourse in full; because he owned the debt wholly or partially by payment, and partially by gift. And the recourse of the guarantor against the principal is not absolute, but is conditioned upon the command being from someone whose admission of debts is valid and who is allowed to donate. So if a minor under guardianship, or a slave under guardianship, commands a man to stand surety for him, the suretyship is valid because its validity depends on the guarantor's undertaking voluntarily, not on the command of the commander.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the rulings of kafalah (suretyship) from the Hanafi matn. It covers three main areas: (1) the believability of the guarantor and principal regarding the amount owed, (2) the creditor's option to demand payment from either party, and (3) the validity of kafalah with or without the principal's command and the guarantor's recourse (ruju'). 

First, regarding admission: When the creditor claims an amount, and the guarantor admits a lesser amount, the guarantor is believed on oath (qawl al-munkar) because he is the denier of the excess. He swears that he does not know that more is owed. If the principal admits a greater amount, that admission only binds the principal himself (iqrar qasir), not the guarantor. An exception is a special case where someone says 'What is owed to you by so-and-so is on me' and then admits a specific amount; by istihsan, that admission binds him even if he denies the same in another context. The text clarifies that the general rule is qiyas, but istihsan is applied here.

Second, the creditor has an absolute option: he may demand payment from the principal, the guarantor, or both together. This is because kafalah is built on inclusion (dam), meaning the guarantor's liability is additional, not substitutional. The principal's liability remains unless the condition of discharge (bara'ah) is stipulated, in which case it becomes hawala (transfer of debt).

Third, kafalah is valid whether the principal commands it or not. If the principal commands it, the guarantor has recourse on him after payment, recovering exactly what he guaranteed, not necessarily what he paid. For example, if he guaranteed good coins and paid bad coins with the creditor's consent, he recovers good coins. The text distinguishes this from a loan where the payer recovers according to what is owed. The guarantor's recourse is conditional on the principal being legally capable of commanding (not a minor or slave under guardianship). Even if the commander is incapable, the kafalah itself is valid because it depends on the guarantor's own undertaking.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A contract whereby a person (guarantor) undertakes to fulfill an obligation owed by another (principal) to a creditor.
الحوالة (al-hawala): Transfer of debt from one person to another.
الضم (al-damm): Inclusion; the principle that kafalah adds liability without removing the original.
الخيار (al-khiyar): Option or choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes kafalah from hawala regarding the principal's liability?
A) In kafalah, the principal is discharged; in hawala, he is not.
B) In kafalah, the principal remains liable; in hawala, he is discharged unless stipulated otherwise.
C) Both discharge the principal.
D) Neither discharges the principal.

Key Takeaway
The creditor's option to demand from any party is a direct result of kafalah being built on inclusion, not transfer.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Recourse of the Guarantor Against the Principal
Commentary on the guarantor's right of recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for the guarantor's recourse after payment, distinguish it from a loan, and identify conditions that affect the right of recourse.

Core Idea
The guarantor recovers what he guaranteed, not what he paid, and only if the principal commanded him.

After paying the debt, the guarantor has the right to recover from the principal. However, this right is not absolute. The amount recovered is based on the guarantee, not the actual payment made, and the principal must have been legally capable of commanding the suretyship. This lesson explains these nuances and contrasts the guarantor's recourse with that of a person who pays a debt without suretyship.

Guided Path
1
Establish the condition for recourse
The guarantor has recourse only if the principal commanded him (amara). If the guarantor acted without command, he has no recourse.
Kafalah is valid without command, but recourse depends on it.
2
Determine the amount of recourse
The guarantor recovers what he guaranteed (diman), not necessarily what he actually paid. For example, if he guaranteed good coins but paid bad coins with consent, he recovers good coins.
This is because his right is based on the guarantee, not the payment.
3
Understand the rationale: ownership of the debt
By paying, the guarantor owns the debt (tamluk al-dayn), like a creditor. He then claims from the principal as a creditor would.
This differs from a loan where the payer is a lender and recovers exactly what was paid.
4
Identify limitations on the principal's capacity
The principal must be legally capable of commanding (e.g., not a minor or slave under guardianship). If incapable, the suretyship is still valid, but the guarantor cannot have recourse.
The validity of kafalah depends on the guarantor's undertaking, not the principal's command.

[٣] قوله : رجع عليه بعد أدائه ؛ بما ضمنه سواء أدّى ما ضمنه أو أدى خلافه، حتى لو كفيل بألف جياد وأدّى ألفاً زيوفاً برضاء الطالب رجع بالجياد، ولو كفل بألف زيوف وأدى جياداً رجع بالزيوف.
أمَّا رجوعه على الآمر ؛ فلأنه أدَّى دينه بأمرِه فيرجعُ به عليه، وأما بما ضمنه ؛ فلأنَّ رجوعه بحكم الكفالة، فكان بما دخل تحتها؛ ولأنَّ الكفيل ملك الدِّينَ بالأداء، فكان بمنزلة الطَّالب، وصار كما لو ملكه بهبة أو إرث.
بخلاف المأمور بأداء الدين، حيث يرجعُ بما أدّى إن أدى أردأ من الدين، وبالدين إن أدى أجود منه؛ لأنه لم يجب عليه شيء حتى يملك الدين بالأداء، بل كان مقرضاً، وبخلاف ما إذا صالح على أقل من الدين، وهو من جنسيه، حيث لا يرجع إلا بقدر ما أدّى إلا إذا صالحه على أن يهبه الباقي ففعل، فحئنذ بجميعه؛ لأنه ملك الدِّينَ كلّه أو بعضه بالأداء، وبعضه بالهبة. ورجوع الكفيل على الأصيل ليس على إطلاقه، بل هو مقيد بما إذا كان الأمر ممن يجوز إقراره على نفسه بالديون، ويملك التبرع حتى لو كان صبياً محجوراً، أو عبداً محجوراً، وأمر رجلاً بأن يكفل عنه، صحت الكفالة؛ لأن صحتها تعتمد التزام الكفيل باختياره، لا أمر الأمر.
The cream of the conclusion for the mainstay of care. The creditor has the right to demand payment from whomever he wishes from his principal and his guarantor, and to demand from both. If he demands from one of them, he may demand from the other. That is, if he does not provide evidence, the guarantor is believed regarding the amount he admits, and he must swear to negate the increase. It is recommended that he swear upon knowledge: 'You do not know that more than this is owed by the principal.' If he becomes surety or admits to the excess, it is binding upon him. He only swears upon knowledge because swearing regarding what is owed by another is only upon knowledge. And if the principal admits to more than what the guarantor admitted, that is confined to him because admission is a limited proof. The word 'what' in his saying 'regarding what he admits' is a relative pronoun, and the pronoun in 'bi-hi' refers to 'what'. His saying 'in what he admits more than it' is a verbal noun: meaning the principal is believed in his admission of more, i.e., more than what the guarantor admits. If it were made a relative pronoun, the meaning would be corrupted because then the estimate of the speech would be: 'the principal is believed in the thing which he admits more than it', i.e., more than that thing. The thing which the principal admits more than it is what the guarantor admitted, and the purpose is that the principal is believed in the excess, not that he is believed in what the guarantor admitted. (And the creditor has the right to demand from whomever he wishes from his principal and his guarantor, and to demand from both. If he demands from one of them, he may demand from the other.) This is unlike the owner if he chooses one of the two usurpers. The statement 'the guarantor is believed' etc.; because he denies the excess claimed by the creditor, and the statement of the denier is accepted with his oath. If the debtor admits to more than what the guarantor admitted, his statement does not affect the guarantor due to the lack of authority over him, because admission against another is not effective except through authority. And this is unlike the case where one says: 'What is owed to you by so-and-so is upon me,' and he admits against himself a thousand, for example, and the guarantor denies what he admitted, where what the debtor admitted is binding upon him by istihsan, while by qiyas nothing would be binding on him. (2) His statement: 'and the creditor...' etc.; meaning the creditor has the option in demanding payment: if he wishes he demands the principal, if he wishes he demands the guarantor, and if he wishes he demands both together; because that is the requirement of kafalah, since it is based on inclusion (dam), as previously established, and that requires the continuation of the first liability, not discharge, unless he stipulates the discharge of the principal, in which case it becomes hawala, just as hawala with the condition of non-discharge of the transferor is kafalah; because the consideration in contracts is for meanings, not merely for wording. The book of kafalah: its definition, conditions, and rulings. And it is valid with and without the command of the principal. Then if he commanded him, he has recourse on him. Because his choosing one of them entails his ownership of it. Meaning when the judge rules accordingly. Such is in the Mabsut of Shaykh al-Islam. So when he owns one of them, it is not possible for him to own the other. (And it is valid with and without the command of the principal, then if he commanded him, he has recourse on him.) (1) His statement: 'entails' etc.; meaning it entails ownership from him when the judge rules it, and he cannot give ownership to the other after that. And demanding payment in kafalah only necessitates that as long as actual satisfaction has not occurred from him. (2) His statement: 'and it is valid' etc.; because it is an act of the guarantor regarding himself by undertaking that the creditor will demand from him, and there is no harm to the principal in that. (3) His statement: 'he has recourse on him after payment' for what he guaranteed, whether he paid what he guaranteed or paid something else. So if he guaranteed one thousand good coins and paid one thousand counterfeit coins with the creditor's consent, he has recourse in good coins. And if he guaranteed one thousand counterfeit coins and paid good coins, he has recourse in counterfeit coins. As for his recourse on the commander, it is because he paid his debt by his command, so he has recourse on him. And as for what he guaranteed, it is because his recourse is by the ruling of kafalah, so it is for what falls under it; and because the guarantor owns the debt by payment, so he is like the creditor, and it becomes as if he owned it by gift or inheritance. Unlike the one commanded to pay the debt, who has recourse only for what he paid if he paid inferior to the debt, and for the debt if he paid superior; because nothing is owed to him until he owns the debt by payment, rather he was a lender. And unlike the case where he settles for less than the debt, and it is of the same type, where he only has recourse for what he paid unless he settles on condition that the creditor remits the remainder and he does so, then he has recourse in full; because he owned the debt wholly or partially by payment, and partially by gift. And the recourse of the guarantor against the principal is not absolute, but is conditioned upon the command being from someone whose admission of debts is valid and who is allowed to donate. So if a minor under guardianship, or a slave under guardianship, commands a man to stand surety for him, the suretyship is valid because its validity depends on the guarantor's undertaking voluntarily, not on the command of the commander.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the rulings of kafalah (suretyship) from the Hanafi matn. It covers three main areas: (1) the believability of the guarantor and principal regarding the amount owed, (2) the creditor's option to demand payment from either party, and (3) the validity of kafalah with or without the principal's command and the guarantor's recourse (ruju'). 

First, regarding admission: When the creditor claims an amount, and the guarantor admits a lesser amount, the guarantor is believed on oath (qawl al-munkar) because he is the denier of the excess. He swears that he does not know that more is owed. If the principal admits a greater amount, that admission only binds the principal himself (iqrar qasir), not the guarantor. An exception is a special case where someone says 'What is owed to you by so-and-so is on me' and then admits a specific amount; by istihsan, that admission binds him even if he denies the same in another context. The text clarifies that the general rule is qiyas, but istihsan is applied here.

Second, the creditor has an absolute option: he may demand payment from the principal, the guarantor, or both together. This is because kafalah is built on inclusion (dam), meaning the guarantor's liability is additional, not substitutional. The principal's liability remains unless the condition of discharge (bara'ah) is stipulated, in which case it becomes hawala (transfer of debt).

Third, kafalah is valid whether the principal commands it or not. If the principal commands it, the guarantor has recourse on him after payment, recovering exactly what he guaranteed, not necessarily what he paid. For example, if he guaranteed good coins and paid bad coins with the creditor's consent, he recovers good coins. The text distinguishes this from a loan where the payer recovers according to what is owed. The guarantor's recourse is conditional on the principal being legally capable of commanding (not a minor or slave under guardianship). Even if the commander is incapable, the kafalah itself is valid because it depends on the guarantor's own undertaking.

Key Terms
الرجوع (al-ruju'): The right of the guarantor to recover from the principal after paying the debt.
الضمان (al-daman): The guarantee; what the guarantor undertakes to pay.
المأمور بأداء الدين (al-ma'mur bi al-ada'): A person ordered to pay a debt, not as a guarantor but as an agent or lender.
تمليك الدين (tamluk al-dayn): Ownership of the debt, acquired by the guarantor through payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A guarantor guarantees 1000 silver coins but pays 1000 gold coins (which are more valuable) with the creditor's consent. How much can he recover from the principal?
A) 1000 gold coins.
B) 1000 silver coins.
C) The market value of the gold coins.
D) Nothing, because he paid more than guaranteed.

Key Takeaway
The guarantor's recourse is based on the guarantee, not the payment; it requires the principal's valid command and legal capacity.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
6 min
Guarantor's Recourse: Conditions and Measure of Payment
Commentary on kafalah rulings regarding guarantor's recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a guarantor has recourse against the principal and determine the amount recoverable.

Core Idea
The guarantor recovers the guaranteed amount, not what he actually paid, and recourse depends on the principal's order and capacity.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rulings on a guarantor's right to claim reimbursement from the principal after paying the creditor. You will learn when recourse is valid, what amount is recoverable, and how the guarantor's payment transfers ownership of the debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
Kafalah is valid with or without the principal's order, but recourse requires the order. Without order, the guarantor is a volunteer (mutabarri') and cannot claim reimbursement.
The matn states: 'If he ordered him, he has recourse; if not, he does not.'
2
Understand the measure of recourse
The guarantor recovers the guaranteed amount (ma daman), not necessarily what he actually paid. Example: guaranteeing fine dirhams but paying counterfeit, he recovers fine dirhams.
Contrast with a simple agent (ma'mur bi ada' al-dayn) who recovers only what he paid.
3
Recognize exceptions to recourse
If the principal lacks legal capacity (minor/slave under guardianship), the guarantee is valid but recourse is not. Also, if the principal pays the creditor first and the guarantor pays unknowingly, no recourse.
Also, ratification after the session does not create recourse; only ratification in the session does.
4
Apply the concept of mulazamah
If the creditor pursues the guarantor, the guarantor may pursue (mulazamah) the principal, but only if the guarantee was by the principal's order and the guarantor does not owe a similar debt to the principal.
Mulazamah is an intense demand, potentially including imprisonment.
5
Distinguish from similar contracts
Contrast kafalah with hawala (transfer of debt) and with a simple loan/agency. The guarantor owns the debt by payment, becoming like the original creditor.
This ownership by payment is the basis for recovering the guaranteed amount, not the actual payment.

Source Text

Then, if he ordered him, he (the guarantor) has recourse against him (the principal) after his payment to his creditor, and he cannot demand from him before it. And if he did not order him, he does not have recourse. And if the guarantor is pursued (mulazim) for the money, he may pursue (mulazama) his principal. Because choosing one of them entails giving ownership to him. Meaning: if the judge so rules... As in the Mabsut of Shaykh al-Islam. So when he owns one, he cannot make the other own it. (Kafalah is valid with the order of the principal and without his order, then if he ordered him, he has recourse against him.) And the claim in kafalah does not necessitate it as long as actual collection does not occur from him. (2) Comment: His saying 'and it is valid...' etc.: because it is an act of the guarantor regarding himself by undertaking that the creditor will claim from him, and there is no harm on the principal in that. [1] Comment: His saying 'after his payment, not before': because he only becomes owner by payment, and before payment he has no ownership. And he specified 'his payment' because if the guarantor pays the beneficiary after the principal's payment without knowing, he does not have recourse against him, as they explicitly stated. [2] Comment: His saying 'and if he did not order him, he does not have recourse': i.e., if the principal did not order the guarantor, he does not have recourse against him for what he paid; because he is a volunteer (mutabarri') in his payment. And the generality of the expression includes the case where he guaranteed without his order and then he (the principal) ratified it after the session; because the kafalah became binding on him, and it was executed without an order that does not cause recourse, so it does not become a cause for recourse. As in al-Kafi. And this is if he ratified after the session. But if he ratified in the session, then it becomes a cause for recourse. As in al-Fusul al-Imadiyya. [3] Comment: His saying 'he may pursue...' etc.: i.e., the guarantor may pursue (mulazama) the principal (al-makful 'anhu) until the principal releases the guarantor; because the principal is the one who put him in this situation, so he must release him from it. And this ruling is restricted to when the kafalah was by his order; otherwise not, because he did not put him in it to release him. And it is restricted in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj also to when the guarantor does not have a debt similar to the one claimed from the principal; otherwise, there is no mulazama or imprisonment. And mulazama originally means intense demand. It is said: so-and-so is mulazim for so-and-so, i.e., he accompanies him with an inseparable companionship.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This is a commentary passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on the rulings of kafalah (suretyship). The main text (matn) states that kafalah is valid whether the guarantor acts with or without the principal's order. However, the guarantor's right of recourse (ruju') against the principal depends on the order: if the principal ordered the guarantee, the guarantor, after paying the debt, can recover from the principal the exact amount guaranteed—not necessarily what he actually paid. For example, if he guaranteed one thousand fine coins but paid one thousand counterfeit coins with the creditor's consent, he recovers the fine coins. The commentary explains that the guarantor owns the debt by payment, becoming like the original creditor. This differs from a simple agent ordered to pay a debt, who recovers only what he paid. The passage then qualifies the recourse: the principal must be legally capable of taking debts and making gifts (not a minor or slave under guardianship). If an incapable person orders a guarantee, the guarantee is still valid (since it depends on the guarantor's undertaking), but the guarantor has no recourse. Furthermore, the guarantor cannot demand payment from the principal before he himself pays the creditor, because he only owns the debt upon payment. If the creditor pressures the guarantor for payment, the guarantor can in turn pressure (mulazama) the principal, but only if the guarantee was by the principal's order and the guarantor does not owe a similar debt to the principal. The commentary stresses that this is a continuous pursuit until the principal frees the guarantor.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A contract of guarantee where a person (kafil) undertakes to be liable for a debt or obligation of another (asil/makful anhu) to a creditor (makful lahu).
الرجوع (al-ruju'): The guarantor's right to claim reimbursement from the principal after paying the guaranteed debt.
الملازمة (al-mulazamah): The guarantor's right to pursue the principal intensively, including through legal means, until the principal frees the guarantor from the debt.
المأمور بأداء الدين (al-ma'mur bi ada' al-dayn): A person ordered to pay another's debt, acting as an agent/lender, not a guarantor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a guarantor (kafil) guarantees one thousand fine (jiyad) dirhams but pays the creditor one thousand counterfeit (zuyuf) dirhams with the creditor's consent, how much does the guarantor recover from the principal?
A) One thousand fine dirhams
B) One thousand counterfeit dirhams
C) The actual amount paid, i.e., the counterfeit dirhams
D) Nothing, because the payment was different

Key Takeaway
The guarantor's right of recourse is not measured by what he paid but by the amount guaranteed, and it is conditional on the principal's order and legal capacity.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate

Surety's Right to Imprison the Principal Debtor
Ruling on surety's right to imprison principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the surety's right to imprison the principal, identify the restriction when the principal is an ascendant of the creditor, and apply the reasoning (ta'lil) to similar cases.

Core Idea
A surety imprisoned for the principal's debt may imprison the principal, except when the principal is the creditor's ascendant.

In the Hanafi school of Kafalah (suretyship), if a surety is imprisoned by the creditor for the principal's debt, the surety has the right to imprison the principal in return. This reciprocal right is based on rational equity: the principal caused the harm. However, this right is restricted when the principal is an ascendant (parent or grandparent) of the creditor, to avoid harming the ascendant. This lesson examines the ruling, its proof, and the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the restriction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Read the first part: 'If he (the surety) is imprisoned, he has the right to imprison him (the principal).' Recognize this as the default ruling.
The default ruling is permissibility (ibahah).
2
Analyze the proof (ta'lil)
The text gives the proof: 'because this harm befell him by his command, so he treats him in like manner.' This is a rational argument (dalil aqli) based on fairness and reciprocal harm.
The proof is a form of qiyas: the surety is harmed by the principal's action, so he may reciprocate.
3
Examine the restriction
Note the restriction from al-Sharnablaliyya: the surety cannot imprison the principal if the principal is an ascendant of the creditor. Understand the rationale: harming the ascendant is prohibited.
This restriction is a form of takhsis (specification) of the general ruling.
4
Consider the objection and resolution
Abu al-Su'ud objects based on a report from al-Khulasa that the principal is not imprisoned. The author resolves by favoring the restriction, citing al-Tahtawi. Follow this reasoning to see how the mufta bihi is determined.
The objection is that if the surety is imprisoned for the principal's debt, why can't the surety imprison the principal? The response: the restriction overrides the general ruling in this specific case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن حيس فله حبسه : لأَنَّهُ لحقهُ هذا الضَّرر بأمره فيعامله بمثله. (وإن أبرئ الأصيل أو أوفى المالَ بَرِئ الكفيل، وإن أبرئ هو لا يَبْرَوْ الأصيل ) ؛ لأنَّ الدِّينَ على الأصيل، فالبراءة عنه تُوجب البراءة عن المطالبة بخلاف العكس.
[۱] قوله : وإن حبس فله حبسه ؛ قيَّده في «الشرنبلالية) بما إذا لم يكن المكفول عنه من أصول الدائن ، فإذا كان المدين أصلاً لا يحبس كفيله ولا يلازم، لما يلزم من فعل ذلك بالأصيل، وهو ممتنع. انتهى. ونظر فيه أبو السعود) بـأنَّ الـذي في عبارة القُهستاني (۳) عن (قضاء) «الخلاصة»: إنَّ الكفيل إذا حبس لا يحبس أصلُ الطَّالب الذي هو مكفول عنه. انتهى. وفيه أنه كيف يحبس الكفيل بسبب دين الأصيل، ولا يحبس الأصيل فيه، وأيضاً (٤) ما المانع من الملازمة على فرض تسليم عدم الحبس، فالوجه ما في «الشرنبلالية ) ، ذكره شيخنا العلامةُ الطِّحْطَاوي في حاشية على الدر المختار).
And if he (the surety) is imprisoned, he has the right to imprison him (the principal): because this harm befell him by his (the principal's) command, so he treats him in like manner. (And if the principal is released or the debt is paid, the surety is released; but if the surety is released, the principal is not released) because the debt is upon the principal, so release from him necessitates release from the claim, unlike the opposite. [1] His saying: 'And if he is imprisoned, he has the right to imprison him' is restricted in al-Sharnablaliyya to when the guaranteed person is not from the creditor's ascendants; for if the debtor is an ancestor, his surety is not imprisoned nor is he seized, because that would entail harm to the principal, which is prohibited. End. And Abu al-Su'ud objected to it, saying that in the expression of al-Quhustani from the Qada of al-Khulasa: If the surety is imprisoned, the principal creditor who is the guaranteed person is not imprisoned. End. And in it is how can the surety be imprisoned for the principal's debt, and the principal is not imprisoned? Also, what prevents seizure assuming no imprisonment? So the correct view is what is in al-Sharnablaliyya, mentioned by our teacher al-Allama al-Tahtawi in his gloss on al-Durr al-Mukhtar.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the chapter of Kafalah: if the surety is imprisoned by the creditor for the debt, the surety has the right to imprison the principal debtor. The reasoning is that the principal caused this harm to the surety by asking him to guarantee, so the surety may retaliate in kind. However, the commentator al-Sharnablaliyya restricts this right: if the principal is an ascendant (parent or grandparent) of the creditor, the surety cannot imprison him because it would harm the ascendant, which is forbidden. There is a discussion: Abu al-Su'ud objects based on a report from al-Khulasa that the principal is not imprisoned even if the surety is. But the author sides with al-Sharnablaliyya, noting that the surety's imprisonment is due to the principal's debt, so it is fair to allow the surety to imprison him. This difference is practical: under the restricted view, a surety for a parent cannot imprison them, while under the unrestricted view he could. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) appears to be the restricted view, as it is mentioned by al-Tahtawi.

Key Terms
حبس (habs): Imprisonment by the creditor for debt; here, the surety's right to imprison the principal in return.
كفيل (kafil): The surety or guarantor who takes responsibility for the debt of another (the principal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the text, when does the surety NOT have the right to imprison the principal?
A) When the principal is a minor
B) When the principal is an ascendant of the creditor
C) When the debt is less than the surety's assets
D) When the surety is imprisoned for his own debt

Key Takeaway
The surety may imprison the principal for recourse, except when the principal is the creditor's ascendant.

When the Surety is Released: The Rule and Its Exception
Ruling on release of surety upon release of principal or payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a surety is released and identify the exception where the surety remains liable despite the principal's release.

Core Idea
Releasing the principal or paying the debt frees the surety, but releasing the surety does not free the principal—unless the guarantee was a hawala.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi Kafalah: the effect of ibra' (release) and payment on the surety's liability. The text establishes a clear asymmetry: freeing the principal frees the surety, but freeing the surety does not free the principal. It also introduces a critical exception when the guarantee was actually a hawala (transfer of debt). Understanding this ruling prevents errors in financial transactions and legal opinions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
Determine the central acts: the creditor's ibra' of the principal or the principal's payment of the debt.
Both acts extinguish the debt.
2
State the Ruling
The surety is released (bari'a) when the principal is released or the debt is paid.
This is the general rule.
3
Follow the Rational Proof
The debt is owed by the principal. Releasing the principal removes the creditor's claim, so the surety's contingent liability vanishes. Conversely, releasing the surety does not affect the debt itself.
Rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the nature of the debt.
4
Recognize the Exception
If the guarantee was made with the condition that the principal be released (i.e., it is actually a hawala), then releasing the principal does not free the surety.
In hawala, the surety becomes the primary debtor; release of the original principal does not discharge the transfer.
5
Apply the Example from al-Khaniya
If the surety proves the principal paid the debt before the guarantee, only the principal is freed (the guarantee was invalid ab initio). If payment was after, both are freed.
This illustrates how the timing of payment affects the surety's release.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أبرئ الأصيل أو أوفى المالَ بَرِئ الكفيل، وإن أبرئ هو لا يَبْرَوْ الأصيل ) ؛ لأنَّ الدِّينَ على الأصيل، فالبراءة عنه تُوجب البراءة عن المطالبة بخلاف العكس.
[٢] قوله : وإن أبرئ... الخ ؛ محل براءة الكفيل بإبراء الطالب الأصيل، إذا لم يكفل بشرط براءة الأصيل، فإذا كان كذلك برأ الأصيل دون الكفيل ؛ لأنها صارت حوالة ، وإنما قال : أبرئ الأصيل، ولم يقل : برئ الأصيل ؛ لأنه لا يلزم من براءته براءته لما في «الخانية (٦) : ضمن له الفاعل فلان فَبَرْهَنَ فلانٌ على أنه كان قضاه إياها قبل الكفالة ، فإنه يبرأ الأصيل دون الكفيل، ولو برهن أنه قضاء بعدها يبرءان. انتهى. فقد برأ الأصيل في الوجه الأول فقط.
And if the principal is released or the debt is paid, the surety is released; but if the surety is released, the principal is not released because the debt is upon the principal, so release from him necessitates release from the claim, unlike the opposite. [2] His saying: 'And if released...' etc. The place of the surety's release by the creditor's release of the principal is when he did not guarantee with the condition of the principal's release. If it is such, the principal is released without the surety because it becomes a hawala (transfer). He said 'the principal is released' and did not say 'the principal is released' because his release does not follow from his release. For in al-Khaniya: 'He guaranteed for so-and-so the doer, and so-and-so proved that he had paid it before the guarantee, then the principal is released without the surety; if he proves payment after it, both are released.' End. So the principal is released only in the first case.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage establishes a key asymmetry in Kafalah: if the creditor releases the principal from the debt, the surety is automatically freed because the debt is extinguished. Similarly, if the principal pays the debt, the surety is freed. However, if the creditor releases only the surety, the principal remains liable; the debt is still owed by him. The commentator adds a nuance: if the surety contracted the guarantee with the condition that the principal would be released (i.e., the surety was only a transfer of liability, hawala), then releasing the principal does not free the surety. The commentator also clarifies that the matn says 'if the principal is released' (a sound passive verb), not 'the principal becomes released' (a stative verb), because the principal's release does not necessarily follow from his release in all cases. For example, if the surety produces evidence that the principal had paid the debt before the guarantee, then the principal is released but not the surety (because the surety guaranteed an already-paid debt, which is invalid). But if the payment was after the guarantee, both are released. This shows that the surety's release depends on the actual existence of the debt at the time of guarantee.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibra'): Release or discharge from a debt by the creditor; here, of the principal or the surety.
حوالة (hawala): A transfer of debt from one person to another; used here to describe when a guarantee is actually a hawala if the surety's liability is conditional on the principal's release.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which case does the surety NOT go free when the principal is released?
A) When the surety proves the debt was paid before the guarantee
B) When the surety proves the debt was paid after the guarantee
C) When the principal is an ascendant of the creditor
D) When the surety is also the creditor

Key Takeaway
The surety is freed only when the principal is released or pays; releasing the surety does not affect the principal's liability, except in a hawala.

Postponement of the Principal's Debt and Its Effect on Surety
Ruling on postponement's effect on surety with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi ruling that postponement of the principal's debt automatically postpones the surety's obligation, and EXPLAIN the asymmetry between principal and surety using the analogy of temporary vs permanent release.

Core Idea
Postponing the principal automatically extends to the surety, but not vice versa.

In Hanafi kafalah, the surety's obligation follows the principal's. If the creditor grants the principal more time, the surety also gets that time automatically. But if the creditor grants the surety more time alone, the principal remains liable immediately. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains this asymmetry and the reasoning behind it, using the concept of temporary release.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on postponement
State the asymmetry: postponing the principal benefits the surety, but not vice versa.
The text says: 'If he postpones from the principal, it is postponed from him (the surety).'
2
Understand the legal reasoning (talil)
The ruling is based on analogy (qiyas) of temporary release (ibra' mu'aqqat) to permanent release (ibra' mu'abbad).
The creditor's postponement is considered a temporary release from the immediate duty to pay.
3
Explore the condition of acceptance
The principal must accept the postponement; rejection may cause it to revert.
From al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: acceptance is a condition.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
There are two opinions on whether the debt returns to the surety if the principal rejects.
One view: debt returns; other: it does not.
5
Apply the distinction between temporary and permanent release
Permanent release is a dropping of right (irrevocable); temporary release is a deferment (revocable by rejection).
Al-Kifayah explains that the surety's rejection can nullify a temporary release but not a permanent one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أخر عن الأصيل تأخر عنه بخلاف عكسه، اعتباراً للإبراء المؤقت بالمؤبد.
(وإن أخر عن الأصيل تأخر عنه بخلاف عكسه) اعتباراً للإبراء المؤقت بالمؤبد. ... وفي «السراج الوهاج»: ويشترط قبول الأصيل البراءة، فإن ردها ارتدت، وهل يعود الدين على الكفيل؟ فيه قولان. وموت الأصيل كقبوله. وفي «النهاية»: إن إبراء الأصيل وتأجيله يرتدان بالرد، وبراء الكفيل يرتد بالرد، وأما تأجيله فلا يرتد بالرد. انتهى.
[١] قوله : وإن أخر ...الخ ؛ حاصله : أنَّ الطالب إذا أَخَّر عن الأصيل فهو تأخير عن الكفيل، وإذا أخر عن الكفيل لم يكن تأخيراً عن الذي عليه الأصيل ؛ لأنَّ التأخير إبراء مؤقت ؛ لكونه إسقاط المطالبة إلى غاية، فيعتبر بالإبراء المؤبد فيما بين الأصيل والكفيل.
فأما الإبراء المؤقت والمؤيَّدُ يفترقان في حق الكفيل، فإنَّ الإبراء المؤبد للكفيل لا يرتد برده ، وأما الإبراء المؤقت يرتد بردّه ، ويكون الدين عليه حالاً ؛ لأنّ الإبراء المؤبد إسقاط في حقه، والإسقاط لا يرتد بالرد. وأما الإبراء المؤقت فهو تأخير المطالبة ، وليس بإسقاط ، ألا ترى أنَّ المطالبة تعود بعد الأجل، والتأخيرُ قابلُ الإبطال، بخلافِ الإسقاط المحض، كذا في «الكفاية») ، وغيرها.
And if he postpones (the debt) from the principal, it is postponed from him (the surety) contrary to the opposite, by analogy of temporary release to permanent release. ... And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: 'The principal's acceptance of the release is a condition; if he rejects it, it returns. And does the debt return upon the surety? There are two sayings. And the principal's death is like his acceptance.' And in al-Nihaya: 'Indeed the release and postponement of the principal return upon rejection, and the release of the surety returns upon rejection, but as for his postponement, it does not return upon rejection.' End. [1] His saying: 'And if he postpones...' etc. Its summary: If the creditor postpones for the principal, it is a postponement for the surety; if he postpones for the surety, it is not a postponement for the principal, because postponement is a temporary release, being a suspension of the claim until a term, so it is treated like permanent release between the principal and surety. As for temporary and permanent release, they differ concerning the surety: permanent release of the surety does not return upon his rejection, but temporary release returns upon his rejection, and the debt becomes due upon him, because permanent release is a dropping of right in his regard, and dropping does not return upon rejection. Temporary release is a deferment of the claim, not a dropping; do you not see that the claim returns after the term, and deferment is subject to nullification, unlike pure dropping. Thus in al-Kifayah and others.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of postponement (ta'khir) of the debt by the creditor. If the creditor grants a postponement to the principal debtor, that postponement also benefits the surety: the surety cannot be sued until the new term expires. However, if the creditor postpones solely for the surety, it does not affect the principal; the principal remains obligated to pay immediately. The reasoning given is that postponement is like a temporary release from the obligation to pay; it is similar to permanent release by analogy. The text then discusses the return of release or postponement if the principal rejects it. According to al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, the principal must accept the release for it to be effective; if he rejects, it returns. There is a dispute about whether the debt returns on the surety: two opinions. Also, the principal's death is equivalent to acceptance. The text from al-Nihaya distinguishes: if the creditor releases the principal or postpones, and the principal rejects, both the release and postponement return (i.e., are undone). If the creditor releases the surety, and the surety rejects, that release also returns. But if the creditor merely postpones for the surety, and the surety rejects, the postponement does not return; it remains in effect? Actually, the text says 'his postponement does not return upon rejection' meaning the surety's rejection does not nullify the postponement granted to him? The commentator reconciles: permanent release (ibra' mu'abbad) is a dropping of right, which cannot be undone by rejection; temporary release (ibra' mu'aqqat, i.e., postponement) is not a dropping but a deferment, so it can be nullified by rejection only in the case of the principal? The commentary says temporary release of the surety returns upon rejection, but then al-Nihaya says the surety's postponement does not return. There is a nuance: 'temporary release' (ibra' mu'aqqat) might be different from 'postponement' (ta'khir). The commentator from al-Kifayah explains that permanent release is a drop that cannot return; temporary release is a deferment that can be nullified. However, the text from al-Nihaya says the surety's postponement does not return, meaning if the creditor gives the surety extra time, the surety cannot reject it to make the debt due immediately? This seems contradictory. The likely interpretation: 'temporary release' (ibra' mu'aqqat) in the commentary refers to a conditional release that ends after a period, while 'postponement' (ta'khir) is a simple delay. The text from al-Nihaya says the surety's postponement does not return upon rejection, meaning if the creditor postpones for the surety and the surety rejects, the postponement still stands (i.e., the creditor cannot demand immediate payment) because the surety's acceptance is not needed for a postponement; it is a unilateral act. But the surety's permanent release does require acceptance? The text is complex. For the student, the key point is the asymmetry: postponing for the principal automatically applies to the surety, but not vice versa. The additional discussion on rejection reflects different views within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
تأخير (ta'khir): Postponement or deferment of the debt for a specified period; a temporary release from the obligation to pay immediately.
إبراء مؤقت (ibra' mu'aqqat): Temporary release from the debt; the creditor suspends the right to demand payment until a future time.
إبراء مؤبد (ibra' mu'abbad): Permanent release; the debt is extinguished and the right to demand payment is dropped forever.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what happens if the creditor postpones the debt for the principal?
A) The surety must pay immediately
B) The surety also gets the postponement
C) The postponement is void unless the surety accepts
D) The principal is freed from the debt

Key Takeaway
Postponement of the principal's debt automatically postpones the surety's obligation; this asymmetry is based on treating temporary release as analogous to permanent release between principal and surety.
