Settlement by Surety and Right of Recourse
Ruling on settlement and recourse in kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a surety's settlement with the creditor releases both parties and when the surety can recoup the amount from the principal.

Core Idea
A settlement by a surety releases both parties; recourse requires prior authorization from the principal.

In this passage, the Hanafi jurists explain the effect of a settlement (sulh) between the surety and the creditor. The key issue is whether the surety can later recover what he paid from the principal debtor. The ruling clarifies that both parties are freed from the original debt, but the surety's right of recourse depends on the principal's authorization.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the ruling
Identify the situation: a surety settles with the creditor by paying a lesser amount to discharge a larger debt. The ruling is that both the surety and the principal are released from the debt.
2
Identify the legal reasoning (talil)
The settlement is connected to the original debt which is due from the principal. Since the principal is released, the surety's obligation as a dependent guarantee also ceases.
The principle: freeing the principal frees the surety.
3
Analyze the condition for surety's recourse
The surety can recoup the amount he paid from the principal only if he guaranteed with the principal's order (authorization). Without authorization, the surety bears the loss.
This is a key condition for recourse.
4
Contrast with ibra' (release without payment)
If the surety merely releases the creditor from the debt (ibra') without paying, he cannot claim from the principal because the debt is extinguished, not transferred.
Payment transfers the claim; ibra' extinguishes it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن صالح الكفيل الطالب عن ألفي على مئة برى الكفيل والأصيل، ورَجَعَ على الأصيل بها إن كَفِلَ بأمرِه) ؛ لأنَّهُ أضاف الصُّلْحَ إلى الألف الذي هو الدين، وهو على الأصيل، فيبرؤ عن تسعمئة ، وبراءته توجب براءة الكفيل، فإن كانت الكفالة بأمره ، رجع الكفيل بما أدى ، وهو المئة.
...
[٢]قوله : فإن صالح الكفيل ؛ وإن صالح الأصيل، والمسألة بحالها فالحكم كذلك أيضاً ؛ لأنه قد برئ بالصلح ، وبراءته توجب براءة الكفيل كما سبق.
(۳) قوله : رجع ؛ إذ بالأداء يملك ما في ذمَّةِ الأصيل، فاستوجب الرجوع بخلاف الإبراء ؛ لأنَّ بالإبراء يسقط الدَّينُ فلا يملكه الكفيل، فلا يرجع.
If the surety settles with the creditor for two thousand for one hundred, the surety and the principal are released, and he (the surety) goes back on the principal for it (the hundred) if he guaranteed by his (the principal's) order. Because he (the surety) connected the settlement to the thousand which is the debt, and it is upon the principal, so he (the principal) is released from nine hundred, and his release necessitates the surety's release. If the guarantee was by his order, the surety recurs with what he paid, which is the hundred. ... [2] His saying: 'If the surety settles...' And if the principal settles, the case is the same, the ruling is similarly because he (the principal) has been released by the settlement, and his release necessitates the surety's release as before. (3) His saying: 'He goes back' because by paying, he comes to own what is in the principal's liability, so he becomes entitled to recourse, unlike release (ibra'), because by release the debt is dropped, so the surety does not own it, and thus does not recur.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where the surety reaches a settlement (sulh) with the creditor to pay a lesser amount (e.g., pays 100 instead of 1000) to discharge the debt. The ruling: both the principal and the surety are released from the debt. The principal is released from the remaining 900 because the settlement is considered as extinguishing the debt partially. And since the principal is released, the surety is also released (as established earlier). The surety may then claim the amount he paid (100) from the principal, but only if the surety had acted with the principal's authorization (i.e., the principal asked him to guarantee). The commentator adds that if the principal himself settles with the creditor, the same applies: the surety is freed. The reason the surety can recoup his payment is that by paying the creditor, he acquires the creditor's right against the principal, which is a form of subrogation. However, if the surety merely releases the creditor from the debt (ibra') without paying, he cannot recoup from the principal because the debt is simply dropped; the surety does not acquire any claim. This distinction underscores that repayment (ada') transfers ownership of the claim, while ibra' extinguishes it.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A settlement agreement between the surety and the creditor to pay a reduced amount to discharge the debt.
رجوع (ruju'): Recourse; the surety's right to claim the amount paid from the principal debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition can the surety recoup the amount paid in a settlement from the principal?
A) If the settlement was agreed by the principal
B) If the surety guaranteed with the principal's order
C) If the settlement was for less than half the debt
D) If the creditor also releases the principal

Key Takeaway
A surety's settlement releases both parties, but recourse requires the principal's prior authorization; payment transfers the claim while ibra' extinguishes it.

How Deferment Affects Surety and Principal: Temporary vs Permanent Release
Ruling on deferment in kafalah with proof by analogy and condition of acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on deferment in kafalah and explain the distinction between temporary and permanent release in terms of revocability and the condition of the principal's acceptance.

Core Idea
Deferment from the principal extends to the surety, but not vice versa; temporary release is revocable upon rejection, permanent is not.

In suretyship (kafalah), the creditor may grant a deferment (ta'khir) to either the principal debtor or the surety. The Hanafi school draws a critical distinction between granting deferment to the principal versus the surety, and further between temporary release (ibra' mu'ajjal) and permanent release (ibra' mu'abbad). Understanding these distinctions is essential for determining the obligations of both parties when deferment occurs and when acceptance or rejection by the principal takes place.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on deferment from the principal
If the creditor defers from the principal, this deferment automatically applies to the surety. The surety benefits from the same postponement.
This is the established rule, based on the principle that the surety is an accessory.
2
Identify the ruling on deferment from the surety
If the creditor defers only from the surety, this does not extend to the principal. The principal remains due immediately.
The reverse does not hold because the primary obligation is on the principal.
3
Understand the analogy: temporary release considered like permanent release between principal and surety
The deferment is a temporary release (ibra' mu'ajjal). For the relationship between principal and surety, it is treated analogously to a permanent release (ibra' mu'abbad) in the sense that if the principal is released from demand, the surety is also released.
This analogy is made for the interrelationship, not for the surety's own right.
4
Distinguish between temporary and permanent release regarding revocability
Permanent release is a complete dropping of the right; it cannot be undone by the principal's rejection. Temporary release is only a postponement; if the principal rejects it, the postponement is void and the debt becomes immediately due on the surety.
The difference lies in the nature: dropping (isqat) vs. mere postponement (ta'khir).
5
Note the condition of the principal's acceptance and the intra-Hanafi dispute
The principal's acceptance is required for the release to be effective. Death of the principal is treated as acceptance. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: if the principal rejects the release, does the debt return on the surety? One view says yes; another says it does not.
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the surety's temporary release returns upon rejection, but permanent release does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أخَّرَ عن الأصيل تأخر عنه بخلاف عكسه ( اعتباراً للإبراء المؤقت بالمؤبد. فإن صالح الكفيل الطالب عن ألفي على مئة برى الكفيل والأصيل ورَجَعَ على الأصيل بها إن كَفِلَ بأمرِه) ؛ لأنَّهُ أضاف الصُّلْحَ إلى الألف الذي هو الدين، وهو على الأصيل، فيبرؤ عن تسعمئة ، وبراءته توجب براءة الكفيل، فإن كانت الكفالة بأمره ، رجع" الكفيل بما أدى ، وهو المئة. وفي «السراج الوهاج»: ويشترط قبول الأصيل البراءة، فإن ردّها ارتدت، وهل يعودُ الدِّينُ على الكفيل؟ فيه قولان وموتُ الأصيل كقبوله، وفي «النهاية»: إن إبراء الأصيل وتأجيله يرتدان بالردّ، وبراء الكفيل يرتد بالردّ ، وأما تأجيله فلا يرتد بالرد. انتهى [١]قوله : وإن أخر ...الخ ؛ حاصله : أنَّ الطالب إذا أَخَّر عن الأصيل فهو تأخير عن الكفيل، وإذا أخر عن الكفيل لم يكن تأخيراً عن الذي عليه الأصيل ؛ لأنَّ التأخير إبراء مؤقت ؛ لكونه إسقاط المطالبة إلى غاية، فيعتبر بالإبراء المؤبد فيما بين الأصيل والكفيل. فأما الإبراء المؤقت والمؤيَّدُ يفترقان في حق الكفيل، فإنَّ الإبراء المؤبد للكفيل لا يرتد برده ، وأما الإبراء المؤقت يرتد بردّه ، ويكون الدين عليه حالاً ؛ لأنّ الإبراء المؤبد إسقاط في حقه، والإسقاط لا يرتد بالرد. وأما الإبراء المؤقت فهو تأخير المطالبة ، وليس بإسقاط ، ألا ترى أنَّ المطالبة تعود بعد الأجل، والتأخيرُ قابلُ الإبطال، بخلافِ الإسقاط المحض، كذا في «الكفاية») ، وغيرها.
And if he deferred from the principal, it is a deferment from him, unlike the opposite (considering temporary release as permanent). If the surety settles with the claimant for one thousand on one hundred, the surety and the principal are released, and he recovers from the principal those one hundred if he guaranteed by his command; because he added the settlement to the thousand which is the debt, and it is upon the principal, so he is released from nine hundred, and his release necessitates the release of the surety. If the guarantee was by his command, the surety recovers what he paid, which is the one hundred. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: And it is a condition that the principal accepts the release; if he rejects it, it returns. And does the debt return upon the surety? In it two opinions. And the death of the principal is like his acceptance. In al-Nihayah: The release of the principal and its deferment return with rejection, and the release of the surety returns with rejection, but his deferment does not return with rejection. End. His statement: 'And if he deferred...' etc.; its summary: That if the claimant deferred from the principal, it is a deferment from the surety, and if he deferred from the surety, it is not a deferment from the one upon whom the debt is (the principal); because deferment is temporary release, as it is dropping the demand until a term, so it is considered like permanent release between the principal and the surety. As for temporary and permanent release, they differ concerning the surety: permanent release of the surety does not return with his rejection, but temporary release returns with his rejection, and the debt becomes due on him; because permanent release is dropping in his right, and dropping does not return with rejection. And temporary release is postponement of demand, not dropping; do you not see that demand returns after the term, and postponement is capable of annulment, unlike pure dropping. Thus in al-Kifayah and others.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of deferment (ta'khir) in the context of suretyship (kafalah). The ruling is that if the creditor grants a deferment to the principal debtor, the surety also benefits from that deferment. However, if the creditor grants a deferment to the surety alone, it does not extend to the principal. The reasoning is that deferment is a form of temporary release (ibra' mu'ajjal), which is analogous to permanent release (ibra' mu'abbad) in this inter-relationship. The commentator clarifies a key distinction: permanent release for the surety cannot be undone if the principal rejects it, because it is a complete dropping of the right. Temporary release, on the other hand, can be undone by the principal's rejection, and the debt becomes immediately due on the surety. The passage also mentions a condition from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that the principal must accept the release, and that death of the principal is akin to acceptance. The 'Kifayah' source is cited.

Key Terms
إبراء مؤقت (ibra' mu'ajjal): Temporary release from demand, i.e., deferment of the right to demand payment until a specified time.
إبراء مؤبد (ibra' mu'abbad): Permanent release from the debt, a complete dropping of the right to demand.
تأخير (ta'khir): Deferment, postponement of the due date of a debt.
ارتداد (irtidad): Returning or revoking; the deferment is voided and the original right revives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The debt is deferred for both.
B) The debt becomes immediately due on the surety.
C) The debt is dropped completely.
D) The surety is also released.

Key Takeaway
Deferment is a temporary release; it affects the surety only if it originated from the principal, and is revocable upon the principal's rejection, unlike a permanent release which is irrevocable.

6 min
The Surety's Right of Recourse After Settlement
Ruling on settlement between surety and creditor and its effect on principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a surety's settlement (sulh) with the creditor and distinguish it from release (ibra') regarding the surety's right of recourse.

Core Idea
Settlement (sulh) transfers debt ownership to the surety; release (ibra') extinguishes it.

When a surety (kafil) reaches a settlement (sulh) with the creditor (talib) for a reduced amount, both the surety and the principal (asil) are freed from the remaining debt. The surety can then recover from the principal what he paid. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling and contrasts it with a pure release (ibra'), where no recovery is possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Transactions
Distinguish sulh (settlement, where the creditor accepts a lesser sum) from ibra' (release, where the creditor forgives the debt without payment). Both discharge the principal and surety, but the surety's rights differ.
Sulh involves a payment; ibra' does not.
2
State the Hanafi Ruling
In sulh by the surety, the surety can recover the amount he paid from the principal. In ibra', he cannot recover anything.
The ruling applies whether the surety or the principal makes the settlement.
3
Understand the Legal Reasoning (Talil)
In sulh, the surety's payment transfers ownership of the debt from the creditor to the surety. He then becomes the creditor and can claim from the principal. In ibra', the debt is simply extinguished, so no ownership transfers.
Contrast: payment creates ownership; forgiveness destroys it.
4
Apply the Rule to a Variation
If the principal himself settles, the same reasoning applies: the principal pays and becomes free, and the surety is also freed. The surety does not recover because the debt was paid by the principal.
The settlement by principal extinguishes the liability entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢]قوله : فإن صالح الكفيل ؛ وإن صالح الأصيل، والمسألة بحالها فالحكم كذلك أيضاً ؛ لأنه قد برئ بالصلح ، وبراءته توجب براءة الكفيل كما سبق. (۳) قوله : رجع ؛ إذ بالأداء يملك ما في ذمَّةِ الأصيل، فاستوجب الرجوع بخلاف الإبراء ؛ لأنَّ بالإبراء يسقط الدَّينُ فلا يملكه الكفيل، فلا يرجع.
His statement: 'If the surety settles' – and if the principal settles, and the matter is the same, the ruling is likewise; because he has become free by the settlement, and his freedom necessitates the freedom of the surety as before. (3) His statement: 'he recovers' – because by payment he owns what is in the principal's liability, so he becomes entitled to recourse, unlike release; because by release the debt falls and the surety does not own it, so he does not have recourse.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling that if the surety makes a settlement (sulh) with the creditor for a lesser amount, both the surety and the principal are released from the remainder. The surety can then recover from the principal the amount he paid, provided he was a surety by the principal's command (kafala bi-amrih). The reasoning is that by paying the settlement amount, the surety effectively purchases the debt from the creditor, so he inherits the right to claim it from the principal. This contrasts with a pure release (ibra'), where the debt is extinguished and the surety cannot recover. The commentary also notes that the ruling applies similarly if the principal himself settles.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A settlement transaction where the creditor accepts a reduced amount in exchange for releasing the debtor from the remainder.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In both, the surety can recover from the principal.
B) In sulh, the surety can recover; in ibra', he cannot.
C) In ibra', the surety can recover; in sulh, he cannot.
D) Neither gives the surety a right of recourse.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a surety who settles with the creditor acquires the debt and can recover from the principal, unlike a release which extinguishes the debt entirely.

Surety's Recovery When Settling with Different Goods
Ruling when surety settles by paying a different type of goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that the surety recovers the full debt when settling with a different type of goods, and defend it against the objection that the debt is on the principal.

Core Idea
When the surety settles the debt with a different type of goods, the debt is legally deemed on the surety's liability so he recovers the full amount.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a practical question in kafalah: If the surety pays the creditor with goods of a different type (e.g., a garment instead of cash), how much can he claim from the principal? The text affirms he recovers the full original debt, then presents and rebuts a hypothetical objection. Let's walk through the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The scenario: the surety (kafil) settles the debt of 1000 dirhams by paying with goods of a different type (jins akhar). The ruling: the surety recovers all 1000 dirhams from the principal.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Primary justification: mubadala
The settlement is an exchange (mubadala) – effectively a sale of the debt to the surety. Therefore the surety owns the debt and can recover the full amount.
The term 'mubadala' implies a reciprocal transaction.
3
Address the objection
Objection: The debt is originally on the principal, not the surety. Ownership of a debt from someone not liable is invalid. So how can the surety own it?
This is a valid point that must be resolved.
4
Hanafi response – two approaches
First, for those who view kafalah as joining liabilities (ضم الذمة), the debt already exists in the surety's liability. Second, for others, the debt is deemed established in the surety's liability as a necessary legal fiction to validate the ownership transfer.
The debt is considered to move from the principal's liability to the surety's liability for the purpose of the exchange.
5
Practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: In the Hanafi school, the surety recovers the full original debt. Opposing schools may limit recovery to the value of the goods paid.
This protects the surety from loss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وإن صالح على جنس آخرَ رَجَعَ بالألف) ؛ لأنه" مبادلة" فيملكه الكفيل، فيرجع" بجميع الألف. فإن قلت: إن الدين على الأصيل، فكيف يملكُهُ الكفيل ؛ لأنَّ تمليك الدِّينِ من غيرِ مَن عليه الدِّينُ لا يصح. قلت : أما عند مَن جَعَلَ الكفالة ضمُّ الذمّةِ إلى الذمّةِ فِي الدِّينِ فظاهر، وأمَّا عند الآخرين، فإنَّ المكفول له إذا مَلَكَ الدَّين من الكفيل إما بالهبة، أو بالمعاوضة، فالدين يُجْعَلُ ثابتاً في ذمَّةِ الكفيل ضرورةً صحة التمليك. كذا قالوا .
And if he settles on a different type of goods, he recovers the thousand; because it is an exchange, so the surety owns it, and he recovers all the thousand. If you say: The debt is on the principal, so how does the surety own it? Because making the debt lawful from someone other than the one upon whom it is due is not valid. I say: As for those who consider surety as joining one liability to another in the debt, it is apparent. And as for others, if the creditor owns the debt from the surety, either by gift or exchange, the debt is deemed to be established in the surety's liability as a necessity for the validity of the ownership. That is what they said.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where the surety settles with the creditor by paying a different type of goods (e.g., a garment instead of cash). In such a case, the surety recovers the full original debt from the principal. The reasoning is that this settlement is an exchange (mubadala), so the surety effectively purchases the debt from the creditor. A hypothetical objection is raised: how can the surety own a debt that is owed by the principal? The answer is twofold. For those who view kafalah as joining of liabilities (ضم الذمة إلى الذمة), it is straightforward. For others, the debt is deemed to be established in the surety's liability as a necessary fiction to validate the transfer of ownership, either by gift or exchange.

Key Terms
مبادلة (mubadala): Exchange; here meaning a settlement where the creditor receives a different type of asset in lieu of the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 500 dirhams
B) 1000 dirhams
C) Nothing
D) The value of the garment

Key Takeaway
When the surety settles the debt with a different type of goods, the debt is legally deemed on the surety's liability, so he recovers the full original amount from the principal.

Settlement on the Subject Matter of Suretyship
Ruling when surety settles his own suretyship obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling when a surety settles only his own liability and state the amount he recovers from the principal, while contrasting the view of Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad.

Core Idea
The surety's settlement on mawjab al-kafalah releases only himself, not the principal.

In suretyship (kafalah), the creditor has a right to demand payment from both the principal and the surety. What happens if the surety negotiates a separate settlement with the creditor to be released from his own obligation, without settling the underlying debt? This lesson examines the Hanafi position: the surety walks free, but the principal remains fully liable, and the surety can then recover the entire debt from the principal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The surety (kafil) settles with the creditor (talib) specifically regarding the 'subject matter of suretyship' (mawjab al-kafalah), i.e., the creditor's right to demand payment from the surety, not the debt itself.
The settlement is on the surety's liability, not the principal's debt.
2
State the ruling
The surety is released, but the principal is not released. The principal's debt remains intact.
This is the Hanafi position (zahir al-riwayah).
3
Understand the legal reasoning (talil)
The settlement is considered an exchange (mubadala). The surety gives something to the creditor in exchange for ownership of the debt that was owed by the principal. Thus, the surety becomes the creditor of the principal.
The commentary clarifies that the debt transfers from the principal's liability to the surety's liability when the creditor takes it from him.
4
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf on recovery
Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad hold that the surety recovers only the lesser of the debt or the value paid. The Hanafi position: he recovers the full debt (the thousand).
Practical consequence: the amount the surety can claim from the principal differs.
5
Apply to a concrete example
Zayd owes Amr 1000. Bakr is surety. Bakr gives Amr a garment worth 500 to release Bakr from suretyship. Result: Bakr released; Zayd still owes 1000, now to Bakr. Bakr can claim 1000 from Zayd.
Under other madhhabs, Bakr would only recover 500 from Zayd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وإن صالح عن مُوجَب الكفالة لم يبرو الأصيل) [ ۱ ] قوله : لأنـه...الخ ؛ يعني لأنَّ هذا الصلح يكون مبادلة، فيصير الألف بمقابلة الثوب، فيملك ما في ذمَّةِ الأصيل، فيرجعُ بكلّه عليه، وتوضيحه أنَّ الألف في الأصيل في ذمَّةِ الأصيل، ثم انتقل عنه، وثبت في ذمَّةِ الكفيل حين أخذ الطالب منه، فيصح تمليك الطالب الدين الألف من الكفيل ؛ لكونه تمليك الدين ممن عليه الدين. وكذا يصح التمليك من الكفيل بالهبة إذا أذن له بالقبض ، فصار كأنه أخرجه عن الكفالة، ووكله بالقبض، فقبضه ثم وهبه، فيصير تمليك الدين ممن عليه الدين مع الكفيل مسلّطاً على الدين في الجملة، وإذا عرفت هذا، فاتضح لك تقرير الإيراد وجوابه وضاحة كاملة. [٢]قوله : مبادلة ؛ ولهذا لو صالح ربُّ المالِ الأصيل على غير جنس الدِّينِ خرجَ الكفيل من كفالته، ولو صالحه على جنس الدين لا يخرج ، كذا في «القنية). [٣]قوله : فيرجع بجميع الألف ؛ وقال مالك والشافعي وأحمد ه: يرجع بالأقل من الدين، ومن قيمة ما دفع ، فإنّه أوفى هذا القدر. [٤] قوله : وإن صالح الكفيل عن موجب الكفالة ؛ أي عما وجب بالكفالة، وهو المطالبة على شيء بشرط إبراء الكفيل خاصة لم يبرأ الأصيل وبرئ الكفيل ؛ لأنَّ هذا الصلح إبراء الكفيل عن المطالبة ، وإبراؤُهُ عن الكفالة يصير فسخاً لكفالته، لا إسقاطاً
And if he settles on the subject matter of suretyship, the principal is not released. [1] His statement: because it is an exchange, so the thousand becomes in exchange for the garment, so he owns what is in the principal's liability, and he recovers all of it from him. Its clarification: the thousand is in the principal's liability, then it is transferred from him and established in the surety's liability when the creditor takes it from him, so the creditor's ownership of the thousand debt from the surety is valid, because it is ownership of a debt from the one upon whom it is due. Similarly, ownership from the surety by gift is valid if he authorized him to take possession. [2] His statement: exchange; therefore if the owner of money settles with the principal on a different type, the surety leaves his suretyship, but if he settles on the same type, he does not. [3] His statement: he recovers all the thousand; and Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad said: He recovers the lesser of the debt and the value of what he paid. [4] His statement: and if the surety settles on the subject matter of suretyship, i.e., on what is obligated by suretyship, which is the demand, on a condition of releasing the surety specifically, the principal is not released and the surety is released; because this settlement is a release of the surety from demand, and his release from suretyship becomes a dissolution of his suretyship, not a dropping (of the debt).
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where the surety makes a settlement with the creditor specifically about the subject matter of the suretyship (mawjab al-kafalah), i.e., the creditor's right to demand payment from the surety. The ruling is that this only releases the surety, not the principal. The commentary explains that such a settlement is effectively an exchange (mubadala) where the surety buys his release. If the creditor settles with the principal on a different type of goods, the surety is released; if on the same type, he remains. A difference with other madhhabs is noted: Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad say the surety recovers only the lesser of the debt or the value paid, but the Hanafi position is that he recovers the full debt. The key point is the distinction between settling the debt itself and settling the obligation of the surety.

Key Terms
موجب الكفالة (mawjab al-kafalah): The subject matter of the suretyship; i.e., the creditor's right to demand payment from the surety.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Both principal and surety are released.
B) The principal is released, the surety is not.
C) The surety is released, the principal is not.
D) Neither principal nor surety is released.

Key Takeaway
Settlement on mawjab al-kafalah releases only the surety; the principal remains liable and the surety recovers the full debt.

Effect of Surety's Statement of Release on Recourse
Intra-Hanafi dispute on surety's recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi positions regarding the surety's statement of release and apply the criterion of linguistic attribution to determine whether recourse is permitted.

Core Idea
A surety's statement 'I am free' (bari'tu) implies payment and gives recourse; the creditor's 'I release you' (abra'tuka) implies dropping and denies recourse.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani over the interpretation of the surety's claim of release. The issue turns on whether the surety's statement 'bari'tu' (without 'ilayya') is an acknowledgment of payment or merely an ambiguous claim. The preferred view, supported by Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, holds that the surety's own attribution of release to himself can only be by payment, thus granting him recourse against the principal. This lesson will walk you through the linguistic reasoning and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios and their default rulings
The passage presents three creditor statements: 'bari'ta ilayya' (with 'to me'), 'bari'ta' (without 'to me'), and 'abra'tuka' (I released you). Note the ruling for each scenario.
The first and third have clear rulings; the second is disputed.
2
Follow the dispute over 'bari'ta' without 'ilayya'
Abu Yusuf (and Abu Hanifa) argue that the surety's action of freeing himself necessarily implies payment. Muhammad argues that the statement is ambiguous, and doubt prevents recourse.
Understand each imam's core premise.
3
Analyze the linguistic proof for each position
Abu Yusuf focuses on the verb form: the surety attributes the release to himself, and a surety can only free himself by paying. Muhammad counters that the release could be due to the creditor's dropping, which is the lesser probability.
Pay attention to the linguistic analysis (sarf/nahw).
4
Examine the analogy to a written document
Abu Yusuf draws an analogy to a document stating 'the surety is free,' which is universally considered an acknowledgment of receipt. Muhammad distinguishes that documents are typically written only for payment releases.
This shows how qiyas is used within the school.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and the thamarah al-khilaf
The author of Al-Hidayah prefers Abu Yusuf's view. The practical difference: under Abu Yusuf, the surety has recourse without proving payment; under Muhammad, he must prove payment.
This is the real-world impact of the ikhtilaf.

وفي أبرأتك لا يرجع

[E] ( وفي أبرأتك لا يرجع ) ، قيل في جميع ذلك إن كان الطالب حاضراً يرجعُ ع) إليه في البيان.

And if the creditor says to the surety: 'You are free from liability to me of the debt,' he (the surety) has recourse against his principal. Similarly, in 'you are free' according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad, because this reconciliation is a release of the surety from the claim, so it does not necessitate the release of the principal. (And if the creditor says to the surety: 'You are free from liability to me of the debt,' he has recourse against his principal) because the release that begins from the surety and ends with the creditor is only by payment, as if he said: 'You are free by payment to me,' so he has recourse for the debt against the principal if the suretyship was by his order. (Similarly in 'you are free' according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad) for the original debt, and releasing the surety does not necessitate the release of the principal; because it is not a cause for dropping the original debt, so the debt remains on the principal as it was before the surety's guarantee. [1] His saying: 'to'; is attached to an omitted circumstantial phrase, i.e., 'while you are paying to me,' so it is a release by collection, not a release by dropping. [2] His saying: 'he has recourse against his principal'; i.e., for what he guaranteed to the guaranteed person by order of the guaranteed party. [3] His saying: 'he said: you are free'; this is an acknowledgment of receipt, so the creditor has no claim against the surety nor the principal, and the surety has recourse against his principal. [4] His saying: 'if the suretyship was by his order'; and if the suretyship was not by his order, then the surety does not have recourse against his principal. [0] His saying: 'Similarly in you are free... etc.'; meaning that if the creditor says to the surety: 'You are free from the debt,' and does not say: 'to me,' then the surety has recourse against his principal according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad, for according to him he does not have recourse. And it is said that Abu Hanifa is with Abu Yusuf in this issue, and the author of Al-Hidayah chose it; therefore he delayed its mention as is his habit, and the Musannif made it a principle and advanced it in mention because it is the more probable of the two possibilities, so it is more appropriate, and he attributed the opposition to Muhammad. And the reason for its probability is what is in Fath al-Qadir: that it is an acknowledgment of a release whose beginning is from the surety being addressed, and its result is establishing the release from him specifically, like 'you stood' and 'you sat,' and the release (that is) from him specifically is like payment, contrary to the release by dropping, for it is not realized by the act of the surety, but by the act of the creditor, so it is not then attributed to the surety. And what Muhammad said, that receipt is not established by doubt, is only valid if the two probabilities are equal. [1] His saying: 'He said... etc.'; its summary is that his saying: 'I am free from the debt' definitively indicates the release of the surety, and release may be by the surety paying the money to the creditor, or by the creditor dropping the debt from the surety, and release by dropping and dropping is the lesser of the two probabilities, so it is established rather than the other. So when we are certain of the occurrence of release and we doubt about recourse, recourse only occurs in the case of payment, not in the case of dropping, so we rule that he does not have recourse due to doubt. (2) His saying: 'And for Abu Yusuf... etc.'; its summary is that his saying: 'I am free from the debt' only admits the release by payment because he mentioned the pronoun of address, which is the open ta, and attributed the release to the addressee who is the demanded party, and he cannot achieve release except by payment, such as by placing the money before him and vacating possession of it in his favor. So it is as if he acknowledged release by payment, and thus he is released, even if no act was done by the creditor; and for this reason, if it is written: 'the surety is free from the debt,' it is an acknowledgment of receipt by consensus. So also here, if a distinction is made between them in terms of wording. And Muhammad distinguished between them that a document is not usually written except when the release is by payment, whereas if it is by dropping, it is not written. [3] His saying: 'he does not have recourse'; because his saying: 'I released you' indicates a release that does not end with another, for the speaker attributed the action to himself, and such release cannot be except by dropping, so this statement is not an acknowledgment of payment, so he does not have recourse. Yes, the creditor may demand the money from the principal, as stated in Al-Kafi and others. [4] His saying: 'he has recourse to him'; because the principle regarding ambiguity is to refer to the speaker of the ambiguous statement if he is present, to remove the probability, especially if custom regarding that wording is shared.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Al-Wiqayah, dealing with the law of suretyship (kafalah). The central issue is: when the creditor says to the surety 'you are free from the debt,' does the surety have the right to recover the amount from the principal debtor? The author presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, with Abu Hanifa's view aligning with Abu Yusuf according to some reports. The passage first discusses the scenario where the creditor explicitly says 'bari'ta ilayya min al-mal' (you are free from liability to me of the debt). Here, the word 'ilayya' (to me) indicates that the release is by way of payment (istifa'), meaning the surety paid the creditor. Therefore, the surety has recourse against the principal if the suretyship was at the principal's request. If the creditor says merely 'bari'ta min al-mal' (you are free from the debt) without 'ilayya', then Abu Yusuf considers that this also implies payment, as the surety's own act of freeing himself can only be by paying. Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, holding that the phrase is ambiguous: it could mean payment or it could mean the creditor simply dropped the debt (ibra'). Since dropping is the lesser probability (because it is easier), Muhammad argues that the surety cannot prove payment by doubt, so he does not have recourse. Abu Yusuf counters that the structure of the sentence attributes the action to the surety (using the second person past tense), and a surety can only 'free himself' by paying, just as a document stating 'the surety is free' is considered an acknowledgment of receipt. The author notes that Abu Hanifa reportedly agreed with Abu Yusuf, and the author of Al-Hidayah preferred this view. The passage then distinguishes the case where the creditor says 'abra'tuka' (I released you). Since the creditor attributes the action to himself, this clearly indicates an ibra' (dropping) by the creditor, not payment by the surety. Therefore, the surety does not have recourse against the principal. However, the creditor may still demand payment from the principal because the ibra' only benefits the surety, not the principal. Finally, if the wording is ambiguous, the general principle is to ask the speaker if present to clarify. This passage demonstrates the Hanafi method of linguistic analysis and qiyas in resolving disputes within the school.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contract of guarantee where the surety (kafil) becomes liable for a debt owed by the principal (asil) to the creditor (mukful lahu/talib).
براءة (bara'ah): Release or discharge from liability; in this context, either by payment (istifa') or by the creditor's dropping (ibra').
إبراء (ibra'): The creditor's voluntary dropping of the debt, which releases the debtor but does not imply payment.
رجوع (ruju'): The surety's right to recover from the principal the amount paid to the creditor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, if the creditor says to the surety 'bari'ta min al-mal' (you are free from the debt) without adding 'ilayya', does the surety have recourse against the principal?
A) Yes, because the surety's own statement of being free implies payment.
B) No, because the statement is ambiguous and doubt prevents recourse.
C) Only if the principal was present when the statement was made.
D) Only if the surety provides written proof of payment.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a surety says 'I am free,' the preferred view is that this implies payment, entitling him to recourse against the principal, unless the creditor says 'I release you,' which indicates a dropping of the debt without recourse.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate

Guarantor's Recourse After Absolution: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Analysis of guarantor's recourse after statements of absolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Imam Muhammad denies recourse while Abu Yusuf grants it when a guarantor says 'برئت من المال', and apply the Hanafi ruling to similar statements.

Core Idea
When absolution is ambiguous, the lesser probability (discharge) prevails, blocking the guarantor's recourse.

A guarantor's statement 'I am absolved' seems straightforward, but its legal consequences hinge on whether the absolution came from payment or creditor discharge. The Hanafi school splits on interpreting this ambiguity: Imam Muhammad denies recourse, while Abu Yusuf grants it. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Imam Muhammad)
Identify Imam Muhammad's ruling: the guarantor saying 'برئت من المال' does not have recourse because the word 'برئت' is ambiguous between payment and discharge; the lesser probability (discharge) is taken, so no recourse.
The lesser probability is established due to doubt (shakk).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Abu Yusuf holds that the guarantor does have recourse because the pronoun 'I' implies payment by the guarantor himself, making it an acknowledgment of payment.
Abu Yusuf's focus is on the linguistic implication of the first-person pronoun.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine Abu Yusuf's dalil: the guarantor can only achieve absolution by paying, so saying 'I am absolved' must mean he paid; therefore he should have recourse.
He analogizes to a written acknowledgment where consensus grants recourse.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Review Imam Muhammad's rebuttal: there are two possible meanings (payment or discharge), and since we only know absolution occurred, we take the lesser probability (discharge) by default, blocking recourse.
The principle 'لا يثبت القبض بالشك' (acceptance is not established by doubt) applies.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Imam Muhammad, the guarantor is free from the creditor but cannot recover from the principal; under Abu Yusuf, he can recover. The text sides with Imam Muhammad.
For 'أبرأتك' (creditor says 'I absolved you'), the ruling is unanimously no recourse.

Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and ruling on the guarantor's recourse after statements of absolution. The discussion centers on two phrases: 'برئت من المال' (I am absolved of the wealth) said by the guarantor, and 'أبرأتك' (I absolved you) said by the creditor. For the first phrase, Imam Muhammad argues that the word 'برئت' indicates absolute absolution but with two possible meanings: payment by the guarantor or discharge by the creditor. Since both are possible, the lesser probability (discharge) is taken, meaning the guarantor is absolved but does not gain recourse against the principal because he did not actually pay. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that the phrase only implies payment because the pronoun 'I' refers to the guarantor, and the guarantor can only achieve absolution by paying; thus it is an acknowledgment of payment, and he should have recourse. The text sides with Imam Muhammad, ruling that the guarantor does not have recourse due to doubt. For the second phrase 'أبرأتك' (I absolved you) said by the creditor, the ruling is clear: the guarantor does not have recourse because this is an outright discharge (isqat), not payment. The creditor may still claim the debt from the principal. An exception is made if the creditor is present and can clarify his intent, in which case his statement is taken into account to resolve the ambiguity. This follows the principle that ambiguous statements are referred back to the speaker when possible.

Key Terms
براءة (bara'a): Absolution from a debt, either by payment (ada') or by discharge (ibra').
إبراء (ibra'): Discharge of debt by the creditor, a voluntary dropping of the claim.
كفيل (kafil): Guarantor who assumes liability for another's debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a guarantor says 'برئت من المال' (I am absolved of the wealth), what is the Hanafi ruling according to Imam Muhammad?
A) He has recourse against the principal because he admitted payment.
B) He does not have recourse because the statement is ambiguous and the lesser probability (discharge) is taken.
C) He has recourse only if the creditor is present.
D) The statement is invalid and the guarantee continues.

Key Takeaway
When a guarantor's statement of absolution is ambiguous, the Hanafi ruling takes the lesser probability (discharge), denying recourse.

Kafalah — Surety

Validity of Conditional Absolution from Kafala
Ruling on suspending guarantor's absolution on a condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi views on conditional absolution from kafala and identify the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.

Core Idea
The later Hanafi view considers conditional absolution from kafala valid because the guarantor's liability is only for demand, making it pure isqat.

In Hanafi fiqh, suspending a legal effect on a condition (ta'liq) is generally valid in acts of dropping rights (isqat) but invalid in acts of transferring ownership (tamlik). Absolution from a debt (ibra') is typically treated as tamlik, hence conditional ibra' is invalid. However, when the guarantor (kafil) is absolved, some later Hanafi authorities argue that the liability is merely for the right to demand, not the debt itself, making ibra' a pure isqat and thus valid when suspended. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the evolution of the school's position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is suspending absolution (ibra') from kafala upon a future condition, e.g., 'If Zaid returns, I absolve you from the guarantee.' The ruling is disputed: invalid in the primary view, valid in the later chosen view.
Focus on the nature of the act: it is a conditional release from a guarantee.
2
State the primary view and its proof
The primary view holds that conditional ibra' is invalid because ibra' contains a meaning of tamlik (ownership transfer), and tamlik cannot be suspended as it resembles gambling (qimar). This applies whether the guarantor owes the debt or just the right to demand, since the right to demand is akin to debt.
The proof is qiyas on other tamlik contracts like sale.
3
State the second view and its proof
The second view holds that conditional ibra' from kafala is valid because what is established on the guarantor is only the right to demand (mutalaba), not the debt itself. Hence, ibra' is pure isqat (dropping of a right), which can be suspended, analogous to divorce (talaq) and manumission (itq).
The proof distinguishes kafala from ordinary debt: the guarantor is not liable for the debt per se.
4
Understand the tarjih mechanism
The author of Fath al-Qadir chose the second view, calling it 'awjah' (more appropriate). Multaqa al-Abhar states 'al-mukhtar al-sihha' (the chosen is validity). This tarjih is based on the strength of the distinction between tamlik and isqat in the context of kafala.
Note the authority weight: later scholars (fatawa mutakhkhirin) prefer validity.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its practical implications
The relied-upon position is that conditional absolution from kafala is valid. Therefore, if a creditor says to the guarantor, 'If Zaid returns, you are free from the guarantee,' and Zaid returns, the guarantor is indeed freed. The guarantee ends upon fulfillment of the condition.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa today.

And when absolution from kafala becomes void, the kafala remains in its original state, so the claimant may demand the guarantor by evidence of the reasoning. So the intended meaning is not the invalidity of the suspension of absolution, because that would entail that the absolution is valid and immediate, and the kafala becomes void by it, and that does not suit the mentioned cause because the suspension itself does not have the meaning of tamlik; rather, that which has the meaning of tamlik is the suspended absolution, so it becomes void, as in Radd al-Muhtar. [3] His statement: 'Upon a condition' – for example, 'If tomorrow comes, you are free from the kafala of the wealth,' and tomorrow comes, he is not free from it, because his condition is void, and his kafala is valid. It is said in Manh al-Ghaffar, quoting from al-Mi'raj: It was said: The intended meaning of 'condition' is a pure condition in which there is no benefit for the claimant at all, like entering the house and the coming of tomorrow, because it is not customary.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of suspending the absolution (ibra') of a guarantor upon a future condition, such as 'If someone arrives, I absolve you.' The ruling is that such conditional absolution is not valid according to the primary view, because ibra' contains an element of tamlik (transfer of ownership), and tamlik cannot be suspended on a condition as it resembles gambling (qimar). This applies whether one holds that the guarantor owes the debt itself or only the right to demand (mutalaba), since the right to demand is akin to debt. However, there is a second view that it is valid, based on the position that the guarantor is only liable for the demand, not the debt, making ibra' pure isqat (dropping of a right), which can be suspended. The author of Fath al-Qadir chooses this second view, and Multaqa al-Abhar states the chosen position is validity (sahha). The passage clarifies that the invalidity means the suspended absolution itself is void, not the act of suspending; thus the guarantee remains operative. The condition must be a 'pure condition' with no benefit to the creditor, like 'if tomorrow comes' – otherwise, conditions with benefit might be treated differently.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending a legal effect upon a future uncertain condition.
إبراء (ibra'): Absolution or discharge of a debt by creditor's act.
إسقاط (isqat): Dropping or relinquishing a right, not involving ownership transfer.
كفالة (kafala): Guarantee or suretyship; a contract where the guarantor stands for the debt or obligation of the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is valid only if the condition benefits the creditor.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon position in the later Hanafi school is that conditional absolution from kafala is valid because the guarantor's liability is only for the right to demand, making ibra' pure isqat.

Conditional Discharge from Surety: Custom Distinction
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional discharge in kafālah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi views on conditional discharge from suretyship and apply the customary-condition distinction to determine validity.

Core Idea
A conditional discharge from suretyship is valid only if the condition is customary.

The Hanafi school has an internal debate about whether a surety can be discharged from liability on a condition. The default ruling says it's invalid because discharge resembles ownership transfer, which can't be conditional. But another view argues that discharge in suretyship is pure dropping, like divorce, and therefore can be conditional. The key to resolving the disagreement lies in the nature of the condition: customary conditions are valid, non-customary ones are not. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The passage discusses whether a surety (kafil) can be discharged from liability (barā'ah) on a future condition (shart). Determine what exactly is being contested.
The act is ta'līq al-barā'ah - suspending discharge on a condition.
2
State the two Hanafi positions
Extract the two views: (1) Invalid because ibrā' involves tamlīk, which cannot be conditional. (2) Valid because ibrā' is pure isqāt, like talāq and itq.
Both views are presented in the text; note which scholars support each.
3
Analyze the reasoning for each view
Examine the rationale: View 1 argues that tamlīk conditional leads to gambling (qimār). View 2 counters that the right in kafālah is only muṭālabah (right to demand), not a debt, so discharge is isqāt, which can be conditional.
Notice the difference between isqāt and tamlīk in terms of revocability upon rejection.
4
Understand the resolution via custom
The commentator resolves the apparent contradiction between narrations by distinguishing customary (mutāraf) conditions from non-customary ones. Non-customary conditions (e.g., 'if you enter the house') invalidate; customary conditions (e.g., 'if I meet you tomorrow') validate.
This resolution is drawn from sources like al-Īḍāḥ and Mabsūṭ of Shaykh al-Islām.
5
Identify the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position
The preferred view is validity when the condition is customary, as chosen by the author of Fatḥ al-Qadīr and Multaqā al-Abḥar. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
This tarjīh is explicit in the passage.

Conditional discharge from suretyship is not valid with a condition, like other discharges, as if he says: 'If so-and-so returns from travel, I discharge you from the debt,' the discharge is not valid. Some intend receipt and payment, and some intend dropping and discharge. The author says: 'It is not valid' because in discharge there is a meaning of ownership transfer, and ownership transfer does not accept conditioning due to being gambling; this is apparent on the view that a debt is established against the surety, and also on the other view because suretyship is an ownership transfer of the right to demand, and the right to demand is like a debt because it is a means to it. And it is said: It is valid because the correct view is that what is established in suretyship against the surety is the right to demand, not the debt, so it is pure dropping like divorce and emancipation; for this reason, discharging the surety does not revert upon rejection because dropping is completed by the dropper, unlike deferment for the surety, which reverts upon rejection because it is not dropping, and unlike discharging the principal from debt because it has a meaning of ownership transfer. The author of 'Fath al-Qadir' chose this second view and said it is more likely, and in 'Multaqa al-Abhar' it says the chosen is validity. End. The author says: 'with a condition' e.g., 'If tomorrow comes, you are free from suretyship for the property,' so when tomorrow comes he is not free from it, since his condition is void and his suretyship is valid. It is said in 'Manh al-Ghaffar' quoting from 'al-Mi'raj': 'The intended condition is a pure condition in which there is absolutely no benefit for the claimant, such as entering the house and the coming of tomorrow, because it is not customary. As for when it is customary, then it is permissible as in the suspension of suretyship, as in 'al-Idah': If the surety for property or person says: 'If I meet you tomorrow, I am free from the property,' and he meets him tomorrow, he is free from the property. Thus he permitted suspension of discharge from suretyship for property, and likewise if he suspends discharge on collecting part, it is permissible, or he suspends discharge for part on prompt payment of part, it is permissible, mentioned in 'Mabsut of Shaykh al-Islam'. So it is known that the intended condition is the non-customary condition, and the difference of the two narrations regarding the validity of suspension is based on this: the narration of impermissibility is when it is non-customary, and the narration of permissibility is when it is customary. End, and here there is a fine research mentioned in 'Radd al-Muhtar'.)
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage deals with an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a conditional discharge (ibrā') from suretyship (kafālah). The core issue is whether discharging the surety from liability can be made conditional upon a future event. The author first states the default ruling: conditional discharge is invalid, just like other discharges conditional on a condition like 'if so-and-so returns from travel, I discharge you from the debt.' The reason given is that discharge (ibrā') involves a transfer of ownership (tamlīk), and tamlīk cannot be suspended on a condition because that resembles gambling (qimār). This reasoning holds whether we consider that a debt is established against the surety or that only a right to demand (muṭālabah) is established, since the right to demand is like a debt. However, a second opinion is presented: discharge is valid because what is actually established in suretyship is only the right to demand, not the debt itself. Thus, discharge is pure dropping (isqāṭ), like divorce and emancipation, and does not involve ownership transfer. Evidence for this is that discharging a surety does not revert upon rejection, whereas deferment does. The commentator then notes that the author of Fatḥ al-Qadīr chose the second view as the stronger one, and the author of Multaqā al-Abḥar states that the chosen position is validity. The commentary further clarifies that the phrase 'suspension of discharge' does not mean the suspension itself is invalid, but rather the conditional discharge is invalid. Then the discussion turns to the nature of the condition: it must be a 'pure condition' (al-sharṭ al-maḥḍ) that brings no benefit to the claimant, such as 'if you enter the house' or 'if tomorrow comes' — these are non-customary. If the condition is customary (mut‘āraf), such as 'if I meet you tomorrow, I am free from the property,' then the discharge is valid. Thus, the dispute between the narrations of permissibility and impermissibility is resolved by distinguishing customary from non-customary conditions. The relied-upon (muftā bihī) position is that conditional discharge is valid when the condition is customary.

Key Terms
تعليق البراءة (ta‘līq al-barā’ah): Suspending the discharge from liability upon the occurrence of a future condition.
إبراء (ibrā’): Discharge from a right or debt; a legal act of dropping a claim.
تمليك (tamlīk): Transfer of ownership of a right to another.
إسقاط (isqāṭ): The act of dropping or waiving a right, without transferring it.
مطالبة (muṭālabah): The right to demand payment or performance from the surety.
شرط متعارف (sharṭ muta‘āraf): A condition that is customary and accepted in transactions, hence not akin to gambling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the condition is any future event.
B) When the condition is customary in transactions.
C) When the condition involves a payment.
D) Never, conditional discharge is always invalid.

Key Takeaway
A conditional discharge from suretyship is valid only if the condition is customary, reflecting the Hanafi resolution of the intra-school dispute.

Subjects Invalid for Kafalah: Hudud, Qisas, and the Sold Item
Clarifies which items cannot be the subject of a surety contract.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the types of subject matters for which kafalah is invalid in Hanafi fiqh and explain the condition of the subject being guaranteed (madhmun).

Core Idea
Kafalah is invalid for hudud, qisas, and non-guaranteed items — only guaranteed debts or items can be covered.

This lesson examines the limits of kafalah (suretyship) in Hanafi law. While kafalah is a valid contract for guaranteeing debts or delivery of guaranteed items, it cannot be used for personal penalties like hudud and qisas, nor for items that are not guaranteed against the principal. Understanding this boundary is essential for structuring valid surety agreements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the explicitly prohibited subjects
The text first states that kafalah is invalid for hudud and qisas because they cannot be exacted from the surety.
2
Analyze the reasoning for hudud and qisas
These punishments are personal; they cannot be performed by proxy (niyābah). The surety cannot undergo punishment on behalf of another.
3
Distinguish between mabī‘ and thaman
The text contrasts the sold item (mabī‘) with the price (thaman). Kafalah for thaman is valid because it is a financial debt, but for mabī‘ it is not automatically invalid.
4
Classify tangible items (a‘yān)
Items are either guaranteed in themselves (value owed upon destruction) or not. Kafalah is valid only for the former.
5
Apply the condition to examples
Test if a given item is madhmun: if the principal is liable for its value, kafalah is valid; if it is a trust (amānah), it is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا الكفالة بما تعدَّر استيفاؤه من الكفيل، كالحدودِ والقصاص، وبالمبيع بخلاف الثَّمَنِ. اعلم أن الكفالة بتسليم المبيع تصح، وتوضيحه : إنَّ الأعيان إمَّا مضمونةٌ بنفسها : وهي ما يجب قيمتها عند الهلاك، أو بغيرها : وهي ما لا يجب قيمتها، أو أمانة، والأوَّلُ تصح الكفالة به ولا تصح بالثاني والثالث؛ لفقدان شرطها، وهو أن يكون مضموناً على الأصيل، لا يخرج عنه إلا بدفع عينه أو بدله.
Kafalah is not valid for what is impossible to exact from the surety, such as hudud and qisas, and for the sold item, unlike the price. Know that kafalah for delivery of the sold item is valid, and its clarification: Indeed, corporeal objects are either guaranteed in themselves—those for which their value is obligatory upon destruction—or guaranteed by something else—those for which their value is not obligatory—or a trust. For the first type, kafalah is valid, and it is not valid for the second and third, because its condition is lost, which is that it be guaranteed against the principal, not departing from him except by delivery of the specific object or its substitute.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage states a general rule: kafalah (suretyship) is not valid for things that cannot be exacted from the surety himself. Examples given are the fixed corporal punishments (ḥudūd) and retaliation (qiṣāṣ), because these are personal punishments that cannot be performed by proxy (niyābah). The reason is that the purpose of these penalties is to punish the offender himself. Additionally, for kafalah to be valid, the subject matter must be something that can be delivered by the surety. Hudud and qisas are not deliverable in this sense. Then the text mentions 'and for the sold item (al-mabī‘), unlike the price (al-thaman).' This seems to indicate that kafalah for the sold item itself is not valid, but the commentary clarifies that this is not absolute. In fact, kafalah for the delivery of the sold item (taslīm al-mabī‘) is valid if the sold item is a guaranteed item (madhmun). The commentary explains that tangible items (a‘yān) fall into three categories: (1) items that are guaranteed in themselves (maḍmūnah bi-nafsihā) – those for which the value is owed upon destruction, like sold goods before delivery; (2) items guaranteed by something else – those for which value is not owed, like items held as trust; and (3) pure trusts. Kafalah is valid only for the first type because the condition is that the item must be guaranteed against the principal (al-aṣīl), meaning that the principal is liable for it, and it can only be discharged by delivering the item itself or its substitute (value). This condition is fulfilled for guaranteed items but not for trusts or non-guaranteed items.

Key Terms
الحدود (al-ḥudūd): Fixed corporal punishments for crimes like theft, adultery, etc.
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retaliation punishment for murder or injury, equivalent to the crime.
مضمون بنفسه (maḍmūn bi-nafsihi): An item that is itself guaranteed, meaning its value is owed upon destruction.
ثمن (thaman): The price (money) in a sale.
مبيع (mabī‘): The sold item (the object of sale).
نيابة (niyābah): Representation; performing an act on behalf of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is kafalah not valid for hudud and qisas?
A) Because they are too severe.
B) Because they are personal punishments that cannot be performed by proxy.
C) Because they are not financial obligations.
D) Because the surety might be harmed.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah in Hanafi law requires the subject matter to be guaranteed against the principal; personal punishments and non-guaranteed items cannot be covered.

Invalidity of Kafalah for Hudud and Qisas
Explaining the invalidity of kafalah for corporal punishments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that kafalah for hudud and qisas is invalid and explain the rational proof (dalil aqli) supporting it.

Core Idea
Kafalah is invalid for corporal punishments because they are not deliverable by a substitute.

In this passage, the commentator explains why kafalah (guarantee) is not valid for hudud (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliation). Understanding this ruling requires grasping the key condition that the object of guarantee must be deliverable by the guarantor. Unlike financial debts, corporal punishments cannot be executed by anyone other than the offender himself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The text states: 'There is no kafalah for what is impossible to exact from the guarantor.' Identify what 'impossible to exact' means in this context.
2
Recognize the examples given
The examples are hudud and qisas. Note that these are specific corporal punishments that cannot be delegated.
3
Understand the rational proof
The commentary provides two reasons: (a) kafalah requires substitution in fulfillment, which is not allowed in punishments, and (b) the object must be deliverable, which hudud and qisas are not.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof).
4
Distinguish from kafalah for the person
The text notes that kafalah for the person who owes hudud or qisas (i.e., guaranteeing his appearance) is valid. This is because it is not kafalah for the punishment itself.
See Al-Binayah reference.

كتاب الكفالة تعريفها وشروطها وأحكامها
ولا الكفالة بما تعدَّر استيفاؤه من الكفيل، كالحدودِ والقصاص، وبالمبيع بخلاف الثَّمَنِ
قال: (ولا الكفالة بما تعدَّر استيفاؤه من الكفيل، كالحدود والقصاص، وبالمبيع بخلاف الثَّمن) اعلم أن الكفالة بتسليم المبيع تصح. أما إذا كان متعارفاً فإنّه يجوزُ كما في تعليق الكفالة، كما في «الإيضاح»: الكفيل بالمال أو النفس لو قال : إن وافيتُك غداً فأنا بريء من المال ، فوافاه غدا يبرؤ من المال. فقد جوز تعليق البراءةِ عن الكفالة بالمال، وكذا إذا علق البراءة باستيفاء البعض يجوز، أو علق البراءة عن البعض بتعجيل البعض يجوز، ذكره في «مبسوط شيخ الإسلام». فعلم أنَّ المراد بالشرط الشرط : الغير متعارف، واختلاف الروايتين في صحة التعليق محمول على هذا، فرواية عدم الجواز فيما إذا كان غير متعارف، ورواية الجواز فيما إذا كان متعارفا. انتهى، وهاهنا بحث نفيس مذكورٌ في ردّ المحتار) 
[١) قوله : ولا كفالة ؛ لأنَّ الكفالة إنّما تصح بمضمون تجري النيابة في إيفاءه، ولا تجري النيابة في العقوبات ؛ لأنَّ المقصود من شرعها جزاء المفسدين عن الفساد، فلا يمكن إقامتها على غير الجاني، ولأنَّ شرط الكفالة كون المكفول به مقدور التسليم من الكفيل، والحد والقصاص ليسا كذلك. [٢]قوله : كالحدود والقصاص ؛ أي كنفس الحدودِ ونفس القصاص، فإنَّ الكفالة بنفس من عليه الحدود والقصاص تجوز، صرَّحَ به في «البناية)، وغيرها. [٣] قوله : بخلاف الثمن ؛ فإنّه يصحُ الكفالة به عن المشتري ؛ لأنه دين كسائر الديون. (٤) قوله: اعلم ؛ لما كان قول المصنف : وبالمبيع يحتمل احتمالاً تصح الكفالة فيه، واحتمالاً لم تصح، فوجب أن يصرح الشارحُ له بما هو المقصود هاهنا، فقال: واعلم أن الكفالة ... الخ. وتوضيحه : إنَّ الأعيان إمَّا مضمونةٌ بنفسها : وهي ما يجب قيمتها عند الهلاك ، أو بغيرها : وهي ما لا يجب قيمتها، أو أمانة، والأوَّلُ تصح الكفاله به ولا تصح بالثاني والثالث ؛ لفقدان شرطها، وهو أن يكون مضموناً على الأصيل، لا يخرج عنه إلا بدفع عينه أو بدله.
(۱) رد المحتار» (٤ : ٣٧٧).
(۲) «البناية» (٦ : ٧٦٠).

وبالمرهون
لكن لو هلكَ لا يَحِبُ على الكفيل شيء ، فمراد «المتن» الكفالة بمالية المبيع ؛ وذلك لأنَّ ماليته غير مضمونة على الأصيل، فإنَّه لو هَلَكَ ينفسخ البيعُ ، ويَحِمُ ردُّ الثَّمَنِ. (وبالمرهون ) : أي بماليته لكن يصح بتسليم المرهون
وتوضيحه : إنَّ الأعيان إما مضمونة بنفسها : وهى ما يجب قيمتها عند الهلاك ، أو بغيرها : وهي ما لا يجب قيمتها ، أو أمانة، والأوَّلُ تصح الكفاله به ولا تصح بالثاني والثالث ؛ لفقدان شرطها، وهو أن يكون مضموناً على الأصيل، لا يخرج عنه إلا بدفع عينه أو بدله. وصورة الكفالة بالمبيع أن يقول للمشتري : إن ملك المبيع فعلي بدله أو قيمته ، فمعنى قول المصنف الله : بالمبيع، بماليته ؛ وذلك لأنَّ ماليتَهُ غير مضمونةٍ على الأصيل: فإنه لو هلك المبيع قبل القبض في يد البائع لا يجب على البائع شيء، وإنّما يسقط حقه في الثمن.
معنى وإذا كان المبيع مضموناً على البائع بسقوط حقِّهِ فِي الثَّمَنِ لا بنفسه لا يمكن تحقَّقُ الكفالة، ة، إذ هي ضمُّ الذمَّةِ إلى الذمَّةِ في المطالبة ، ولا يتحقق الضم بين المختلفين، فإنّما ثبت على الأصيل وهو سقوط حقه في الثمن لا يمكن إثباتُهُ في حق الكفيل. وما أمكن إثباته على الكفيل من كونه مضموناً عليه بالقيمة لا يمكن إثباته على الأصيل، بخلاف الكفالة بتسليم المبيع، حيث يصح لتحقق معنى الضم فيها، ونظير الكفالة ببدل الكتابة، حيث لا يمكن الإيجاب على الكفيل بمثل ما وجبَ الكفالة بالمبيع على الأصيل، كذا في «البحر» (١) ، و«الكفاية»(۲)، وغيرهما. [١] قوله : لا يجب على الكفيل شيء ؛ لأنَّ العقد قد انفسخ، ووجب على البائع رد الثمن، والكفيل لم يضمن الثمن. [٢] قوله : وبالمرهون ؛ أي ولا تصح الكفالة بماليَّة المرهون ؛ لأنَّ المرهون غيرُ مضمون على المرتهن بنفسه، وإنما يسقط دينه إذا هلك ، فلا يمكن إيجابُ الصَّمَانِ على الكفيل بما لا يجب على الأصيل.
Chapter of Kafalah: Its definition, conditions, and rulings. There is no kafalah for what is impossible to exact from the guarantor, such as hudud and qisas, and for the sold item, contrary to the price. He said: (There is no kafalah for what is impossible to exact from the guarantor, like hudud and qisas, and for the sold item, contrary to the price). Know that kafalah for delivery of the sold item is valid. As for if it is customary, then it is permissible as in suspending kafalah, as in Al-Idah: the guarantor for money or himself if he says: If I meet you tomorrow then I am free from the money, and he meets him tomorrow, he becomes free from the money. He allowed suspending the release from kafalah for money, and likewise if he suspends the release upon exacting part, it is permissible, or if he suspends the release of part upon accelerating part, it is permissible, mentioned it in Mabsut Shaykh al-Islam. So it is known that what is meant by condition is the non-customary condition, and the difference of the two narrations regarding the validity of suspension is based on this: the narration of impermissibility [applies] to when it is non-customary, and the narration of permissibility [applies] to when it is customary. End. And here is an exquisite investigation mentioned in Radd al-Muhtar. [1] His statement: There is no kafalah; because kafalah is only valid for something guaranteed in which substitution in its fulfillment is permissible, and substitution is not permissible in corporal punishments because the purpose of legislating them is to punish the corrupters for corruption, so it cannot be imposed on other than the offender, and because the condition of kafalah is that the object of guarantee be deliverable by the guarantor, and hudud and qisas are not such. [2] His statement: Like hudud and qisas; i.e., the same hudud and the same qisas, for kafalah for the person upon whom are hudud and qisas is permissible, as stated in Al-Binayah and others. [3] His statement: Contrary to the price; for kafalah for it from the buyer is valid because it is a debt like other debts. (4) His statement: Know; since the statement of the author 'by the sold item' carries a possibility that kafalah is valid in it and a possibility that it is not, it was necessary for the commentator to clarify what is intended here, so he said: and know that kafalah... etc. And its clarification: that corporeal properties are either guaranteed in themselves: i.e., what requires its value upon destruction, or by other than themselves: i.e., what does not require its value, or trust property. The first: kafalah for it is valid, and it is not valid for the second and third due to the lack of its condition, which is that it be guaranteed on the principal, not removed from him except by payment of its original or its substitute. 

And with the mortgaged property: but if it perishes, nothing is due from the guarantor. So the intention of the Matn is kafalah for the monetary value of the sold item; and that is because its monetary value is not guaranteed on the principal, for if the sold item perishes, the sale is annulled and the price must be returned. (And with the mortgaged): i.e., its monetary value, but it is valid for delivery of the mortgaged. And its clarification: that corporeal properties are either guaranteed in themselves: i.e., what requires its value upon destruction, or by other than themselves: i.e., what does not require its value, or trust property. The first: kafalah for it is valid, and it is not valid for the second and third due to the lack of its condition, which is that it be guaranteed on the principal, not removed from him except by payment of its original or its substitute. And the form of kafalah for the sold item is that he says to the buyer: 'If you own the sold item, then upon me is its substitute or its value.' So the meaning of the author's statement 'by the sold item' is by its monetary value; and that is because its monetary value is not guaranteed on the principal: for if the sold item perishes before possession in the hands of the seller, nothing is due from the seller, but only his right in the price falls. The meaning: And if the sold item is guaranteed on the seller by the fall of his right in the price, not in itself, kafalah cannot be realized, for it is joining a liability to a liability in demand, and joining cannot occur between two different things. For what is established on the principal—namely the fall of his right in the price—cannot be established against the guarantor. And what can be established against the guarantor—namely that he is responsible for the value—cannot be established against the principal. Contrary to kafalah for delivery of the sold item, where it is valid because the meaning of joining is realized. And analogous is kafalah for the substitute of Kitabah, where it is not possible to impose on the guarantor the same as what is due on the principal. So it is in Al-Bahr, Al-Kifayah, and others. [1] His statement: Nothing is due from the guarantor; because the contract has been annulled and the seller must return the price, and the guarantor did not guarantee the price. [2] His statement: And with the mortgaged; i.e., kafalah for the monetary value of the mortgaged is not valid; because the mortgaged is not guaranteed on the mortgagee in itself, but only his debt falls if it perishes, so it is not possible to impose liability on the guarantor for what is not due from the principal.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rulings of kafalah (suretyship/guarantee) from a Hanafi fiqh text. It begins by stating the general rule: kafalah is not valid for anything that cannot be exacted from the guarantor, such as hadd punishments (fixed corporal punishments) and qisas (retributive justice). This is because kafalah only applies to financial obligations where substitution by a guarantor is permissible. Corporal punishments are personal and cannot be delegated; the purpose of such punishments is to punish the actual offender. Additionally, the condition of kafalah is that the object of guarantee must be deliverable by the guarantor, which is not the case for hudud and qisas. However, kafalah for the person himself who owes hudud or qisas is valid—i.e., guaranteeing that he will appear. The text then contrasts the sold item (mabee') with the price (thaman): kafalah for the price is valid because it is a debt like any other debt, but kafalah for the sold item requires clarification. The sold item, before delivery, is not guaranteed on the seller in the sense of liability for its value; if it perishes, the sale is annulled and only the price is returned. Therefore, kafalah for its monetary value is not valid because the principal is not liable for that value. However, kafalah for delivery of the sold item is valid, as it involves joining a liability to another in demand, which is possible. The commentary also discusses the issue of suspending kafalah on a condition, noting that there are two narrations in the Hanafi school: one prohibits it when the condition is non-customary, and the other permits it when it is customary. Finally, the text states that kafalah for the mortgaged item (marhoon) is also not valid for its monetary value, because the mortgagee is not liable for the value of the mortgaged item; if it perishes, his debt merely falls. Thus, the condition that the object be guaranteed on the principal is not met.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A contract where a guarantor joins his liability to that of the principal for fulfillment of an obligation.
الحدود (al-hudud): Fixed corporal punishments prescribed by Shariah (e.g., amputation for theft, flogging for zina).
القصاص (al-qisas): Retributive punishment (eye for eye) in cases of murder or bodily harm.
النيابة (al-niyabah): Substitution; the concept that someone else can fulfill the obligation on behalf of the obligor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because they cannot be fulfilled by a substitute (niyaba).

D) Because the guarantor must be a relative.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah is invalid for hudud and qisas because these punishments cannot be delegated to a substitute; they are personal to the offender.

Kafalah — Surety
